ABSTRACT
Chemical exchange saturation transfer (CEST) is a relatively new contrast method within the
ﬁeld of magnetic resonance imaging (MRI). CEST can be used to provide enhanced contrast for
small amounts of molecular targets with protons engaged in chemical exchange with water in
a frequency-speciﬁc manner. The contrast is observed indirectly through a reduction in water
signal generated by selective saturation of the exchangeable protons and a subsequent exchange
transfer of the signal loss. CEST has shown great potential for in vivo molecular imaging, but
many challenges are yet to be conquered on the journey to clinical translation.
This study focused on CEST imaging from a clinical perspective with the purpose of bringing
CEST closer to clinical translation. All investigations hereby took their starting point from clinical
ﬁeld strength at 3 T and Myo-Inositol (MI) was chosen as the molecular target of this study.
Non-invasive clinical imaging of MI can have major impact on several pathological disorders that
alter in vivo MI concentration levels, such as Alzheimer’s disease and diabetes mellitus. Magnetic
resonance spectroscopic imaging (MRSI) has already shown a capability of achieving MI contrast
at 3 T, but with too low sensitivity and too low spatial resolution to have clinical impact.
The challenges and capabilities of MICEST (CEST imaging of MI) at 3 T were thoroughly
investigated for an aqueous in vitro environment by means of Bloch–McConnell (BM) simulations
and two diﬀerent approaches for CEST imaging. The investigated in vitro environment was
represented by two diﬀerent phantoms constructed for this study with purpose of testing the
dependence of MICEST on concentration levels and pH values, respectively. All MICEST imaging
used a phase cycled rectangular pulse for selective saturation. The ﬁrst tested approach for CEST
imaging applied a constant saturation power, while the other approach applied variable saturation
power and required parameter mapping of relaxation properties and ﬁeld inhomogeneity eﬀects
for post-processing.
MICEST imaging by means of the ﬁrst approach proved capable of achieving MI contrast
superior to MRSI with improved spatial resolution and improved sensitivity, However, the contrast
was most possibly contaminated by relaxation eﬀects, for which reason an initial focus for future
investigations of CEST should be obtaining relaxation-independent MICEST contrast reﬂecting
pure concentration contrast. The suspicion of possible contamination by relaxation eﬀects was
provided by BM simulations.
The main product of this study is the ﬁrst step towards clinical translation provided by a
broad and solid foundation for future studies of MICEST. This foundation consists of references
to many potential pitfalls of the technique and suggested approaches for further investigations.
i
==PAGE:8==
==PAGE:9==
RESUMÉ
Chemical exchange saturation transfer (CEST) er en relativt ny kontrastmetode inden for magnetic
resonance imaging (MRI) forskningsfeltet. CEST can udnyttes til at skabe forstærket kontrast for
små mængder af molekylære mål med protoner i engageret i kemisk udveksling med vand på
en frekvens-speciﬁk måde. Kontrasten observeres indirekte gennem en reduktion i vandsignalet
genereret af selektiv saturation af de udvekslende protoner og en efterfølgende udvekslings-
overførsel af signaltabet. CEST har vist stort potentiale for in vivo molekylær billeddannelse, men
mange udfordringer mangler stadig at blive overvundet på vejen til klinisk translation.
Dette projekt fokuserede på CEST billeddannelse fra et klinisk perspektiv med formålet at
bringe CEST nærmere klinisk translation. Alle undersøgelser tog herved udgangspunkt i en
klinisk feltstyrke på 3 T og Myo-Inositol (MI) blev valgt som det molekylære mål for dette projekt.
Non-invasiv klinisk billeddannelse af MI kan få stor betydning for ﬂere patologiske tilstande,
der ændrer in vivo koncentrationsniveauerne for MI, deriblandt Alzheimers og diabetes mellitus.
Magnetic resonance spectroscopic imaging (MRSI) har allerede udvist en evne til at kunne opnå
MI kontrast ved 3 T, men med for lav sensitivitet og for lav spatiel opløsning til at have klinisk
betydning.
Udfordringerne og egnetheden af MICEST (CEST billeddannelse af MI) ved 3 T blev grundigt
undersøgt for et vandigt in vitro miljø, ved hjælp af Bloch–McConnell (BM) simuleringer og to
forskellige tilgangsvinkler til CEST billeddannelse. Det undersøgte in vitro miljø blev repræsen-
teret af to forskellige fantomer sammensat til dette projekt med formålet at teste MICESTs
afhængighed af hhv. koncentrationsniveauer og pH-værdier. Al MICEST billeddannelse anvendte
en ‘phase cycled’ rektangulær puls til den selektive saturation. Den først testede tilgangsvinkel
til CEST billeddannelse anvendte en konstant saturationsstyrke, mens den anden tilgangsvinkel
anvendte varierende saturationsstyrke og krævede kortlægning af parametre for relaksation-
segenskaber og felt inhomogenitetseﬀekter til post-processering.
MICEST billeddannelse via den første tilgangsvinkel beviste evne til at opnå MI kontrast
bedre sammenlignet med MRSI, og med forbedret spatiel opløsning of forbedret sensitivitet.
Dog var kontrasten højst sandsynligt kontamineret af relaksationseﬀekter, hvorfor det første
fokus for fremtidige undersøgelser af CEST bør være at opnå en relaksations-uafhængig MICEST
kontrast, der afspejler ren koncentrationskontrast. Mistanken om mulig kontaminering fra
relaksationseﬀekter blev leveret af BM simuleringer.
Hovedproduktet fra dette projekt er et første skridt mod klinisk translation i form af et bredt
og solidt fundament for fremtidige projekter af MICEST. Fundamentet består af referencer til
mange potentielle faldgruber for metoden og foreslåede fremgangsmåder til videre undersøgelser.
iii

PREFACE
This Master’s thesis has been written as a partial fulﬁllment of a Master of Science degree in
Biomedical Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU).
The project has been carried out in cooperation with the Department of Electrical Engineering
at DTU, Kgs. Lyngby, Denmark, and General Electric (GE) Global Research, Munich, Germany.
The work on this thesis was carried out in the period from August 24, 2015 to February 14, 2016
and was assigned a total workload of 30 ECTS credits. The majority of the project period from
September 8 to December 21, 2015 was spent at the GE Global Research facilities in Munich. GE
Global Research provided both the needed research facilities for the project as well as an excellent
team of researchers for professional discussions.
acknowledgment
As an appreciation of diﬀerent contributions to this thesis, I would like to express my thanks to a
group of people.
First of all, I would like to thank my supervisors. Thanks go to my main supervisor, Professor
Jan Henrik Ardenkjær-Larsen, for giving me the opportunity to write this thesis, and for valuable
feedback during the parts of the project period spent in Denmark. I would also like to thank
Senior Scientist, Rolf Schulte, for his guidance through the main part of the project period spent
in Germany, and for his open oﬃce door always allowing me to step by and get answers to my
questions.
A special thanks go to all students and scientists connected to the MRI department of the
GE Global Research facilities in Munich. Thanks for providing an warm and inspiring research
environment.
Finally, I would also like to thank family and friends for moral support and proofreading.
Rie Beck Hansen

xiii

INTRODUCTION
1
Magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) has been part of the most active research areas within medical
technology since its invention in the early 1970s. In combination with the already established
nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) spectroscopy techniques to study chemical environments on
a nuclear level, in vivo magnetic resonance spectroscopy (MRS) developed into one of the most
versatile forms of spectroscopy. From relatively simple imaging methods, magnetic resonance
spectroscopic imaging (MRSI) has evolved to include complex imaging acquisition techniques,
spectral editing, ultra fast imaging schemes and various other forms of manipulation of the
magnetic nuclei, including the method of: chemical exchange saturation transfer (CEST).
Since CEST was ﬁrst introduced in 2000 by Ward and Baliban as a new class of contrast agents
for MRI [1], the research ﬁeld has only grown in popularity. By providing enhanced contrast for
molecular targets with protons engaged in chemical exchange with water in a frequency-speciﬁc
manner, the technique has made great promises for in vivo molecular imaging. The contrast is
observed indirectly through a reduction in water signal generated by selective saturation of the
exchangeable protons and a subsequent exchange transfer of the signal loss. The sensitivity of the
method is much higher than what is achievable through direct detection, because the signal loss
is ampliﬁed through many exchange events during an extended period of selective saturation.
Despite the great potential of the technique many challenges are yet to be conquered on the
journey to clinical translation. These challenges include, among others, inherent properties of
clinically relevant molecular targets that make their detection diﬃcult at lower ﬁeld strengths,
contaminating eﬀects in vivo that may reduce the speciﬁcity of the CEST eﬀect, and optimal
pulse sequence design.
1.1
problem statement
The overall aim of this study is to explore, implement and test the usability of CEST from a
clinical perspective. This aim is sought accomplished through 1) implementation of the method
at clinical ﬁeld strength with all experiments performed on a 3 T whole-body MRI scanner, and 2)
by focusing on obtaining clinically relevant, non-invasive, and metabolite speciﬁc CEST contrast.
The main problems that arise from this aim relate to the challenges listed above: inherent
exchange properties of the clinically relevant molecular targets making their detection diﬃcult at
3 T, and contaminating eﬀects challenging the isolation of the CEST eﬀects.
The main molecular target of this thesis is Myo-Inositol (MI), a cyclic sugar alcohol with in
vivo concentration levels known to be altered in connection with several pathological disorders.
This makes MI a potential biomarker1 and hereby clinically relevant. But in order to be conﬁrmed
as such, MI needs to be detectable with a technique achieving reproducible results, while being
1A biomarker is a naturally occurring molecule, gene, or characteristic by which a particular pathological or
physiological process, disease, etc. can be identiﬁed [2].
1
==PAGE:26==
2
Chapter 1. Introduction
sensitive enough to conﬁrm or reject a correlation of MI to the cause or progression of the disease
in question. MI has six hydroxyl groups with protons engaged in chemical exchange with water
making it a possible CEST contrast agent. Hereby CEST could be the method conﬁrming MI as a
biomarker. However, the chemical exchange of the hydroxyl protons of MI with water happen
at a relatively fast rate, which is one of the inherent properties that makes CEST imaging of MI
challenging at 3 T.
1.2
objectives
The overall objectives of this thesis are:
1. To understand the CEST eﬀect in terms of how it is generated, the underlying mechanisms,
and confounding factors.
2. To critically assess some of the current published research papers within the ﬁeld relevant
to the aim of this study.
3. To numerically simulate the MICEST experiment to understand the impact of diﬀerent
parameters on the MICEST eﬀects, and to optimize sequence parameters for maximum
MICEST eﬀects.
4. To carry out scan sessions using the provided pulse sequence Phase Cycle CEST to evaluate
achievable MICEST eﬀects in vitro and to investigate dependence on chemical environment
in terms of concentration and pH.
1.3
thesis overview
The structure of this thesis is based on six main chapters: Background, Theory, Methods, Results,
Discussion, and Conclusion.
The Background chapter begins with an introduction to diﬀerent NMR fundamentals as
necessary prerequisites for the thesis subject. Next the CEST eﬀect itself is described explaining the
basic principles and underlying mechanisms. With the CEST eﬀect established, the opportunities
as well the challenges of the technique are presented. This is done in the light of recent research
publications. Finally the Background chapter provides a greater insight into some of the molecular
targets of CEST in general and into the target of this thesis, MI.
The Theory chapter has the purpose of laying out a foundation for some of the methods
presented in the following Methods chapter. Since the main methods applied to fulﬁll the
study objectives involve simulations and scan sessions, the theoretical foundation consists of an
introduction to the Bloch equations and the Bloch-McConnell equations for the simulations, and
an analytical solution to assist CEST eﬀect isolation together with a description of the used pulse
sequence Phase Cycle CEST for the scan sessions.
In the Methods chapter all used methods are outlined. First of all, the chapter introduces the
two diﬀerent approaches to the CEST experiment applied for both the simulations and the scan
==PAGE:27==
1.3. Thesis Overview
3
sessions – one ‘standard’ approach and one approach using variable saturation power named
vCEST. Subsequently, the Matlab implementation of the numerical Bloch-McConnell simulations
of MICEST is presented, followed by a description of all simulations included in the thesis together
with their respective simulation parameters. All simulations and scan sessions are separately
numbered in chronological order. Next, the hardware used and the phantoms constructed are
stated. Two phantoms were build, one with the purpose of investigating the concentration
dependence of the MICEST eﬀect and one with the purpose of investigating pH dependence.
Then all pulse sequences applied are described both in terms of their purpose and the sequence
parameters used for each scan session. Furthermore and last, how the scan session data were
post-processed and statistically evaluated are described.
The Results chapter presents the results from the simulations and the scan sessions in chrono-
logical order. In total seven simulations and 3 scan sessions were done.
In the Discussion chapter various aspects of the produced results are discussed, the applied
methods are evaluated, and future approaches are suggested in a clinical perspective.
The ﬁnal Conclusion chapter sums up the most important conclusions drawn throughout the
thesis, from the Background chapter to the main points of Discussion chapter.

==PAGE:29==
BACKGROUND
2
At this point many excellent review papers [3–7] have been published on the subject of CEST, re-
viewing everything from the basic mechanisms and experimental pitfalls to promising approaches
and clinical translation. This chapter will ﬁrst of all introduce fundamental principles of NMR
spectroscopy and MRI as prerequisites for understanding the CEST technique and other methods
applied in this thesis. Next, the basic principles of CEST itself, which make up the foundation of
this project, will be the main focus. Following, in the last section, some of the major challenges
and opportunities of the technique will be outlined with a special focus on translation to the
clinic, while giving a critical evaluation of recent publications within the ﬁeld. The last section
will also include a description of the molecular targets of this thesis.
2.1
nmr fundamentals
NMR is the study of magnetic properties (and energies) of nuclei that possess nuclear spin angular
momentum. A property that make them act like magnetic dipoles. This is the case for atoms
with an odd number of protons and/or odd number of neutrons. These nuclei are, due to this
property, often referred to as spins in the NMR context. Hydrogen (1H) possess nuclear spin
angular momentum and is by far the most studied NMR species due to its natural abundance in
the human body (99.985 % [8], mostly in water) and its high sensitivity (relative sensitivity of 1.00
[8]). Unless noted, all nuclear spins referred to in this thesis will be those of 1H. The source of the
magnetic resonance (MR) signal in 1H (proton) imaging arise through interactions of the proton
spins with two types of magnetic ﬁelds: a main static magnetic ﬁeld B0, and a radio frequency
(RF) ﬁeld B1.
When nuclear spins are subjected to a static magnetic ﬁeld B0, two things happen: 1) the spins
tend to align with the direction of the ﬁeld (deﬁned as the longitudinal or z-direction) giving rise
to a net magnetization moment M (a vector with a x-, y-, and z-component) (see Figure 2.1a),
and 2) the spins exhibit precessional behavior with a well-deﬁned resonance frequency called the
Larmor frequency. By adopting a lef-handed convention for rotation (often done when describing
MR), the relation between the Larmor frequency and the magnetic ﬁeld is given by the following
expression, known as the Larmor equation:
ω0 = γB0
(2.1)
Here γ is the gyromagnetic ratio, a constant speciﬁc for each atom. For 1H, γ/(2π) = 42.58 MHz/T
[9]. In terms of left-handed rotation, the positive gyromagnetic ratio of protons means that
they precess clockwise with respect to Eq. (2.1). In the presence of only a static ﬁeld, the net
magnetization moment M will eventually reach an equilibrium state referred to as M0= [0,0,M0].
By applying an RF ﬁeld, B1, rotating at the Larmor frequency in the transverse plane of the
static ﬁeld (the xy-plane) the magnetization will be excited (see Figure 2.1b). The B1 ﬁeld induces
5
==PAGE:30==
6
Chapter 2. Background
Figure 2.1: Nuclear spin interactions with the static magnetic ﬁeld B0 and with the RF ﬁeld
B1. a) Alignment of magnetization in the presence of a static magnetic ﬁeld B0. b) Excitation of
net magnetization M by applying a B1 RF ﬁeld rotating in the transverse plane and tuned to the
Larmor frequency. c) 90◦excitation in the laboratory frame of reference. d) 90◦excitation in the
rotating frame of reference. From [9].
a torque on the magnetization, which causes the net magnetization M to rotate away from its
equilibrium position in the z-direction, while precessing about the z-axis. The rotation frequency
away from the equilibrium position is given by:
ω1 = γB1
(2.2)
analogous to Eq. (2.1), and the ﬁnal rotation angle is dependent on the B1 ﬁeld strength and
duration. The rotation angle is often referred to as the ﬂip angle and is given as:
α = γB1tp
(2.3)
for an excitation pulse applied with a constant amplitude (B1) for a certain time (the pulse width,
tp), a so-called rectangular RF pulse.
When the strength and duration of the B1 ﬁeld is set for a ﬂip angle of 90◦, the magnetization
is rotated to lie completely in the xy-plane with no z-component. For a single excitation this
results in the largest possible MR signal. Figure 2.1c depicts the behavior of the magnetization
for a 90◦excitation in the stationary laboratory frame of reference, while Figure 2.1d shows the
behavior in the rotating frame of reference. The rotating frame of reference simpliﬁes the complex
==PAGE:31==
2.1. NMR Fundamentals
7
motions of the magnetization vector M greatly, as this frame rotates at the Larmor frequency of
the applied RF magnetic ﬁeld B1 about the z-axis. Hereby the rotating B1 ﬁeld appears stationary.
After excitation, the magnetization M will eventually return to its equilibrium position in
the z-direction, M0, which means that the established xy-component must decay away, while
the z-component of the magnetization must be restored. The time constants related to the
return to equilibrium are called relaxation time constants. T1 is the relaxation time constant
that characterizes the longitudinal relaxation, i.e. the return to equilibrium in the z-direction.
T1 relaxation is also called spin-lattice relaxation as it involves energy exchange between the
nuclear spins and the surrounding lattice. At a microscopic level, the lattice provide random
ﬂuctuating magnetic ﬁelds as surrounding magnetic dipoles undergo Brownian motion1. When
the xy-components of the ﬂuctuating ﬁelds approach the spins’ resonant frequency, they act
like an RF ﬁeld and hereby make the longitudinal relaxation is more eﬃcient (shorter T1). T1
increases with B0.
T2 is the transverse relaxation constant and is also called the spin-spin time constant. The same
random ﬁeld ﬂuctuations that account for T1 relaxation are also accountable for T2 relaxation.
However, where the longitudinal relaxation depends on the xy-components of the ﬂuctuating
ﬁelds, the transverse relaxation is aﬀected by both the z-components and the xy-components;
therefore T2 ≤T1. The z-components of the ﬂuctuating ﬁelds cause dephasing (loss of phase
coherence) of the transverse magnetization. The T2 relaxation process is dominated by the z-
components, especially by those resonating with frequencies close to zero. Therefore T2 is, by
and large, independent of the static ﬁeld strength. The inverse of the relaxation times are called
the relaxation rates, i.e. R1 = 1/T1 and R2 = 1/T2.
Additionally to the dephasing caused by random ﬁeld ﬂuctuations, any spatial inhomogeneities
in the magnetic ﬁelds over time will cause further transverse relaxation. The relaxation time
constant that describes the decay of transverse magnetization due to both the accumulated phase
diﬀerences and any local ﬁeld inhomogeneities is called T2* (“T2 star”). As T2 relaxation only
account for the transverse magnetization decay caused by spin-spin interactions, T2* is always
shorter than T2. Lost phase coherence caused by local ﬁeld inhomogeneities can be regained by
means of so-called refocusing pulses, hereby leaving ‘pure’ spin-spin T2 relaxation [10].
Given a non-zero precessing transverse component of the magnetization M, an MR signal can
be measured by means of Faraday’s law of induction. The MR signal is received as an induced
electromagnetic force in an RF coil oriented to detect changes in magnetization in the xy-plane.
The RF receive coil can either be the same coil used to generate the rotating B1 ﬁeld (the transmit
coil, resulting in a transmit/receive coil), or a separate receive-only coil. The measured complex
time signal is called a free induction decay (FID) and is illustrated in Figure 2.2 as a function of
time in the xy-plane, while being subjected to T2 relaxation.
1Brownian motion is the erratic random movement of microscopic particles, as a result of continuous bombardment
from molecules of the surrounding medium [2].
==PAGE:32==
8
Chapter 2. Background
Figure 2.2:
The complex three-dimensional FID shown in the transverse plane over time
following an excitation pulse. The FID can be completely described by its two projections in the
xt- and yt-plane, corresponding to the real and imaginary components, respectively. From [8].
2.1.1
Image Formation
Spatial mapping to get an MR image of the ensemble of tiny oscillators inducing RF signals, is
usually based on controlling their relative phases and frequencies with linear gradient ﬁelds.
MR images can be created both from whole volumes spatially mapped at once as a result of
three-dimensional imaging acquisition schemes, but are more often created from acquisition
schemes that spatially map one slice at a time. Creating multiple two-dimensional images can be
advantageous, since three-dimensional imaging can be time consuming and result in sub-optimal
contrast or spatial resolution.
The essential concept to spatially map a two-dimensional slice of spins is to make the resonance
frequency ω0 of the spins position-dependent, such that a Fourier transformation will reveal
frequencies corresponding to spatial positions. A linear gradient ﬁeld is a magnetic ﬁeld where
the ﬁeld strength varies linearly with position. Mathematically, applying a gradient ﬁeld in the
z-direction (Gz) generates a total magnetic ﬁeld at an arbitrary z-position equal to:
B(z) = B0 + z ·Gz
(2.4)
More generally, this equation can be rephrased to apply to an arbitrary position vector r:
B(r) = B0 +r·G
(2.5)
As a consequence the Larmor equation, Eq. (2.1), should then be rewritten as:
ω(r) = γB(r) = γB0 +γ·r·G
(2.6)
Hereby the spins will be frequency-encoded in the direction the gradient is applied. By applying
a linear gradient ﬁeld in one direction, however, only that dimension can be spatially mapped. In
order to end up with a ﬁnal two-dimensional MR image after Fourier transformation, the other
==PAGE:33==
2.1. NMR Fundamentals
9
dimension needs to be mapped as well. This is usually done by also applying a linear gradient in
that direction, e.g. y-direction, after the other gradient and just before recording the FID (the
sequence readout). This gradient will cause the phase of the spins to be a linear function of the
y-position, as a results of Eq. (2.6). Hereby the spins will also be spatially phase-encoded (and
indirectly frequency-encoded) [8, 9].
To only excite the spins within one slice of a volume, leaving the other spins unperturbed, is
called slice selective excitation. And in the same fashion, exciting all spins simultaneously within
a volume is called non-selective excitation. Selective excitation of a single slice is performed
in the presence of a linear gradient ﬁeld before spatially mapping the spins within that slice.
Because the B1 ﬁeld has to be tuned to the Larmor frequency in order to perform excitation, a
B1 excitation pulse can in the presence of a linear gradient ﬁeld, be designed to have a temporal
frequency bandwidth that matches the resonance frequency bandwidth of the spins in the slice of
interest. To achieve a slice with a rectangular proﬁle, the Fourier transform of the B1 frequency
content needs to be a rectangular function, i.e. ideally B1 must be a sinc function. However, as
sinc functions are inﬁnite and B1 must have a ﬁnite duration, perfect rectangular proﬁles are
impossible to obtain and can only be approximated [9].
The two-dimensional map of spatial frequencies that can be collected and used to reconstruct
an MR image after frequency- and phase-encoding a selected slice is called k-space. How the
spatial frequencies within k-space are sampled, the k-space trajectory, and to which extent,
determines the spatial resolution and the ﬁeld of view (FOV) of the resultant MR image. The
lower spatial frequencies contain image contrast information, while the higher spatial frequencies
contain image detail information.
Most anatomical MR imaging sequences apply k-space sampling schemes where only one
k-space line is read out at a time following excitation. Hereby the excitation scheme is repeated
N times, with a repetition time TR, to provide the needed number of lines and sampling density
of the k-space. For fast MRI sequences, on the other hand, either all data points of the k-space are
sampled following a single excitation, or greater parts of the k-space are sampled following each
excitation still reducing the total number of excitations. Fast imaging sequences are especially
favorable for functional or molecular MR imaging, because these imaging techniques often require
image acquisition at approximately the same rate as the physiological changes of interest. An
example of a fast imaging sequence is the popular echo planar imaging (EPI) sequence. An EPI
sequence can be either a gradient-echo (GRE) EPI with T2* relaxation or a spin-echo (SE) EPI with
T2 relaxation. Furthermore, when a full k-space readout follows only one excitation using an EPI
sequence, it is speciﬁed as a single-shot EPI (SSEPI) sequence. Figure 2.3 shows an example of a
pulse sequence chart for a GRE-SSEPI sequence. Figure 2.3a shows the transmitted and received
RF signals, the gradients applied in the frequency-encoding direction, and the gradients applied
in the phase-encoding direction. Figure 2.3b shows the k-space trajectory [8].
==PAGE:34==
10
Chapter 2. Background
Figure 2.3: Pulse sequence chart (A) and corresponding k-space trajectory (B) of a GRE-SSEPI
sequence. Black k-space lines correspond to frequency-encoding gradients applied during signal
acquisition. Red lines correspond for phase-encoding gradients called blips. From [8].
While SSEPI has the great beneﬁt of reducing the scan time to achieve excellent temporal
resolution, it is at the cost of several possible artifacts that one has to be aware of both when
choosing the scan parameters for the sequence and when interpreting the ﬁnal reconstructed
images. The most common artifacts result from imperfections in the magnetic ﬁelds used to collect
the images (static and gradient ﬁelds), since the fast acquisition places more strict requirements
on the MR hardware. Static ﬁeld inhomogeneities can result in both signal losses and geometric
distortions.
Geometric distortions in SSEPI images are caused by the long readout periods following
excitation, as these come with reduced sampling frequency of each echo and reduced gradient
strengths. In anatomical images, which have much shorter readout intervals, small ﬁeld variations
across the image plane may only cause sub-pixel distortions. But in SSEPI images the long time
spent acquiring data from k-space can cause noticeable distortions spanning several pixels. Field
inhomogeneities along a single direction in the image plane (e.g. x or y for an axial slice) causes
stretching or shearing, which might be possible to adjust for during post-processing. However,
geometric distortions of SSEPI images rarely occur in only a single direction, but rather in more
complex patterns due to more irregular gradient variations across the image. Small ﬁeld variations
along the through-plane direction (e.g. z-direction for the axial slice) can cause oﬀ-resonance
excitation relative to the selected slice and hereby substantial signal loss. Such signal-dropouts
cannot be restored [10].
2.1.2
Chemical Shift
The resonant frequency ω of the nuclear spin does not only depend on the gyromagnetic ratio γ
and the magnetic ﬁeld B0 as implied in the Larmor equation in Eq. (2.1), when no gradient ﬁelds are
applied. It is also highly sensitive to the chemical environment of the spin. A small displacement
of the resonant frequency due to the chemical environment is referred to as a chemical shift. The
chemical shift is caused by shielding of the nuclei by the surrounding electrons as a response to
the magnetic ﬁeld B0. The electrons hereby create a magnetic moment that opposes the B0 ﬁeld,
==PAGE:35==
2.1. NMR Fundamentals
11
why the magnetic ﬁeld sensed by the nucleus will be reduced. The eﬀective ﬁeld experienced by
the nucleus can be expressed as:
B = B0 (1−σ)
(2.7)
where σ is a shielding constant dependent on the chemical environment. From the Larmor
equation (Eq. (2.1)) the eﬀective Larmor frequency can then be expressed as:
ω = ω0 (1−σ)
(2.8)
Chemical shifts are rarely expressed in units of Hz as this would make their values dependent
on magnetic ﬁeld strength. To make the chemical shifts independent of B0 they are instead
expressed in terms of parts per million (ppm) with respect to a reference frequency ωref:
δ
=
ω−ωref
ωref
×106
=
σref −σ
1−σref
×106
≈
(σref −σ)×106
because σref ≪1
(2.9)
The most widely used reference for the 0 ppm sample in a 1H NMR spectrum is TMS (tetramethyl-
silane, Si(CH3)4), as it exhibits maximum shielding, such that most other NMR resonances have
smaller σ and thereby positive δ. With respect to TMS water has a chemical shift of approximately
4.7 ppm.
By placing a sample of material in a uniform B0 ﬁeld, exciting it by means a B1 ﬁeld, measuring
its FID, and then Fourier transforming the FID, the resultant NMR spectrum will reveal multiple
peaks at diﬀerent frequencies corresponding to diﬀerent chemical shifts in the sample. An example
of this is given in Figure 2.4, showing a measured FID of a 31P sample and its corresponding
Fourier transform with multiple peaks. As can be seen in Figure 2.4b, it is customary to plot the
NMR chemical shift scale from left to right (due to historical reasons). It is also usual practice to
refer to the high-ppm region as downﬁeld and the low-ppm region as upﬁeld.
Figure 2.4: A) The measured FID of a sample containing 31P following an excitation pulse. B)
Fourier transforming the FID reveals an NMR spectrum composed of resonances from phospho-
creatine and adenosine triphosphate (ATP). From [8].
Selective excitation, as described earlier, involves a B1 excitation pulse designed to only excite
spins resonating with certain frequencies. As this can be utilized to perform slice selection for
imaging in the presence of a linear gradient ﬁeld, selective excitation can also be used for only
==PAGE:36==
12
Chapter 2. Background
exciting selected nuclear species with known chemical shifts. I.e. within NMR spectroscopy
selective or non-selective pulses refer to whether the pulses are designed to only excite selected
spins with speciﬁc chemical shifts or excite the entire NMR spectrum. Non-selective pulses are
also called hard pulses, while selective pulse also are known as soft pulses.
Magnetic resonance spectroscopic imaging (MRSI) is the name of the technique, when several
FIDs are measured and spatially mapped across a selectively excited volume simultaneously. The
k-space of an MRSI image does therefore not only have dimensions of spatial frequencies, but
also a time dimension of the sampled FIDs. After the FIDs have been Fourier transformed to
multiple NMR spectra, spectral information can be extracted to reveal metabolic contrast in the
resultant MRSI image. However, spatial resolution of MRSI is very low with current techniques.
The ability of NMR spectroscopy to distinguish multiple diﬀerent nuclear resonances makes it
a powerful tool for investigating properties of chemical environments in terms of both molecular
structures and more dynamic features such as metabolic processes and chemical exchange [8, 9].
2.2
basic principles of cest
2.2.1
What is CEST?
The underlying principle of the CEST experiment can be explained rather simply through the
schematic overview in Figure 2.5, which takes a two-pool proton exchange system as its starting
point. CEST relies on the presence of such two pools, a larger solvent pool (pool a) and a smaller
solute pool (pool b), where the solute protons resonate at a frequency diﬀerent from the solvent
protons and engage in chemical exchange with the solvent protons. The exchange system is given
as:
a
kab
−−−*
)−−−
kba
b
(2.10)
where kab is the rate constant for the forward reaction and kba is the rate constant for the
reverse reaction. The solvent pool is also referred to as the water pool and the solute pool is
sometimes referred to as the CEST pool. Chemical exchange in this example means that the
protons physically move back and forth between the two diﬀerent molecular environments of
pool a and pool b, respectively (Figure 2.5a).
The chemical shift diﬀerence (i.e. the diﬀerence in resonance frequency between the two
pools in units of ppm) is deﬁned as ∆ω and is given as 3.5 ppm in Figure 2.5. This corresponds
to the chemical shift diﬀerence of protons of amide groups (–NH) relative to water protons.
The equilibrium exchange rate of the two pools is deﬁned as kex = kba + kab, i.e. as the sum
of the exchange rates between the two pools. In a the CEST experiment, however, the proton
concentration in pool a, the water pool (∼110 M), is always signiﬁcantly larger than that in
the solute pool (usually at a millimolar level), which means that the exchange from the water
pool to the solute pool becomes close to negligible and that kex ∼= kba. Exactly how the relative
magnitudes of kba and kab are related to the proton fraction (or pool ratio) of the two pools is
given in the following expression:
fb = kab
kba
= M0b
M0a
= nb ·[b]
na ·[a]
(2.11)
==PAGE:37==
2.2. Basic Principles of CEST
13
where M0a and M0b are the equilibrium magnetizations of the pool a and pool b protons, respec-
tively, n is the number of protons per molecule for the respective pool, and the square brackets
indicate concentration. The proton fraction fa of pool a is the reciprocal of fb. And as [a] ≫[b]
for the CEST experiment, fb is usually less than 1 % [3].
By selectively saturating the solute proton spin population, using RF irradiation at the solute
proton frequency2, one can induce a partial saturation of the water pool as the saturation will be
transferred via chemical exchange. However, considering the small pool ratio, a single saturation
transfer will not be detectable as a signiﬁcant loss of magnetization of the water pool. In order
to achieve a detectable decrease in water signal, one needs to apply the saturation pulse for a
certain saturation time (tsat), allowing multiple exchange processes and saturation transfers to
occur (Figure 2.5b). The saturation transfer process will continue until the system reaches steady
state or the saturation pulse is turned oﬀ. The ﬁnal reduction in the water signal can then be
imaged with any routine fast imaging sequence, such as e.g. an EPI sequence.
By sweeping through multiple saturation frequencies with the selective saturation RF pulse, a
Z-spectrum can be generated. The Z-spectrum is the signal intensity of the water after saturation
(Ssat), normalized by the water signal without saturation (S0), and given as a function of the
saturation frequency relative to the water frequency (∆ω) (Figure 2.5c). Signal losses at saturation
frequency oﬀsets other than that of water are identiﬁed as CEST eﬀects.
Part of the Z-spectrum is characterized by the symmetric line shape of the direct water
saturation (DWS), which is centered at the water frequency and caused by imperfect frequency
selectivity of the saturation pulse. The DWS is also referred to as a water spillover eﬀect and
its line shape is mainly determined by the B1 amplitude of the saturating ﬁeld, the T1, and T2
relaxation times. Under certain circumstances the DWS may interfere with the detection of CEST
eﬀects at the solute frequency, e.g. if the line shapes of the eﬀects are overlapping. To resolve
this issue a common metric used to isolate the CEST eﬀects is the magnetization transfer ratio
asymmetry (MTRasym), that exploits the assumed symmetry of the DWS. The MTRasym is given
by:
MTRasym(∆ω) = Ssat(−∆ω)−Ssat(∆ω)
S0
(2.12)
and hereby reveals an extracted positive CEST eﬀect centered at the solute frequency, in this case
at ∆ω = 3.5 ppm for amide protons (Figure 2.5d).
Under the assumptions of a two-pool exchange system, steady state of the exchange process,
and no spillover (i.e. perfect selectivity of the saturation pulse), it is possible to provide a simpliﬁed
analytical solution to the pure CEST eﬀect, the so-called proton transfer ratio (PTR):
PTR = fb ·α·kba ·T1a
³
1−e−tsat/T1a
´
(2.13)
This equation suggests that the CEST eﬀect is enhanced by an increase in proton fraction of the
solute protons fb, by greater saturation eﬃciency α, by faster exchange rate kba, and by longer
relaxation times for the water pool T1a (if tsat ≫T1a) [3].
2RF saturation refers to equilibrating the nuclear spin populations of the proton energy states (spin up and spin
down) such that there is no net magnetization.

Neglecting transverse relaxation, the saturation eﬃciency can be approximated by:
α ≈
(γB1)2
(γB1)2 +(kba)2
(2.14)
This suggests that in order to saturate faster exchanging protons eﬃciently, the only solution
is to apply more RF power [3]. However, in the analytical solutions given in the review paper
by Zaiss et al. [6], it is emphasized that although a greater B1 amplitude of the saturating ﬁeld
increases saturation eﬃciency, it also decreases spectral selectivity as the peak widths of both
the CEST eﬀect and the DWS are increased. This demonstrates that the B1 amplitude is a crucial
parameter for the CEST experiment aﬀecting both the PTR and the selectivity.

2.2.2
Chemical Exchange Types
The sources of CEST eﬀects in a Z-spectrum are referred to as CEST agents. A CEST agent
provides the molecular environment of the exchanging protons other than that of water. The
chemical shift diﬀerence, the exchange rate and the proton concentration of the CEST agent are
all important properties that determine how powerful a signal enhancement the agent can create
in a molecular imaging experiment. The focus of this thesis is on endogenous CEST agents, which
enable noninvasive CEST imaging, however several exogenous CEST agents3 also exist. Many
exogenous CEST agents are speciﬁcally designed to optimize the above-mentioned properties to
achieve a maximum CEST eﬀect. Most exogenous agents employ paramagnetic species4, as these
have large chemical shift diﬀerences that allow much higher exchange rates without leaving
the slow exchange regime (the exchange regimes are deﬁned in the next section, Section 2.2.3).
Paramagnetic agents are denoted paraCEST (exogenous), while diamagnetic agents are denoted
diaCEST (both exogenous and endogenous). The main diﬀerence between diaCEST and paraCEST
agents is the chemical shift. For most diaCEST agents the chemical shift is between 0−4 ppm
relative to water (for hydroxyl (–OH), amine (–NH2), and amide (–NH) exchange groups) [7],
while paraCEST agents exist with chemical shift diﬀerences all the way at e.g. ∼50 ppm for
Europium [3].
Besides being classiﬁed as either para- or diaCEST, CEST agents are also organized based
on the type of chemical exchange they engage in: atom (proton) exchange (both endogenous
and exogenous diaCEST agents and some exogenous paraCEST agents), molecular exchange
(paraCEST and hyperCEST agents), and compartmental exchange see Figure 2.6a, b, and c,
respectively.
Furthermore, diﬀerent CEST experiments are also named after the speciﬁc molecular target
they are trying to image. So when reading CEST papers one might encounter names such as
gluCEST (glutamate), glucoCEST (glucose), and aptCEST (amide protons on endogenous cellular
proteins and peptides), just to mention a few.
3Exogenous contrast agents include artiﬁcial substances, usually administered intravenously.
4Having a paramagnetic metal ion, often one of the lanthanides.
==PAGE:40==
16
Chapter 2. Background
Figure 2.6: CEST agents classiﬁed by exchange type. a) Proton exchange: magnetic labeling of
physically exchanging protons, which is the case for most diaCEST agents and several paraCEST
agents. As an example, hydroxyl, amide and amine protons in a peptide are shown. b) Molecular
exchange: magnetic labeling of exchanging molecules, in this case a water molecule coordinated
to Europium in a paraCEST agent. c) Compartmental exchange: Magnetic labeling of compartmen-
talized water molecules in a fast-exchange environment resulting in a single average resonance
frequency for compartmental water that is diﬀerent from bulk water. From [3].
2.2.3
Chemical Exchange Regimes
The rate at which the protons of a two-pool system are chemically exchanging, is divided into
three regimes: slow, intermediate, and fast. When kex ≪∆ω the exchange rate is determined as
slow, and opposite, when kex ≫∆ω the exchange is fast. When kex ≈∆ω, the exchange takes
place in the intermediate regime. However, the exact borders between these regimes are not
clear-cut. Be aware of the fact that chemical shift diﬀerences will be expressed in units of Hz
(spin revolutions per second) when compared to exchange rates in units of exchange per second
(s−1), which make them dependent on the static ﬁeld strength. Therefore the same exchange
system can be in the fast regime on a 3 T MRI scanner, while being in the intermediate regime on
a 7 T scanner. The chemical exchange rate is not constant either, as it depends on the chemical
environment, especially pH value and temperature.
Figure 2.7 illustrates a slow exchange situation unrelated to the CEST experiment, i.e. without
application of selective saturation pulses and with more similar pool sizes. Consider one of the
pools, let say pool a, in a two-pool system and assume that the protons of this pool is on-resonance.
Immediately after a 90◦pulse the magnetization will be along the Y-axis as shown in Figure
2.7a. During free precession three processes take place: 1) the magnetization decays as a result
==PAGE:41==
2.2. Basic Principles of CEST
17
of transverse relaxation, 2) the magnetization decays due to the magnetization transferred to
the other pool, pool b, with a constant rate kab, and 3) magnetization is also transferred to pool
a from b with a rate kba. When the chemical shift of the pool b protons (∆ωb) (and hereby
their precession frequency) diﬀers signiﬁcantly from that of the pool a protons (∆ωa), such that
kex = (kba +kab) ≪∆ω = (∆ωb −∆ωa), then each time a magnetization transfer takes place from
b to a, the transferred pool b spins will occupy a random position in the (X,Y)-plane relative to
the pool a spins. Hereby the spins will not add up coherently to enhance the signal, but instead
be phase-scrambled and add up to zero (Figure 2.7b) [11].
Figure 2.7: Magnetization of a spin population involved in slow chemical exchange. (a) at the
start of the free induction decay (FID), (b) after some time. From [11].
The eﬀect of slow exchange between two pools (compared to no exchange) is line broadening.
This is due to an apparent relaxation rate that can be observed to be kab faster than the true
relaxation rate of pool a, R2a.
In the fast exchange situation, where kex ≫∆ω, each spin exchange so fast between the
diﬀerent precession frequencies (∆ωa and ∆ωb) that it does not precess with either frequency.
Instead one will observe an average chemical shift dependent on the proton fractions of the two
pools: ∆ωav = fa·∆ωa+fb·∆ωb. However, if the exchange rate is not extremely fast, the averaging
becomes ineﬃcient and one will observe some line broadening. Hence in the slow exchange
regime the line broadening increases with an increase in exchange rate, and in the fast regime
the line broadening increases with slower exchange. Notice that in the CEST experiment where
fb ≪fa, the average chemical shift will be ∆ωav ≈∆ωa in the fast regime, which theoretically
makes pool b, the CEST pool, undetectable.
Figure 2.8 exempliﬁes how the line shapes of an exchanging system is aﬀected in terms of
exchange regime. This is done through an arbitrary exchange system with two pools (A and B),
where ∆ω = ωA −ωB, kex = kA +kB, and fB = (fA)−1 = 2. Notice again that the proton fraction
in pool A compared to pool B is much bigger compared to that in a typical CEST experiment
(with water and a much smaller solute pool), as it better illustrates the changes in line shapes
across exchange regimes directly. The eﬀect of the intermediate exchange regime is illustrated
at the central lines in Figure 2.8. The exchange systems in the intermediate regime are more
diﬃcult to interpret because, as the name indicates, they are somewhere in between the other
two regimes. The intermediate regime spectra have very broad line shapes covering multiple
frequencies diﬃcult to distinguish.
==PAGE:42==
18
Chapter 2. Background
Figure 2.8: Exchange regime diﬀerences in line shapes for a two pool exchange system with
chemical shifts ωA and ωB and exchange rates kA and kB. Edited version from [12].
In the CEST community it is stated that a CEST agent has to fulﬁll the slow to intermediate
exchange condition on the nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) time scale in order to be able to
achieve successful contrast [3–7]. This condition is deﬁned as:
kex ≤∆ω
(2.15)
This requirement makes sense when studying the eﬀects of slow versus fast exchange in Figure 2.8,
since slow exchange seems to be necessary in order to keep the two pools separable for selective
saturation. However, despite that fact, published papers exist that claim to be able to image
relatively fast exchanging CEST agents (more or less aware of the fact), where Eq. (2.15) does
not apply [13, 14]. This is promising for the aim of this thesis as many clinically relevant CEST
agents are within the fast regime at 3 T and as it suggests that the requirement of Eq. (2.15) might
not be as strict as previously stated [3–7].
2.3
cest challenges and opportunities
The CEST technique comes with great promises of the ability to image important physiological
and pathological parameters, such as pH and metabolite levels, non-invasively (using endogenous
CEST agents) and with much improved spatial resolution compared to the MRSI methods. Various
endogenous CEST agents hereby serve as potential non-ionizing biomarkers of disease diagnosis
and treatment monitoring. Speciﬁc examples will follow at the end of the chapter in Section 2.3.1.
Current methods for molecular imaging in vivo include positron emission tomography (PET),
optical imaging, and, as already mentioned, MRSI. While PET has high sensitivity, it uses radioac-
tive isotopes, often with short half-lives, and has suboptimal spatial resolution. Optical imaging
==PAGE:43==
2.3. CEST Challenges and Opportunities
19
is mainly challenged in terms of penetration depth, which currently limits the success of the
technique to small-animal models. MRSI has already been used for quantiﬁcation of a wide range
of metabolites, but poor spatial resolution as well as long acquisition times challenge the clinical
use of MRSI [7].
However, the full potential of the CEST approach is also yet to be reached. And there are
several challenges to be conquered before CEST can be translated successfully to the clinic. First
of all, the two-pool CEST system shown in Figure 2.5 is much too simple to describe the in
vivo environment. Multiple possible types of magnetization transfer pathways exist that may
contribute to the Z-spectrum in a saturation transfer experiment [3]. The pathways are complex
and usually involve both chemical exchange and dipolar cross-relaxation5 simultaneously or
sequentially. To get an overview of the pathways contributing to the Z-spectrum (and possibly
contaminating it, depending on the perspective), they are divided into three diﬀerent groups based
on their imaging features: magnetization transfer (MT), the nuclear Overhauser eﬀect (NOE)
and CEST [5]. The in vivo Z-spectrum is a convolution of all present pathway contributions.
Modeling an in vivo CEST system hereby require a multi-pool model.
MT
is related to saturation transfer through chemical exchange and/or cross-relaxation to water
protons from macromolecular protons in the solid or semi-solid phase with short T2 relaxation
times (on the order of µs). Figure 2.9 illustrates a typical MT saturation transfer experiment with
a very broad MT signal visible in the Z-spectrum. The broad linewidth is caused by the short T2
and is usually on the order of 100 kHz. Hereby the MT eﬀect will inevitably aﬀect all CEST eﬀects
coming from endogenous agents (having small chemical shifts). However, luckily enough the
MT signal can often be assumed (approximately) symmetric around the water frequency, which
means that the MTRasym approach from Eq. (2.12) to extract an isolated CEST eﬀect is still valid,
at least in a three-pool system including only water, MT, and CEST eﬀects.
5Cross-relaxation refers to zero or double quantum transitions between energy states in a dipole-dipole coupled
spin system. Further explanation is beyond the scope of this thesis, but can be found in [11].
==PAGE:44==
20
Chapter 2. Background
Figure 2.9:
Saturation transfer experiment to detect MT eﬀects caused by macromolecular
protons in the solid phase (referred to as “‘bound’ water” in the ﬁgure). The outer left spectrum
is the Z-spectrum indicating relative water saturation as a function of saturation frequency oﬀset.
Edited ﬁgure from [8].
The NOE
emerges from dipolar interactions between physically closely placed spins that
result in cross-relaxation transfer, exchange relayed transfer, or a combination of both. In the
Z-spectrum NOE eﬀects are most often observed between water and aliphatic protons (protons of
non-aromatic hydrocarbons) in the chemical shift diﬀerence range of -1 to -5 ppm, which means
that they resonate upﬁeld relative to water, i.e. opposite to endogenous CEST agents resonating
downﬁeld (positive chemical shift diﬀerences) [6]. Figure 2.10 gives a good example of how the
presence of NOEs aﬀects the Z-spectrum. The ﬁgure shows Z-spectra for glycosaminoglycans
(GAGs) in solution and in tissue. In solution, CEST eﬀects from both amide (–NH) and hydroxyl
(–OH) groups are clearly visible, resonating at separable frequencies (Figure 2.10b). In tissue
NOEs are present as well, resonating at −1.0 and −2.6 ppm corresponding to the –CH and
N-acetyl in GAG (Figure 2.10c). Figure 2.10c also demonstrates the eﬀect of the broad MT signal
present in tissue.
CEST
itself might also be a nuisance factor in the CEST experiment. The nuisance arise when
more than one CEST agent is present at the same time, which is normally the case for in vivo
studies. Hereby several CEST eﬀects will contribute to the Z-spectrum simultaneously, which
might make them diﬃcult to distinguish, especially if they have similar chemical shifts and
consequently overlap.
One approach to deal with the multiple eﬀects present in the in vivo CEST experiment is
multi-Lorentzian ﬁtting of the Z-spectrum [15–18]. As the MTRasym will not reveal an isolated
CEST eﬀect if the asymmetry assumption of the MT eﬀect is invalid, if NOEs are present opposite
to the resonance of the CEST agent of interest, or if the line shapes of other CEST agents overlap,
Lorentzian ﬁtting is suggested as an alternative and more speciﬁc CEST eﬀect identiﬁcation
method. The main downside to Lorentzian ﬁtting is a strong dependence on starting values and
boundary conditions. The right choice of these require suﬃcient prior knowledge of the spectrum
components, which might be diﬃcult to achieve [16].
==PAGE:45==
2.3. CEST Challenges and Opportunities
21
Figure 2.10: Z-spectra for glycosaminoglycans (GAGs) in solution and in cartilage showing
CEST and NOE eﬀects at 11.7 T. Corresponding MRS 1H spectra with cutoﬀwater peaks are shown
at the top of the Z-spectra for reference. a) Structure of the GAG-unit, showing three hydroxyl
groups (–OH), an amide proton (–NH) and several aliphatic protons in the ring (–CH) and the
N-Acetyl side chain. b) Z-spectra of 125 mM GAG units in solution, showing predominantly
amide and hydroxyl exchange eﬀects. c) Z-spectra of cartilage from bovine patella in PBS buﬀer
showing exchange and much increased NOE eﬀects together with a broad MT eﬀect. From [3].
Besides the challenge of isolating speciﬁc CEST eﬀects from other concurring magnetization
transfer pathways, it can also be a challenge to dissociate the eﬀects from intrinsic properties of
the CEST experiment. Based on the simpliﬁed analytical solution to the pure CEST eﬀect given as
the proton transfer ratio (PTR) in Eq. (2.13), the CEST eﬀect increases with proton concentration
of the CEST pool and with greater exchange rate (within the limits of slow exchange). These
are the two main inﬂuential parameters of interest, as the CEST eﬀect through these can reveal
information about the concentration of the CEST agent as well as its chemical environment in
terms of pH. However, as can be seen in Eq. (2.13), the relaxation time T1 of the water pool also
inﬂuences the CEST eﬀect. Some papers even claim that the relaxation properties of the water
pool can dominate the CEST eﬀects if left uncompensated [18, 19].
A recent publication from 2015 by Zaiss et al. [18] suggests AREX (apparent exchange-
dependent relaxation) as a metric to make CEST eﬀects independent of T1 relaxation, while also
properly isolating the eﬀects when combined with multi-Lorentzian ﬁtting. The publication
criticizes the CEST community for using the asymmetry analysis method MTRasym too often
and uncritically, while also criticizing the community for ignoring eﬀects of MT and relaxation
properties even when the CEST eﬀects have been properly isolated. Part of the conclusion of the
==PAGE:46==
22
Chapter 2. Background
publication, with respect to its own study results, is that many previous CEST papers claiming to
show pure CEST eﬀects, possibly rather showed water relaxation shine-through eﬀects.
Diﬀerent controllable and uncontrollable experimental parameters also have major eﬀects on
the outcome of the CEST experiment. B0 ﬁeld inhomogeneities caused by local susceptibility-
induced ﬁeld shifts can induce frequency variations as high as 1 ppm in regions near air-ﬁlled
cavities, while smaller variations can be seen across the entire region of interest (ROI). These
frequency shifts leave signiﬁcant traces in the Z-spectrum in terms of displacements of the
chemical shifts with respect to the frequency selective saturation pulses. Before the Z-spectrum
is post-processed, it is therefore important that any B0 inhomogeneities are compensated. An
example of the signiﬁcance of B0 inhomogeneity compensation is given in the review paper by
Kogan et al.: “While a 20% GagCEST asymmetry was originally reported in cartilage at 3T, later
studies showed that proper correction of B0 inhomogeneities lead to negligible GagCEST contrast at
3T” [7]. B1 ﬁeld inhomogeneities across the ROI also aﬀect the extracted CEST eﬀects, as they
induce variations in saturation eﬃciency and thereby non-physiological variations in the CEST
eﬀect.
In relation to the controllable experimental parameters careful considerations should be made
for both the choice of saturation pulse (or pulses) for magnetization preparation and for the choice
of the subsequent imaging pulse sequence. The most simple and often made choice of saturation
pulse is a long continuous wave (CW) rectangular pulse. The main advantages of choosing
a CW pulse are high saturation eﬃciency and straightforward optimization through only two
parameters; saturation power (B1) and saturation time (tsat). However clinical translation of CEST
imaging using CW pulses can be problematic, especially with respect to hardware constraints as
most clinical scanners have ampliﬁer duty cycle limitations that do not allow suﬃciently long
CW pulses for saturation. As an alternative many papers choose to use a train of selective shaped
pulses instead, often called pulsed-CEST. Optimization of this approach is complicated by the
many choices of waveforms for the pulse shape [4].
No matter the choice of saturation pulse(s) characteristics, however, the right choices of
saturation power and saturation time are vital for optimal CEST eﬀect detection. Typically B1
should be optimized experimentally for each new in vivo application, since a high saturation
power might be favorable in some applications to reach optimal saturation eﬃciency for fast
exchanging species, while lower B1 amplitudes might be desired in other applications to maintain
spectral selectivity and limit Z-spectrum contamination from other eﬀects, while staying within
speciﬁc absorption rate (SAR) limits. Extra attention to SAR limits should be given at high B0
ﬁelds as SAR is proportional to both B12 and the square of the static magnetic ﬁeld (B02). The
saturation time, tsat, will in most cases be optimal, when it is long enough for the exchange system
to reach steady state. How long it takes for the steady state to be reached is again dependent on
the speciﬁc in vivo application in question, as the steady state criterion depends on the speciﬁc
saturation pulse method applied and the relaxation times of the exchange system. However,
since the spillover eﬀect is also increased with long tsat an optimal saturation time may exist
where spillover is not yet fully eﬀective, but steady state is also yet to be reached [6]. Without
steady state assumptions, though, any analytic interpretation of the Z-spectrum will be greatly
==PAGE:47==
2.3. CEST Challenges and Opportunities
23
challenged.
To return to the choice of pulse sequences, the choice of imaging sequence might also aﬀect
the CEST experiment. The use of fast imaging sequences is necessary to reduce scan times and
obtain acceptable temporal resolution for dynamic contrast investigations. But research in how
the imaging sequence parameters (e.q. k-space sampling scheme, repetition times (TR), and ﬂip
angle) should be optimized for CEST imaging is limited.
To sum up, the CEST technique is surely challenged on many levels, both for the choices
of how to image the eﬀects but also for the post-processing when inhomogeneities need to be
compensated and CEST and other eﬀects need to be isolated. As one conclusion, related to the
molecular speciﬁcity of the CEST experiment, in the review paper by Vinogradov et al. states:
“Thus, the question rises, is the experiment selective enough to a speciﬁc molecule, or
are we detecting complex eﬀects proportional to some complex combination of diﬀerent
molecular contributions. To the best of our knowledge, today, there is no deﬁnite answer
to this question.” [5]
However the technique still holds great promises of clinical opportunities if the right precautions
are taken and no hasty conclusions are made.
2.3.1
Molecular Targets
Focusing on endogenous CEST agents, this section will present clinically relevant and promising
molecular targets for the CEST technique in general, as well as the speciﬁc molecular target of this
thesis, Myo-Inositol (MI). Table 2.1 gives an overview of important properties of the exchanging
proton groups amide (–NH), amine (–NH2) and hydroxyl (–OH).
The by far most investigated CEST eﬀects are caused by amide proton transfer abbreviated
aptCEST. As stated in Table 2.1 aptCEST is not suitable for generating a speciﬁc metabolite
contrast, as its eﬀects originate from multiple (unknown) endogenous proteins and peptides.
Instead aptCEST have been explored for pH imaging and diﬀerentiation between glioma and
radiation necrosis based on general cytosolic peptide and protein content [20]. CEST eﬀects from
aptCEST have been shown to strongly correlate with pH because the exchange rate for the –NH
groups is slowed down dramatically with decreasing pH. This is especially in clinical interest
with respect to diagnosis of acute ischemia causing acidosis damages in connection with a stroke
[5, 21]. The main reason to the greater interest in aptCEST (measured in number of publications)
compared to other endogenous CEST agents, is the amide protons’ excellent properties for the
CEST experiment. As stated in Table 2.1 amide protons are safely placed within the slow exchange
regime (∆ω ≫kex), even at 3 T. Therefore the main challenge of aptCEST is interpreting the
eﬀects physiologically, while isolating them from contaminating factors.
Where aptCEST has the disadvantage of uncertain physiological signiﬁcance, this is less
of a challenge for gluCEST originating from CEST imaging of the amine (–NH2) groups of
glutamate. The ability to perform in vivo glutamate imaging non-invasively and at high spatial
and temporal resolution will open up for great diagnostic opportunities, especially within the ﬁeld
of neuropsychiatry. This is because glutamate is one of the major excitatory neurotransmitters in

the brain and is likely to be involved in nearly all signal processing functions of the central nervous
system (CNS). The exchange rate of the –NH2 groups of glutamate are also very dependent
on pH values, why gluCEST has been investigated for pH imaging, as well. The ﬁrst paper on
gluCEST (from 2012) claimed to be able to obtain gluCEST mapping of a healthy human brain,
demonstrated by white and grey matter separation and PET imaging as reference [22]. A recent
paper from 2015 investigated the use of gluCEST as an imaging biomarker of epileptic seizure
foci [23]. Both of these two gluCEST papers were based on imaging on a 7 T scanner in order to
approach the slow to intermediate exchange regime (see Table 2.1, the amine groups of glutamate
has a chemical shift diﬀerence of 3.0 ppm). However, both papers also chose to use the MTRasym
metric for CEST eﬀect evaluation. While the most recent paper, [23], does so uncritically, the
paper from 2012 does at least acknowledge the possible contributions to the measured eﬀect
from some other CEST agents: “About 70–75 % of the observed CEST is from glutamate, with the
remaining 25–30 % coming from creatine, GABA and other macromolecules.”, but it does not mention
possible NOE contamination, relaxation eﬀects, or possible concurring eﬀects from aptCEST at
3.5 ppm [22].
CEST imaging of hydroxyl (–OH) groups was the topic of another recent publication “Inves-
tigating Hydroxyl Chemical Exchange Using a Variable Saturation Power Chemical Exchange
Saturation Transfer (vCEST) Method at 3 T” by Clark et al. [14]. The aim of the study is similar to
the one of this thesis (studying hydroxyl exchange at 3 T), why their proposed method vCEST also
was sought implemented in this study. The paper claims to be able to minimize the challenges of
imaging fast exchange by creating a MTRdiﬀmetric, which is not calculated based on asymmetry
assumptions and is uninﬂuenced by the spillover eﬀect. By applying a saturation power linearly
dependent on the chemical shift diﬀerence, the DWS spectrum will theoretically become constant
making it trivial to remove from the Z-spectrum by simple subtraction. The paper suggests
==PAGE:49==
2.3. CEST Challenges and Opportunities
25
numerical simulation of the DWS spectrum by means of experimentally measured relaxation
times and ﬁeld inhomogeneities. This does however make the method extremely sensitive to the
accuracy and precision of these measurement techniques. The MTRdiﬀis calculated as:
MTRdiﬀ(∆ω) = Ssat,DWS(∆ω)
S0,DWS
−Ssat(∆ω)
S0
(2.16)
where Ssat,DWS and S0,DWS are the simulated spectra of the DWS with and without saturation,
respectively.
The paper by Clark et al. [14] conducts its studies by investigating glycogen and glucose
in vitro and gagCEST in vivo (all hydroxyl exchange). The CEST eﬀects are evaluated as the
‘area under the curve’ (AUC) of the MTRdiﬀcurve within speciﬁc chemical shift diﬀerence ranges.
With several eﬀects overlapping in the in vivo MTRdiﬀcurve, this evaluation method does not
reveal completely isolated CEST eﬀects in vivo. Furthermore, relaxation eﬀects are not mentioned,
however, it is mentioned as future work to test the sensitivity of the method towards relaxation
at the end of the paper.
Myo-Inositol
Myo-Inositol (MI) is the main molecular target of this thesis. It is a cyclic sugar alcohol with six
exchangeable hydroxyl groups. MICEST imaging is CEST imaging of the hydroxyl (–OH) groups
of MI, which resonate at 0.5−1.0 ppm downﬁeld from water and have exchange rates ranging from
500−1500 s−1 (Table 2.1). The CEST eﬀects seen at the MI resonances are sometimes referred to
as MICEST eﬀects. The exchange and chemical shift properties of MI places it in the fast exchange
regime at 3 T, since there is far more exchange processes occurring per second compared to spin
revolutions per second. Figure 2.11 shows high-resolution NMR spectra (acquired at 9.4 T) of
200 mM MI dissolved in distilled water from [13]. Notice that the resonance axis depicts the
chemical shift diﬀerence relative to water identical to that in the Z-spectrum. Figure 2.11a shows
the acquired spectrum at pH 6.0 together with the chemical structure of MI. Figure 2.11b shows
the spectrum at pH 7.0. The chemical exchange rate of the hydroxyl groups of MI is slower
at a pH-value of 6.0 compared to at 7.0, which is evident in Figure 2.11 by the more distinct
peaks around 1 ppm in Figure 2.11a compared to the broad resonance peak covering multiple
frequencies in Figure 2.11b. Typical 1H MRSI of MI takes the –CH peaks as its starting point
(normally the doublet-of-doublets resonating around −1.2 ppm upﬁeld from water, corresponding
to ∼3.5 ppm on the usual NMR axis [8]).
MI is primarily located in glial cells6 where it is known to function as an osmolyte. Practically
this means that during osmotic stress, the osmotic balance is preserved by regulation of MI
transport across the cell membrane. MI is a precursor of membrane phospho-inositides and
phospholipids and it is also involved in cell membrane and myelin sheet structures. Increased
membrane decomposition or damage to the myelin sheets can result in increased concentrations of
free MI. MI has been proposed as a glial marker, in which context an increase in MI concentration
6Glial cells make up the connective tissue of the nervous system, consisting of several diﬀerent types of cells
associated with neurons [2].
==PAGE:50==
26
Chapter 2. Background
Figure 2.11: High-resolution 1H NMR spectra of 200 mM MI and distilled water acquired at
9.4 T. (A) MI spectrum at pH 6.0 showing the three –OH peaks downﬁeld from water. Resonances
from the –CH groups are shown upﬁeld. The inset shows the chemical structure of MI. (B) The
MI spectrum at pH 7.0 shows the –CH resonances as well as a broad –OH resonance [13].
is interpreted as glial cell proliferation in response to damage to the CNS. The detection of MI is
challenged with current methods (low spatial resolution for MRSI), but MI concentration levels
have nonetheless been investigated in response to several diseases. Elevated MI levels have
been observed in Alzheimer’s disease, gliomatosis cerebri, diabetes mellitus, recovering hypoxia,
progressive multifocal leukoencephalopathy, systemic lupus erythematosus, familial hemiplegic
migraine, in some patients with canavan disease, and in multiple sclerosis [13]. Decreased brain
levels of MI have been observed in chronic hepatic and hypoxic encephalopathies, stroke, acute
thyrotoxic Graves’ disease, toxoplasmosis, cryptococcosis, and lymphoma [13]. Healthy levels of
MI in the human brain range from 4-9 mM according to Table 2.2 in [8].
The ﬁrst MICEST paper from 2011 by Harris et al. [13], pursued in vivo brain mapping of
MI in a healthy human brain. The exchange rate of MI was estimated to be ∼600 s−1 measured
at 9.4 T, pH 7.0, and at room temperature (∼25◦). At 7 T the relative resonance frequencies in
units of Hz of the MI hydroxyl groups range from 149 Hz to 447 Hz (Table 2.1), which means that
the exchange takes place in the fast regime. However, since Harris et al. evaluate the resonance
frequencies in units of radians per second when they compare ∆ω to kex, i.e. [149−447 Hz]·
2π = [936−2809 rad/s], they wrongly deduce that the exchange takes place in the slow regime.
Nevertheless, they do manage to get MTRasym curves spanning the expected saturation oﬀsets,
and they also manage to observe a signiﬁcantly higher MICEST asymmetry in white matter
==PAGE:51==
2.3. CEST Challenges and Opportunities
27
(5.2±0.5 %) compared to gray matter (4.3±0.5 %) in a study of 5 healthy subjects. They conclude
that the observed MICEST contrast predominantly is due to endogenous MI concentration, based
on an in vitro evaluation of other CEST eﬀect contributions to the Z-spectrum [13]. Hereby
possible MT, NOE, and relaxation eﬀects do not receive any recognition.
A more recent paper about MICEST imaging from 2013 was also written by Harris et al. [24].
In this paper, the MICEST contrast was investigated in transgenic mouse models of Alzheimer’s
disease at 9.4 T (hereby approaching the intermediate exchange regime). The ﬁve mice with
Alzheimer’s disease showed a 50 % higher MICEST asymmetry compared to ﬁve age matched wild
type controls. The results were validated with increased MI concentration measured with MRS
and with immunohistochemistry showing glial cell proliferation. They decided that Z-spectrum
contributions from other metabolites with exchangeable protons would be negligible, based on
their used saturation parameters and assumed brain concentrations of the other metabolites. The
eﬀect of DWS was also evaluated, with the conclusion that it might have caused underestimations
of the MICEST contrast by decreasing the saturation transfer eﬃciency. Possible MT, NOE,
and relaxation eﬀects are ignored. The conclusions of the paper are not aimed towards human
application, probably due to the high (non-clinical) ﬁeld strength, but instead suggest that the
presented results show how MICEST mapping could aid drug discovery eﬀorts in transgenic
models of Alzheimer’s disease [7, 24].
To sum up, MI has the potential of being a biomarker for several diseases. The MICEST
research publications found in the literature study for this thesis are few in numbers and are all
related to higher ﬁeld strengths. Hereby fulﬁllment of the objectives of this thesis could provide
novel information to the CEST research ﬁeld.
==PAGE:52==
==PAGE:53==
THEORY
3
This chapter will give theoretical descriptions of essential tools needed for the applied methods
of this thesis.
As a theoretical starting point the Bloch equations will be presented. Next McConnell’s
modiﬁcations of the Bloch equations to incorporate the properties of a chemical exchange system
will be outlined. Following, an analytical solution to the spillover eﬀect in the Z-spectrum will
be given as it relates to the vCEST method. And last, but not least, the chapter will include a
description of the saturation pulse applied for the CEST imaging in this thesis and its eﬀect on
the nuclear spins in a CEST experiment.
3.1
bloch eqations
The Bloch equations are a set of coupled diﬀerential equations, which are used to describe the
dynamics of nuclear magnetization in the presence of a magnetic ﬁeld. They were ﬁrst stated in
the iconic NMR paper “Nuclear Induction” by Bloch in 1946 [25]. Using left-handed convention
for rotation, the Bloch equations are given as:
dM

where M is the three-dimensional net magnetization vector, B is the three-dimensional applied
magnetic ﬁeld vector, γ is the gyromagnetic ratio, T1 and T2 are the relaxation time constants,
M0 is the equilibrium magnetization caused by the static ﬁeld B0, and i, j, and k are unit vectors
in the x-, y-, and z-directions, respectively. The cross-product relation describes the precessional
behavior of the net magnetization vector, while the relaxation terms describe exponential decay
and restoration of the transverse and longitudinal components, respectively. The exponential
behavior is an approximation [9]. In matrix-vector notation, Eq. (3.1) is given as:

3.2
bloch-mcconnell eqations
The Bloch-McConnell (BM) equations represent a modiﬁcation of the Bloch equations to describe
the magnetic resonance dynamics of a single nuclear species, which is transferred back and fourth
between two (or more) magnetic environments by molecular processes. The BM equations were
ﬁrst presented by McConnell in his paper “Reaction Rates by Nuclear Magnetic Resonance” from
1958 [26]. The BM equation system provides a useful starting point for the analysis of both slow,
intermediate and fast chemical exchange studied under various conditions.
The simplest way to present the BM equations is using a two-pool exchange system, like the
one given in Eq. (2.10) and depicted schematically in Figure 3.1 with assigned properties of the
two pools in terms of relaxation rates, exchange rates and equilibrium magnetizations.
==PAGE:55==
3.2. Bloch-McConnell Equations
31
Figure 3.1: Two-site exchange system with assigned properties for the BM equations. A small
pool b reﬂecting the exchangeable CEST agent protons and a larger pool a reﬂecting the bulk
water protons. R1, R2, and M0 are the longitudinal relaxation rate, the transverse relaxation rate,
and the thermal equilibrium magnetization, respectively. kba and kba are the exchange rates.
Edited version from [27].

by the frequency ωRF of the oscillating B1 ﬁeld, then the time evolution of the magnetization
components for the spins in pool a (Mxa, Mya, Mza) and the spins in pool b (Mxb, Myb, Mzb)

and ∆ωb are the precessional frequencies (dependent on B0) of the two pools, respectively, relative
to the current frequency oﬀset ωRF of the RF saturation pulse, such that e.g. ∆ωa = ωRF −ωa. And
M0a and M0b are the thermal equilibrium magnetizations, proportional to the proton fractions
of the respective pools (see Eq. (2.11)) [28, 29]. Notice, however, that the BM equations have
adopted a right-handed convention for rotations. In terms of right-handed sign convention the
Larmor equation is given as: ω = −γB, in order to result in clockwise rotation (negative sense in
a right-handed coordinate system) for the positive gyromagnetic ratio of protons.
==PAGE:56==
32
Chapter 3. Theory
The BM equations can be expanded to account for multiple exchanging CEST pools simultane-
ously by at ﬁrst adding dimensions to the magnetization vector e.g. making it 9-dimensional for a
three-pool system instead of 6-dimensional as in the two-pool case. And of course by expanding
the A matrix, e.g. to a 9×9 matrix while adding the right interactions. In BM equations accounting
for more concurring eﬀects, exchange eﬀects between other pools than water are often assumed
negligible.
A numerical solution example to the BM equations will be given in the special situation of a
phase cycled preparation pulse for saturation, identical to the one used for the experimental part
of this thesis. The example will be given in Section 3.4 of this chapter after describing the pulse
sequence. In addition to this, how the BM equations can be used for numerical simulations of a
CEST experiment is described in Section 4.2 of the methods chapter.
3.3
direct water saturation
It is diﬃcult to obtain exact analytical solutions to the BM equations without making some
assumptions. A widely used approach to solving the equations analytically is the weak saturation
pulse (WSP) approximation. In the WSP approximation it is assumed that no direct water
saturation occurs when the RF ﬁeld is applied to the solute pool, i.e. that when ∆ωb = 0, then
∆ωa →∞in Eq. (3.7). Assuming steady state of the exchange process as well, the DWS as a
function of relaxation rates, exchange rates, and RF pulse amplitude and frequency oﬀset can be
expressed as follows, given by [14, 15]:
