non-trivial to remove [14]. This makes the vCEST method very prone to B0 inhomogeneity.
3.4
phase cycled rectangular rf preparation pulse
Phase Cycle CEST, a phase cycled rectangular RF preparation pulse for CEST imaging, was
developed by Mitsuharu Miyoshi for GE Healthcare Japan in 2014 [28] and ﬁrst presented in
an abstract for the International Society for Magnetic Resonance in Medicine (ISMRM) Annual
Meeting 2014. It was developed as an alternative to CW pulse preparation and pulsed-CEST
preparation. The motivation for its development was that CW saturation pulses are diﬃcult to
implement clinically due to RF ampliﬁer limitations on clinical scanners, while pulsed-CEST
Z-spectra can be diﬃcult to interpret both analytically and if the used pulse shape induces side
lobes in the spectra.
Figure 3.2 shows the pulse sequence chart of the Phase Cycle CEST preparation pulse. The
pulse sequence consists of a train of rectangular pulses with a pulse width (or pulse duration)
==PAGE:58==
34
Chapter 3. Theory
of tp = 0.232 ms and a total iteration time (time from the start of one pulse to the start of the
next) of Titer = 0.560 ms. Following the rectangular pulses is a so-called killer gradient, which is
also referred to as a spoiler gradient in some literature, as it spoils or kills any residual transverse
magnetization before the image acquisition begins. As Figure 3.2 implies, the imaging sequence
applied after the killer gradient is a fast imaging SE-SSEPI sequence. The combined scanner
sequence implementation of the Phase Cycle CEST preparation pulse, the killer gradient and
the SE-SSEPI sequence has been named EPI CEST. The saturation time tsat of the preparation
pulse is given by the number of rectangular pulses times the iteration time. Hereby, to get a total
saturation time of e.g. tsat = 3 s, one needs to apply approximately 5357 rectangular pulses.
Figure 3.2: Pulse sequence chart of Phase Cycle CEST showing the pulse preparation consisting
of phase cycled rectangular pulses, a killer gradient, and image acquisition by means of a single-
shot spin-echo EPI fast imaging sequence. From [30].
Each rectangular RF pulse is non-selective and applied on-resonance with water. The train of
rectangular RF pulses is phase cycled. For this sequence, phase cycling means that the RF phase
(which provides the rotation axis of the excitation), φi, of each rectangular pulse is ∆φ diﬀerent
from the previous. I.e. ∆φ = φn+1 −φn, and for the nth pulse the RF phase is φn = n ·∆φ.
Each MRI scan using EPI CEST with the Phase Cycle CEST pulse preparation reconstructs to
one MR image and one sample in the Z-spectrum for each voxel. The location of that sample on
the saturation oﬀset axis in each Z-spectrum is determined by a combination of the applied phase
diﬀerence ∆φ for the phase cycling and the iteration time, Titer.
How the saturation is created is best explained by following the magnetization dynamics in
the rotating frame of reference: The phase of the ﬁrst RF pulse is φ1 = 0, which will result in
excitation of all spins from their equilibrium state. Following, all proton spins, other than those
of water, will precess with a frequency given by their chemical shift diﬀerence, ∆ω, relative to
water. After the iteration time, Titer, all those spins will each have accumulated a certain phase.
The second rectangular pulse is applied with a phase diﬀerence ∆φ compared to the previous and
hereby cause rotation of the coordinate system, i.e. the rotating frame of reference. Rotating the
coordinate system in one direction is equivalent to rotating the spins in the opposite direction.
Hereby the second rectangular pulse will result in the most eﬃcient excitation for those spins
that have accumulated a phase exactly equal to ∆φ. And likewise, when the next pulse is applied,
after another iteration time period, the same spins that were most eﬃciently excited before will
again experience the most eﬃcient excitation. By exciting the same spins multiple times, not
==PAGE:59==
3.4. Phase Cycled Rectangular RF Preparation Pulse
35
allowing full longitudinal recovery, these will eventually become more and more saturated with
less Mz magnetization, approaching a steady-state. I.e. by tuning the phase diﬀerence to the
phase accumulated during the iteration time for spins with a certain chemical shift diﬀerence,
one will obtain a sample in the Z-spectrum at a saturation oﬀset equal to that speciﬁc chemical
shift diﬀerence.
After the full rectangular pulse train and after the killer gradient has dephased all residual
transverse magnetization, the data acquisition begins. Hereby the water signal, being more or
less saturated dependent on the phase diﬀerence, will be spatially mapped to reveal an MR image.
The pulse sequence was originally developed with the purpose of imaging aptCEST on a 3 T
scanner, why Titer was chosen such that the amide protons after this time, due to their chemical
shift diﬀerence of ∆ω = 3.5 ppm, would have a phase increment of -π/2 (one quarter of a full
revolution in radians). This calculation was done as follows:
Titer = 1
4 ·
1
3 T·42.58 MHz/T·3.5 ppm = 1
4 ·
1
127.74 Hz/ppm·3.5 ppm = 0.559 ms
(3.13)
Hereby the maximum spectral bandwidth obtainable with the Phase Cycle CEST sequence is
14 ppm (Z-spectrum from 7 to −7 ppm) corresponding to applying phase diﬀerences ranging
from π to −π.
Figure 3.3 shows how a full Z-spectrum with 16 samples can be acquired for each voxel in
a two-dimensional MR image. The top of the ﬁgure shows a chosen axial slice of the imaged
subject. This same slice is then imaged 16 times with the EPI CEST sequence with a new phase
diﬀerence for the phase cycling in Phase Cycle CEST for each image. The bottom of the ﬁgure
shows a Z-spectrum composed of the water signal measured in the same voxel g1 for each image.
The eighth pulse sequence is applied with a phase diﬀerence ∆φ = 0, which means that it will
saturate the non-precessing water most eﬃciently. Hereby it reveals the minimum point in the
Z-spectrum of relative water saturation. The 16-sample Z-spectrum is acquired with saturation
oﬀsets ranging from 7π/8 to −π resulting in a spectral resolution of (14 ppm)/16 = 0.875 ppm.

Phase Cycle CEST sequence for pulse preparation. Edited version from [31].

METHODS
4
In this chapter the speciﬁc methods applied to achieve the results of the thesis will be presented.
The results are given in the next chapter and origin from a total of seven simulations and three
scan session. To provide an overview of the simulations and scan sessions when presenting the
methods applied in connection with each one of them, they have been numbered in consecutive
order, i.e. as Simulation 1, Simulation 2, ..., Simulation 7, and Scan Session 1, Scan Session 2, Scan
Session 3.
The ﬁrst section in this chapter outlines the two diﬀerent CEST approaches tested in this
thesis – a ‘standard’ CEST approach and the vCEST approach. The next section will establish
how the BM equations introduced in Section 3.2 were rearranged to simulate a two-pool CEST
experiment using the Phase Cycle CEST preparation pulse. This section will also present an
overview of the performed simulations together with their individual simulation parameters.
Next the hardware used (scanner and coil) will be stated, followed by a description of the two
phantoms assembled for the scan sessions – Phantom 1 and Phantom 2. Then the pulse sequences
applied will be outlined and explained, while specifying exactly how they were implemented
for each scan session. And last the post-processing methods used to achieve the ﬁnal CEST
eﬀect results and the statistics used to evaluate them will be described. All data post-processing
was carried out in Matlab version: R2014b (8.4.0.150421), 64-bit (maci64), September 15, 2014
(MathWorks, Natick, MA, USA).
A very short summary of the content and purpose of each simulation and scan session is
given below.
Simulation 1: Reproduced aptCEST simulations from the ISMRM abstract by
Miyoshi et al. [28] with the purpose of validating simulation implementa-
tions of the Phase Cycle CEST sequence.
Simulation 2: MICEST simulations using pool system parameters from liter-
ature with the purpose of optimizing the MICEST eﬀects based on the
controllable sequence parameters, saturation time and ﬂip angle.
Scan Session 1: Scan session held on November 16, 2015 of Phantom 1 using
a 32 experimental design to test the eﬀects of the sequence parameters
saturation time and ﬂip angle.
Simulation 3: MICEST simulations with updated pool system parameters cor-
responding to the eﬀects seen in vitro.
Simulation 4: Reproduced simulation results from [14] using the vCEST ap-
proach to validate its simulation implementation using Phase Cycle CEST.
37
==PAGE:62==
38
Chapter 4. Methods
Simulation 5: MICEST simulations using the vCEST approach with the purpose
of investigating achievable eﬀects for the subsequent scan sessions.
Scan Session 2: Scan session held on December 10, 2015 of Phantom 1 using
the vCEST approach to evaluate achievable MI concentration contrast.
Scan Session 3: Scan session held on December 21, 2015 of Phantom 2 using
the vCEST approach to test pH dependence of the MICEST contrast.

4.1
cest imaging approaches
Two diﬀerent approaches to CEST imaging were applied, both using the Phase Cycle CEST
preparation pulse. The ﬁrst approach is a ‘standard’ CEST approach with constant saturation
power, while the second approach applies the vCEST method with variable saturation power
originally suggested by Clark et al. in 2015 [14] and ﬁrst introduced in this thesis in Section 2.3.1.
Since the scan sessions in this study were conducted using aqueous phantoms to investigate
the in vitro imaging properties of MICEST, contributions from other CEST agents, MT eﬀects,
and NOEs could be ignored, and the system could be treated as an approximate two-pool system.
Hereby the MICEST eﬀects from the simulated or measured Z-spectra obtained from using the
standard CEST approach were evaluated using asymmetry analysis by means of the MTRasym
metric (see Eq. (2.12)).
The vCEST approach relies on a simulation of the DWS spectrum in order to calculate the
metric MTRdiﬀfrom Eq. (2.16). For the simulations performed in this study using the vCEST
approach, the DWS spectrum was simulated similarly to the Z-spectrum using the same simula-
tion parameters, but with only the water pool present. For the scan sessions using the vCEST
approach the DWS spectra were simulated from experimentally measured relaxation times and
ﬁeld inhomogeneities.
The MICEST eﬀects from the vCEST approach were evaluated using the MTRdiﬀmetric.
To incorporate an in vivo perspective into the evaluation, each MTRdiﬀcurve was ﬁtted with
a Lorentzian in the ﬁnal MICEST eﬀect estimation. Hereby the evaluation method would be
adaptive towards a future multi-pool system by just transforming the single-Lorentzian ﬁtting
algorithm into a multi-Lorentzian.
The Lorentzian ﬁtting algorithm was implemented using the following function:
L(∆ω) = Lbase + A ·
Γ2/4
Γ2/4+(∆ωb −∆ω)2
(4.1)
==PAGE:63==
4.2. Bloch-McConnell Simulations
39
The ﬁtting of the MTRdiﬀcurves was done using nonlinear ﬁtting in the least-squares sense,
minimizing the least-squared error through choices of the ﬁt parameters baseline, Lbase, amplitude,
A, and full width at half maximum (FWHM, Γ) for the Lorentzian within certain boundaries. The
ﬁtting for the post-processing of the vCEST in vitro data also implemented ∆ωb as a ﬁt parameter
within certain boundaries, as the chemical shift diﬀerence of the MI pool in vitro was an uncertain
parameter. The ﬁnal MICEST eﬀect was estimated as the amplitude A of the ﬁtted Lorentzian.
Table 4.1 state the starting points and boundaries for the ﬁt parameters. The boundaries were
chosen experimentally and with inspiration from [17], which applies a multi-Lorentzian ﬁt model
for aptCEST. The lower boundary for the FWHM of the MTRdiﬀcurve for MICEST was set
relatively high due to the relatively fast exchange of MI and to avoid ﬁtting noise for the in vitro
data post-processing.
Table 4.1: Starting points and boundaries of all ﬁt parameters for the Lorentzian ﬁtting of the
MTRdiﬀcurves for vCEST. For the baseline parameter diﬀerent lower and upper boundaries were
used for the simulation and scan session ﬁttings. For the simulations using vCEST ∆ωb was not a
ﬁt parameter but given as part of the simulation input parameters.

4.2
bloch-mcconnell simulations
Several BM simulations were run during the course of the project period in order to gain insight
into the behavior of the Z-spectrum given diﬀerent experimental and exchange system parameters.
And as stated above, a total of seven simulations will be presented in this thesis. Common for all
simulations is that they were based on a two-pool exchange system and that they assumed a 3 T
clinical ﬁeld strength.
Simulation 1, featuring the ﬁrst simulations performed, was carried out with the purpose of
recreating simulation results presented by Miyoshi et al. in the abstract for the ISMRM Annual
Meeting 2014 [28]. One simulation was done to numerically simulate a Z-spectrum using a CW
pulse while assuming steady-state, and one numerical simulation was done of a Z-spectrum using
the Phase Cycle CEST pulse.
==PAGE:64==
40
Chapter 4. Methods
4.2.1
Bloch-McConnell simulation using a CW pulse
Simulation of a Z-spectrum using a CW pulse under the assumption of steady-state is fairly
simple given Eq. (3.6) as the starting point. Eq. (3.6) is here presented in a more compact form:
d
dt M(t) = A·M(t)+B
(4.2)
where M(t) is the six-dimensional magnetization vector of pool a and b given by M(t) = [Mxa,Mya,
Mza,Mxb,Myb,Mzb]T . The vector B is given as B = [0,0,R1aM0a,0,0,R1bM0b]T , and A is given
by Eq. (3.7). By assuming steady-state, i.e. when t →∞and dM(t)/dt = 0, it is possible to
express the following numerical solution by means of matrix inversion:
0 = A·M(∞)+B
⇔
M(∞) = −A−1 ·B
(4.3)
In order to simulate the Z-spectrum with N samples, M(∞) needs to be derived N times with
a new saturation oﬀset ωRF, and hereby change of the variables ∆ωa and ∆ωb in matrix A, for
each derivation. The ﬁnal simulated Z-spectrum is then given by the extracted longitudinal
magnetization of the water pool, Mza, normalized by the initial magnetization, M0a, as a function
of saturation oﬀset.
The Matlab implementation of the simulation is presented in Source Code C.3 in Appendix C.
4.2.2
Bloch-McConnell simulation using Phase Cycle CEST
Simulation of a Z-spectrum using the Phase Cycle CEST pulse involves a more iterative approach,
like the one introduced Miyoshi et al. in [28]. First of all, the iteration time Titer is divided into
16 simulation steps, which gives a discretization of the full phase cycled pulse train at:
∆t = Titer /16 = 0.560 ms/16 = 35 µs
(4.4)
intervals. Given a rectangular pulse width of e.g. Titer /2, each pulse is then divided into 8
simulation steps. In each time interval with the rectangular pulse applied, the magnetization
components of the two pools are subjected to the following changes:
M((n +1)∆t) = RF(∆t)·CE(∆t)·RE(∆t)·FP(∆t)·M(n∆t)+B(∆t)
(4.5)
where RF is an RF rotation matrix rotating the magnetization about the x-axis (right-handed
rotation), CE is a chemical exchange matrix, RE is a relaxation matrix, and FP is a free precession
matrix. The 6×6 interaction matrices and the six-dimensional vector B providing longitudinal
==PAGE:65==

In each time interval during the interpulse delay the magnetization components of the two
pools are subjected to changes caused by all interaction matrices except the RF matrix:
M((n +1)∆t) = CE(∆t)·RE(∆t)·FP(∆t)·M(n∆t)+B(∆t)
(4.11)
As explained in Section 3.4, the RF phase of each succeeding rectangular pulse is increased in
correspondence with the phase diﬀerence ∆φ of the current saturation oﬀset. With respect to

(4.12)
In order to provide a better overview of the practical simulation of a full Z-spectrum using
Phase Cycle CEST, a pseudo-code of the Matlab implementation is given below in Algorithm 1.
The full Matlab source code is given Source Code C.2 in Appendix C.
Input: all simulation variables
Output: Z-spectrum Z
initialize a magnetization array M;
foreach saturation oﬀset do
deﬁne RP matrix with respect to the current phase diﬀerence ∆φ(i);
forall the iteration times within the total saturation time do
if the RF pulse is on then
update M with respect to the following changes: M ←RF · CE · RE · FP · M + B;
else if the RF pulse is oﬀduring the interpulse delay then
update M with respect to the following changes: M ←CE · RE · FP · M + B;
end
update M with respect to the new RF phase: M ←RP · M;
end
end
extract the ﬁnal longitudinal magnetization of the water pool Mza as a function of
saturation oﬀset;
deﬁne Z-spectrum as the normalized water signal: Z = Mza/M0a;
Algorithm 1: Pseudo-code of a BM numerical simulation of the Z-spectrum of a two-pool
CEST system using the Phase Cycle CEST preparation pulse.
==PAGE:67==
4.2. Bloch-McConnell Simulations
43
As indicated in Algorithm 1 and as more clearly outlined in the Matlab code in Appendix C,
the BM simulation of a two-pool system requires several simulation variables as input. These
variables include both pool system parameters as well as sequence parameters.
Pool system simulation parameters:
First of all, in order to simulate the Z-spectrum using
Phase Cycle CEST, both pool a and pool b relaxation times are needed (T1a, T2a, T1b, and T2b),
and second, the chemical shift diﬀerence of pool b, the CEST pool, is required (∆ωb). In addition
to this the simulation also requires the chemical exchange rate of the system (kba), the pool ratio
and the initial magnetization of pool a, the water pool, in units of thermal polarization (M0a).
Sequence simulation parameters:
In terms of sequence parameters, the simulation requires
information about the total saturation time (tsat) and the power of the rectangular RF pulses
(B1). These two will at times in the following sections be referred to as the controllable sequence
parameters. The desired Z-spectrum resolution is also a required input.
A few changes to the Phase Cycle CEST simulation implementation were required for the
simulations applying the vCEST approach. First of all the sequence parameter input determining
the B1 power was changed to a variable dependent on the saturation oﬀset, and was hereby
required in units of [µT/ppm]. Secondly, the DWS spectrum was simulated as well, i.e. as a one-
pool system excluding the CEST pool. The Matlab implementation is given in Source Code C.14
in Appendix C. The implementation diﬀers from the pseudo-code in Algorithm 1 by also updating
the RF matrix for each saturation oﬀset to correspond to the current saturation power, and by
simulating the DWS curve without the CE matrix, i.e. ignoring the CEST pool interaction.
Furthermore the vCEST simulations for Simulation 6 also included oﬀ-resonance eﬀects of
the water pool by allowing B0 ﬁeld inhomogeneity as a simulation input. B0 ﬁeld inhomogeneity
will result in the water spins accumulating a certain phase during the iteration time. In order to
simulate this the free precession matrix FP was updated to be:
FP =


where φa is the phase accumulated by the water spins of pool a in units of radians per second.
Simulation 1
As already mentioned, the goal of the ﬁrst simulation was to validate the Matlab simulation
implementation of the Phase Cycle CEST sequence. This was attempted through reproducing
simulation results from the ISMRM abstract by Miyoshi et al. [28]. The simulations are based on
a two-pool aptCEST system, i.e. with a water pool (pool a) and an amide proton pool (pool b).
==PAGE:68==
44
Chapter 4. Methods
The simulations were done using the following parameters provided in the abstract [28]. The
pool system parameters: M0a = 0.99, T1a = 1500 ms, T2a = 60 ms, T1b = 750 ms, T2b = 30 ms, pool
ratio = 1.01 %, chemical shift diﬀerence ∆ωb = 3.5 ppm, and exchange rate kba = 148.5 s−1. The
sequence parameters used were: tsat = 3.5 s, mean B1 amplitude of 1.75 µT, and a Z-spectrum
resolution of 0.875 ppm (32 phase cycles between ∆φ = ±2π).
The simulation employed two changes to the Phase Cycle CEST preparation pulse compared
to how it is implemented in the EPI CEST scanner sequence. These changes were: 1) a greater
bandwidth with saturation oﬀsets ranging from 14 to -14 ppm (i.e. with phase diﬀerences from
2π to −2π), and 2) a pulse width tp = 0.28 ms instead of the actual tp = 0.232 ms.
Simulation 2
For the second simulation, the pool system was changed from an aptCEST system to a MICEST
system with exchangeable hydroxyls of pool b instead of amide groups. Hereby the pool system
parameters needed to be adapted to simulate MICEST. The initial magnetization and the relaxation
times were kept unaltered, while the pool ratio was changed to 1.08 % to simulate a 200 mM MI
concentration, the chemical shift diﬀerence was set to ∆ωb = 1 ppm, and the exchange rate was
simulated at 600 s−1. These choices of chemical shift diﬀerence and exchange rate were made on
the basis of information provided about hydroxyl exchange in Table 2.1 and on results presented
in [13]. In terms of sequence parameters the Z-spectrum resolution was increased to 0.219 ppm
(64 phase cycles between ∆φ = ±π), while the saturation time and B1 amplitude initially were
kept unchanged.
As opposed to Simulation 1 the EPI CEST settings were here (and from here on) simulated
more closely, i.e. with a bandwidth of saturation oﬀsets ranging from 7 to -7 ppm, and a pulse
width tp = 0.232 ms.
Next, using the same pool system simulation parameters (except for the pool ratio), the CEST
eﬀect as a function of concentration was sought optimized through the sequence parameters
tsat and B1. This was done by means of unconstrained nonlinear optimization of the slope
found from linear regression, hereby assuming a linear relation between the CEST eﬀect and the
concentration based on Eq. (2.13). The linear regression was done on the basis of the MTRasym
value at 1 ppm as a function of the concentration values 10 mM, 50 mM, 100 mM, and 200 mM
corresponding to the pool ratios 0.054 %, 0.27 %, 0.54 %, and 1.1 %, respectively.
In order to investigate the eﬀect of the saturation time and B1 amplitude sequence parameters
on the CEST eﬀect in a greater perspective, the CEST eﬀect (again estimated as the MTRasym
value at 1 ppm) was simulated in a grid of 10×10 diﬀerent combinations of saturation times and
ﬂip angles of the rectangular pulses. The saturation times were simulated from 0 s to 3 s, while
the ﬂip angles were simulated from 0◦to 15◦(corresponding to a mean B1 amplitude simulated
from 0 µT to 1.75 µT). To furthermore investigate how the sequence parameters eﬀect on the
CEST eﬀect would depend on the the exchange rate, the grid simulation was repeated for four
diﬀerent exchange rates within the range provided in Table 2.1, i.e. 500 s−1, 600 s−1, 1000 s−1,
and 1500 s−1.
==PAGE:69==
4.2. Bloch-McConnell Simulations
45
Simulation 3
The third simulation followed the ﬁrst scan session, with the purpose of repeating some of the
previous simulations, but now with updated pool simulation parameters in better correspondence
with the observed chemical environment of the pool system in vitro.
First of all, the T1 map obtained from the ﬁrst scan session revealed much shorter T1 times
than originally simulated. Therefore both T1a and T1b were updated for the new simulations.
T1a was updated to 400 ms as a rounded average of the observed T1 values. T1b was updated to
333 ms. Secondly, both the exchange rate and the chemical shift diﬀerence of the pool system were
updated to better correspond with the observed CEST eﬀects in the Z-spectra of the EPI CEST
scan. The updated exchange rate was increased to 1500 s−1 and the chemical shift diﬀerence of
the MI pool was updated to the smaller value of 0.6 ppm. Furthermore the sequence parameters of
the simulation were updated to correspond to those achieving some of the best CEST eﬀect results
in the post-processing procedure. These sequence parameters were a saturation time of 1.5 s
and a ﬂip angle of 10◦corresponding to a mean B1 amplitude of 1.165 µT. All other simulations
parameters were left unaltered from Simulation 2.
Two sets of simulations were done to investigate the the linear relationship of the MICEST
eﬀect and the MI concentration, based on two diﬀerent estimations of the MICEST eﬀect. Both
simulation sets used the concentrations 10 mM, 50 mM, 100 mM, and 200 mM corresponding to the
pool ratios 0.054 %, 0.27 %, 0.54 %, and 1.1 %, respectively. One linear regression was estimated on
the basis of the MICEST eﬀect as the MTRasym value at 0.6 ppm as a function of the concentration
values, and the other was estimated on the basis of the MICEST eﬀect as the area under the curve
(AUC) of the MTRasym curve from 0.5 to 1.5 ppm as a function of the concentration values.
Simulation 4
Simulation 4 sought to reproduce simulation results from the vCEST paper by Clark et al. [14].
The simulation of interest was “Simulation 3” in the paper, which simulated a two-pool system
with 200 mM glycogen in an aqueous environment. The goal of Simulation 4 was to both validate
the Matlab simulation implementation of the vCEST method, while also validating the usability
of Phase Cycle CEST for vCEST as Clark et al. used pulsed-CEST.
For the simulation in [14] a peak B1 amplitude of 1 µT/ppm was used for the pulsed-CEST
sequence. The used pulse shape was a single-lobed sinc function with Gaussian apodization.
The pulse duration was 75 ms with a repetition time of TR = 155 ms. The z-magnetization was
calculated after 65 repetitions, corresponding to approximately 5 s pulsed irradiation over 10 s
pulse sequence. Translating the saturation power of this pulse sequence to a mean B1 amplitude
for the Phase Cycle CEST sequence was not straightforward. Instead the mean B1 amplitude of
Phase Cycle CEST was found experimentally as the one achieving a MTRdiﬀcurve most similar
to the one presented in the paper.
The following simulation parameters from [14] were used: pool system parameters (M0a =
0.99, T1a = 2000 ms, T2a = 1000 ms, T1b = 1000 ms, T2b = 29 ms, pool ratio = 2.0 %, chemical shift
diﬀerence ∆ωb = 1.0 ppm, and exchange rate kba = 1500 s−1), sequence parameters (tsat = 3.5 s,
==PAGE:70==
46
Chapter 4. Methods
mean B1 amplitude of 0.36 µT/ppm (experimentally adjusted), and a Z-spectrum resolution of
0.219 ppm (64 phase cycles between ∆φ = ±π)).
Simulation 5
Simulation 5 implemented the vCEST method for the MICEST pool system latest simulated in
Simulation 3. Due to time constraints a more comprehensive evaluation for the choice of sequence
parameters was not possible. Instead a saturation time of 3.5 s was chosen to approach steady
state (deemed necessary for the vCEST method by [14]), while the mean B1 power as a function of
saturation oﬀset was chosen based on three diﬀerent tested values of 0.5 µT/ppm, 1.0 µT/ppm, and
2.0 µT/ppm. The mean B1 amplitude of 1.0 µT/ppm was estimated as the best choice. Otherwise
the simulation parameters, including relaxation times, were identical to those of Simulation 3.
Instead of applying a zero saturation power for the 0 ppm point, the saturation power for the
adjacent saturation oﬀsets was repeated to retrieve the central Z-spectrum point.
The simulation was repeated for four diﬀerent pool ratios corresponding to the four MI
concentrations of 200 mM, 100 mM, 50 mM, and 10 mM. The MICEST eﬀect of each simulation
was estimated as the amplitude of the ﬁtted Lorentzian of the calculated MTRdiﬀcurve as explained
in Section 4.1. A linear regression was done of the estimated MICEST eﬀects as a function of their
corresponding concentration.
Simulation 6
Simulation 6 was carried out after the analysis of the two scan sessions using vCEST, i.e. Scan
Session 2 with Phantom 1 and Scan Session 3 with Phantom 2. The purpose of the simulation was
to provide possible explanations to some of the eﬀects seen in the scan results obtained for the
two vCEST scan sessions. First the T2 relaxation time of the water pool was updated to 300 ms,
to better reﬂect the phantom environment. All other simulation parameters were kept unaltered.
Similar to Simulation 5, the simulation was repeated for four diﬀerent MI concentrations of
200 mM, 100 mM, 50 mM, and 10 mM, and a linear regression was done of the estimated MICEST
eﬀects as a function of their corresponding concentration.
The next simulations had the purpose of simulating the sensitivity of the vCEST approach
towards static ﬁeld inhomogeneity. The vCEST paper by Clark et al. [14] stated that ﬁeld
inhomogeneities could be a problem for the method as they would induce modulations to the Z-
and DWS spectra non-trivial to compensate for. However, it was also stated that within ±0.2 ppm,
the induced modulations would be acceptable in terms of the resultant MTRdiﬀcurve. This claim
was evaluated.
Simulation 7
Simulation 7 provided two extra simulations to investigate relaxation parameter dependence of
both the standard CEST and vCEST approach. First with constant T2a = 750 ms, while varying
T1a from 500 ms to 2000 ms in steps of 500 ms. And second with constant T1a = 1500 ms, while
varying T2a from 150 ms to 1050 ms in steps of 300 ms.
==PAGE:71==
4.3. Hardware Speciﬁcations
47
The other used parameters of the simulations were: pool system parameters (M0a = 0.99,
T1b = 333 ms, T2b = 30 ms, pool ratio = 1.01 %, chemical shift diﬀerence ∆ωb = 0.6 ppm, exchange
rate kba = 1500 s−1), sequence parameters (tsat = 3.5 s, mean B1 amplitude of 1.165 µT for the
standard approach and 1.165 µT/ppm for vCEST, and a Z-spectrum resolution of 0.219 ppm (64
phase cycles between ∆φ = ±π).
4.3
hardware specifications
All scan sessions were carried out at the Imetum Garching facility in collaboration with GE
Global Research on a GE Discovery MR750w 3.0 T whole-body human scanner (GE Healthcare,
Milwaukee, WI, USA). All scan sessions were conducted using a 12-channel GEM head array coil
(GE Healthcare, Waukesha, WI, USA).
4.4
phantoms
To be able to verify simulation results in vitro, two MI phantoms were composed. One phantom,
Phantom 1, with the purpose of exploring MICEST eﬀects as a function of MI concentration, and
a second phantom, Phantom 2, with the purpose of investigating pH dependence of the estimated
MICEST eﬀects.
The phantoms were composed with guidance from the “LCModel & LCMgui User’s Manual”
by Provencher [32] in how to make metabolite solutions for phantoms, combined with supervision
from co-supervisor Rolf Schulte. As a starting point, a standard solution with 200 mM MI was
mixed. Besides MI (I5125 Sigma-Aldrich), the standard solution consisted of sodium azide (NaN3)
(S2002 Sigma-Aldrich) to prolong shelf life and phosphate-buﬀer (17202 Sigma-Aldrich) with pH
7.2 (25◦) to assure pH control. The concentrations of the standard solution components are given
in Table 4.2 together with their molar masses and the calculated mass of each substance required
for a volume of 0.1 L water. The substance masses were calculated as follows (example with MI):
m(MI) = 200 mM·0.1 L·180.2 g/mol = 3603.2 mg
(4.14)

The phantoms were mixed in spherical ﬂasks as spheres have inﬁnite axes of symmetry, which

less diluted version of the standard solution.

4.4.1
Myo-Inositol Phantom 1
Phantom 1 was composed of one larger ﬂask with a 2 L volume (excluding the neck of the ﬂask),
and four smaller ﬂasks with a volume of 35 mL each. Figure 4.1a is an MR image of a coronal
slice of the phantom showing cross sections of both the larger ﬂask and the four smaller ﬂasks.
Figure 4.1b shows a picture of the phantom. The four smaller ﬂasks were ﬁlled with combinations
of the 200 mM MI standard solution and deionized water in proportions to achieve four diﬀerent
MI concentrations of 200 mM, 100 mM, 50 mM, and 10 mM, respectively. The speciﬁc proportions
are stated in Table 4.3.
To all four ﬂasks was also added 0.1 % 0.5 M Omniscan gadodiamide (a gadolinium-based
MR contrast agent). Gadolinium has paramagnetic properties that facilitate both longitudinal
and transverse relaxation, however, primarily longitudinal relaxation. Hereby the purpose of
adding gadolinium was to provide T1 shortening and as a result thereof, achieve T1 times closer to
those present in vivo. The proton fractions stated in the ﬁrst column of Table 4.3 were calculated
by means of Eq. (2.11), with na = 2 (for the two hydrogen atoms of water), nb = 6 (for the six
hydrogen atoms of MI), and with the concentration of water ([a]) equal to 55.49 M. After all MI
concentrations had been mixed, the necks of the small ﬂasks were fastened inside the neck of
the large ﬂask, after which the large ﬂask was ﬁlled with deionized water with a 0.1 % volume
of Omniscan. However, for the ﬁrst phantom scan session on November 16, 2015, the large
ﬂask was incorrectly ﬁlled with pure deionized water resulting in a long T1. This mistake was
corrected before the second scan session on December 10, 2015. The consequence of this mistake
is discussed in Chapter 6.

pulse seqences
In the following sections the pulse sequences applied during the three scan sessions will be
described including the speciﬁc settings used. For all scan sessions across the diﬀerent pulse
sequences and protocols the shimming values were held constant. Similarly, the same slice was
chosen. All scan sessions used a coronal slice of the phantoms with a superior-inferior frequency-
encoding direction due to the construction of the phantoms and the available positioning in the
applied head coil. An example of how the scan sessions were conducted in terms of phantom
positioning is given in Figure 4.3 showing a picture of the setup for Scan Session 2 with Phantom 1
on December 10, 2015.

4.5. Pulse Sequences
51
4.5.1
EPI CEST
The EPI CEST scan sequence was used for all three scan sessions included in the Results chapter.
The GE manual to the sequence is included in Appendix B and was used in the choice of most
sequence settings. The sequence details for each scan session are given below. The ﬁrst scan
session applied standard CEST method, while the second and the third scan session applied the
vCEST approach.
Scan Session 1
For the ﬁrst scan session held on November 16, 2015, the eﬀect of the controllable sequence
parameters (saturation time and ﬂip angle) on the CEST experiment was investigated. For this
purpose a 32 experimental design with two replicates was chosen – that is a factorial design with
two factors (saturation time and ﬂip angle), each at three levels, and with two replicates resulting
in a total of 18 experiments. The statistical background of this design is given in Section 4.7.
The EPI CEST sequence details for the experiments were as follows: CEST (TR = 4000 ms, TE =
25.7 ms (minimum), ﬂip angle ‘factor levels’ of 6◦, 8◦, and 10◦, saturation time ‘factor levels’ of 0.5
s, 1.0 s, and 1.5 s, 64 phase cycles acquired between ∆φ = ±π); SSEPI (ﬂip angle = 90◦, acquisition
matrix with 96 frequency-encoding steps and 96 phase-encoding steps, FOV = 16 × 16 cm2,
bandwidth = 250.5 kHz, slice thickness 5 mm). Acquisition time for each EPI CEST scan was 4:28.
Scan Session 2
The second scan session held on December 10, 2015, applied the vCEST approach. However, to
avoid spending time on pulse programming to incorporate the use of variable saturation power for
the EPI CEST pulse sequence, the sequence was instead repeated several times to achieve multiple
full CEST data sets with diﬀerent saturation powers applied through the sequence parameter ﬂip
angle. Having these multiple data sets enabled the construction of one vCEST data set.
For Scan Session 2 the variable saturation power was chosen with a mean B1 amplitude of
1 µT/ppm, identical to the variable saturation power used for Simulation 5. Hereby 15 EPI CEST
scans were done with ﬂip angles linearly varying from 28.2◦to 1.9◦in steps of approximately
1.9◦, to achieve a ﬁnal vCEST data set with 31 spectral points from 3.28 ppm to -3.28 ppm for
each Z-spectra. The resultant spectral resolution was hereby 0.219 ppm. The variable saturation
power was converted to a variable ﬂip angle as follows:
Variable ﬂip angle = 1 µT/ppm·Titer /tp ·γ· tp ·180◦/(π rad)
= 1 µT/ppm·0.560 ms/0.232 ms·42.58MHz/T·2π·0.232 ms·180◦/(π rad)
= 8.58◦/ppm
(4.15)
Similarly to Simulation 5, the saturation power for the 0 ppm point was chosen as the power
of the adjacent points, i.e. a ﬂip angle of 1.9◦was used for the three central points of the vCEST
Z-spectra.
The EPI CEST sequence details for the 15 scans were identical except for the ﬂip angle and
were as follows: CEST (TR = 8000 ms, TE = 23.40 ms (minimum), 15 diﬀerent ﬂip angles of 28.2◦,
==PAGE:76==
52
Chapter 4. Methods
26.3◦, 24.4◦, 22.5◦, 20.7◦, 18.8◦, 16.9◦, 15.0◦, 13.1◦, 11.3◦, 9.4◦, 7.5◦, 5.6◦, 3.8◦, and 1.9◦, saturation time
of 3.5 s, 64 phase cycles acquired between ∆φ = ±π); SSEPI (ﬂip angle = 90◦, acquisition matrix
with 96 frequency-encoding steps and 96 phase-encoding steps, FOV = 22×22 cm2, bandwidth
= 375 kHz, slice thickness 5 mm). Acquisition time for each EPI CEST scan was 8:56. Total
acquisition time for the 15 EPI CEST scans was hereby 2:14:00.
Scan Session 3
Scan Session 3 held on December 21, 2015, also applied the vCEST approach, but with Phantom 2
instead of Phantom 1 used for Scan Session 2. Again a variable saturation power was chosen
with a mean B1 amplitude of 1 µT/ppm, i.e. with a variable ﬂip angle of 8.58◦/ppm. A total
of 10 EPI CEST scans were done with ﬂip angles linearly varying from 18.8◦to 1.9◦in steps of
approximately 1.9◦, to achieve a ﬁnal vCEST data set with 21 spectral points from 2.19 ppm to
-2.19 ppm for each Z-spectra. The resultant spectral resolution was hereby 0.219 ppm, equal to
that of Scan Session 2.
To reduce the EPI distortions observed in the EPI CEST data from both Scan Session 1 and 2,
the number of frequency- and phase-encoding steps were reduced for this scan session, hereby
reducing the readout period for the SSEPI acquisition.
The EPI CEST sequence details for the 10 scans were identical except for the ﬂip angle:
CEST (TR = 8000 ms, TE = 19.7 ms (minimum), 10 diﬀerent ﬂip angles of 18.8◦, 16.9◦, 15.0◦,
13.1◦, 11.3◦, 9.4◦, 7.5◦, 5.6◦, 3.8◦, and 1.9◦, saturation time 3.5 s, 64 phase cycles acquired between
∆φ = ±π); SSEPI (ﬂip angle = 90◦, acquisition matrix with 40 frequency-encoding steps and 40
phase-encoding steps, FOV = 15×15 cm2, bandwidth = 334 kHz, slice thickness 5 mm). Acquisition
time for each EPI CEST scan was 8:56. Total acquisition time for the 10 EPI CEST scans was then
1:29:20.
4.5.2
MRSI Pulse Sequence
Both phantoms were scanned with an MRSI sequence with the purpose of validating phantom
contents, but also for comparison purposes with MRSI representing an existing non-invasive
metabolic imaging method.
The MRSI images were created using a chemical shift imaging (CSI) sequence. The se-
quence settings for each scan session are given below. The CSI data were reconstructed to a
three-dimensional data set having two spatial dimensions and one spectral dimension. The recon-
struction was done by means of a main Matlab function ‘recon_csi.m’ and multiple sub-functions
all provided and written by co-supervisor Rolf Schulte. These have not been included in the
appendix, but they can be shown upon request by contacting the author of this thesis or Rolf
Schulte. In order to isolate the –CH MI resonances at approximately 3.5 ppm, water suppression
was applied to the MRS spectra by means of Hankel singular value decomposition (HSVD). HSVD
decomposes the MRS time domain signal into decaying sinusoids, and hereby enables water
suppression by subtraction of the sinusoid representing the water signal. The ﬁnal MI CSI image
was calculated by taking the sum of the spectral peaks at 3.5±0.1 ppm.
==PAGE:77==

Scan Session 1
CSI sequence details: Matrix = 16×16, FOV = 16×16 cm2, ﬂip angle = 90◦, bandwidth = 5000 Hz,
voxel size 10.0×10.0×10.0 mm3. Total scan time was 10:13.
Scan Session 3
The MRSI image of Phantom 2, was not created from a scan sequence on December 21 as the
other Scan Session 3 images, but from a CSI sequence on December 18.
The CSI sequence details were: Matrix = 24×24, FOV = 15×15 cm2, ﬂip angle = 90◦, band-

4.5.5
T1 Mapping
As T1 relaxation is known to have an impact on the CEST eﬀect (see Eq. (2.13)), T1 mapping
was done for all three scan sessions. The estimated T1 maps were calculated from a series of
images reconstructed from sequences with increasing inversion times (TI). With guidance from
the T1 mapping ‘gold standard’ stated by [34] and by means of the T1 mapping scanner protocols
available, each T1 map was calculated on the basis of four spin-echo (SE) inversion recovery (IR)
sequences with diﬀerent inversion times.
An SE-IR sequence is based on multiple excitations repeated with a given repetition time
(TR), deﬁned as the time from the start of one excitation to the beginning of the next. In an SE-IR
sequence the TR is also called the inversion time, TI. During an SE-IR sequence, the longitudinal
magnetization will over time reach a steady-state magnitude dependent on the TI, i.e. dependent
on the available time for T1 recovery between pulses. The dependence is given as:
Mz( TI ) = M0
³
1−e−TI/T1
´
(4.18)
The Mz magnetization was measured using a fast spin-echo (FSE) sequence.
In the post-processing calculations of the T1 maps, the measured signals from the four images
with diﬀerent TIs were ﬁtted voxel-wise to Eq. (4.18) by means of non-linear least squares ﬁtting.
The non-linear ﬁtting was done using the built-in Levenberg-Marquardt algorithm in Matlab.
Hereby a T1 time could be estimated for each voxel.

4.5.6

T2 mapping was done for both phantoms for the two scan sessions applying the vCEST method

4.6
cest post-processing
All CEST post-processing was done in Matlab. A ‘batch program’ was written for each of the
three scan sessions for the individual and adapted post-processing of the obtained CEST data sets.
These ‘batch’ Matlab scripts can be found in Appendix C.
Scan Session 1
For Scan Session 1 the post-processing began with reading the EPI CEST Dicom data with the
appropriate Matlab functions from the Image Processing Toolbox. After reading the data, the
data were separated into the unsaturated reference image S0 and the saturated Ssat images, and
the Z-spectra were created from normalizing the Ssat images with the S0 image. Next the B0
inhomogeneity was sought compensated by the “Center Frequency Oﬀset (B0) Compensation”
method made available by Mitsuharu Miyoshi. The compensation method relies on an assumption
of Z-spectrum symmetry near 0 ppm, and works by applying a parabolic ﬁtting function centered
at 0 ppm to the Z-spectrum and iteratively shifting the spectrum until the ﬁt is deemed acceptable
(using a least-squares ﬁtting method). To allow shifts less than one sample the method involves
Fourier transformation of the data. The full compensation method is given in Source Code C.10.
As the ‘subject’ of Scan Session 1 was Phantom 1, ﬁve diﬀerent ROIs were chosen to represent
ﬁve regions with diﬀerent concentrations of MI, i.e. 200 mM, 100 mM, 50 mM, 10 mM, and 0 mM.
These were chosen manually such that they had same number of voxels (145 each), would be
centered at the approximate ‘center of mass’ for each of the ﬂasks, and such that they would to
be undisturbed by the EPI distortions across the diﬀerent images.
Before the MTRasym was calculated to estimate the MICEST eﬀect voxel-wise, the Z-spectra
were interpolated to a spectral resolution of 0.05 ppm using spline interpolation. Due to ripples
in the extracted MTRasym curves caused by the Fourier transformation for the inhomogeneity
compensation, the ﬁnal MICEST eﬀects were evaluated as the AUC of the MTRasym curves from 0.5
to 1.5 ppm. This chemical shift range was chosen based on observed broad signal characteristics
in the MTRasym curves and with 0.5 ppm as the lower limit to avoid observed artifacts close to
the water frequency.
==PAGE:81==
4.6. CEST Post-Processing
57
Scan Session 2
The post-processing of Scan Session 2 began with ﬁrst reading the EPI CEST Dicom data from
the 15 EPI CEST scans and separating the S0 and Ssat images. Second, speciﬁc Ssat images were
extracted from each of the data sets to construct a single vCEST data set consisting of 31 Ssat
images. From the data set acquired with a ﬂip angle of 28.2◦the 3.28 ppm and the -3.28 ppm
saturation oﬀset Ssat images were extracted, from the data set acquired with a ﬂip angle of
26.3◦the 3.06 ppm and -3.06 ppm Ssat images were extracted, and so on. To achieve normalized
Z-spectra all Ssat images of the assembled vCEST data set were normalized by the S0 image from
the data set acquired with a ﬂip angle of 1.9◦.
The vCEST method requires voxel-wise simulation of the DWS spectrum to calculate the
MTRdiﬀcurves and estimate MICEST eﬀects. The DWS spectra were simulated by means of
parameters provided by the four parameters maps: T1, T2, B0, and B1. However, due to EPI
distortions of the EPI CEST images required during this scan session, the parameter maps needed
to be registered to the distorted vCEST images before DWS simulation. The image registration
was done by a combination of manual and automatic registration methods based on the S0 image
and the structural 1H image from the B0 ﬁeld map protocol. Manually, several pairs of points
visually corresponding to each other for the two images were selected, and automatically, the
edges of the images were extracted through image segmentation. Combining the manually
chosen point pairs and the point pairs of the image edges, these could be given as input to the
Matlab function ‘ﬁtgeotrans’. ‘ﬁtgeotrans’ takes pairs of control points, and uses them to infer
geometric transformation. Furthermore, the registration was additionally improved using the
Matlab function ‘imregister’. The starting images, the pairs of control points, and the result of the
image registration are shown in Figure A.4 in Appendix A.
Following image registration, voxel-wise DWS spectrum simulation and subsequent MICEST
eﬀect estimation was done. As mentioned in the beginning of this chapter, the MICEST eﬀects for
vCEST were estimated as the amplitude of Lorentzian functions ﬁtted to the calculated MTRdiﬀ
curves.
Five ROIs were chosen to represent ﬁve regions with diﬀerent concentrations of MI, i.e.
200 mM, 100 mM, 50 mM, 10 mM, and 0 mM. These were chosen similarly to the ROIs for Scan
Session 1 and also with 145 voxels each.
Scan Session 3
The post-processing of the EPI CEST data from Scan Session 3 also began with reading the
obtained EPI CEST Dicom data and separating the S0 and Ssat images. Here the data originated
from 10 EPI CEST scans. The speciﬁc Ssat images were extracted from each scan to construct
the vCEST data set. The vCEST Ssat images were normalized by the S0 image from the data set
acquired with a ﬂip angle of 1.9◦to achieve the normalized Z-spectra.
As the EPI CEST data from Scan Session 3 showed minimal EPI distortion due to the reduced
acquisition matrix, image registration was not required. Instead the parameter maps were simply
resized using bilinear interpolation to have the same resolution as the normalized Ssat images.
==PAGE:82==
58
Chapter 4. Methods
Following, voxel-wise DWS spectrum simulation and subsequent MICEST eﬀect estimation was
done.
As the ‘subject’ of Scan Session 3 was Phantom 2, four ROIs were chosen to represent the
three regions with 100 mM MI and diﬀerent pH values of 7.4, 6.6, and 5.5, and one reference
region with 0 mM MI and pH 7.4. Each ROI consisted of only 9 voxels due to the reduced image
resolution.
4.7
statistical evaluation
Scan Session 1
As already mentioned a 32 experimental design with two replicates was chosen for Scan Session 1,
with the purpose of investigating the eﬀect of the controllable sequence parameters saturation time
and ﬂip angle on the CEST experiment. This experimental design gives a total of 32 = 9 treatment
combinations, resulting in eight degrees of freedom. Two replicates results in 2·32 −1 = 17 total
degrees of freedom and 32(2 −1) = 9 degrees of freedom for the error. Figure 4.4 shows the
geometry of the 32 design with the assigned low, intermediate and high levels of the two factors.
Figure 4.4: Geometry of the experimental 32 design showing the nine treatment combinations
of saturation times and ﬂip angles for Scan Session 1 on November 16, 2015.
Choosing a three-level design makes it possible to model curvature in the response function.
Letting x1 represent saturation time, x2 represent ﬂip angle, and y represent the response, MICEST
eﬀect, a second-order response surface model relating the response to the factor levels can be
given by:
y = β0 +β1x1 +β2x2 +β11x2
1 +β22x2
2 +β12x1x2 +ϵ
(4.21)
where β0,1,2,11,22,12 represent the unknown parameters and ϵ is the error.
By letting x3 = x2
1, x4 = x2
2, x5 = x1x2, β3 = β11, β4 = β22, and β5 = β21, the response surface
model becomes a linear regression model:
y = β0 +β1x1 +β2x2 +β3x3 +β4x4 +β5x5 +ϵ
(4.22)
==PAGE:83==
4.7. Statistical Evaluation
59
Hereby a surface model can easily be estimated by means of least-squares regression (seeking to
minimize the sum of the squares of the errors) of a system of linear equations [35].
The model ﬁtting was evaluated by checking the residuals for normality. The chosen method
for assessing the normality was plotting the histogram of the residuals and the corresponding
normal distribution calculated from the residual mean and the standard deviation.
==PAGE:84==
==PAGE:85==
RESULTS
5
This chapter presents all simulation and scan results. Altogether with the overall purpose of
gaining a better understanding of the CEST experiment and investigating obtainable MICEST
eﬀects and contrast at 3 T. The results are presented in chronological order, to make it easier for
the reader to follow how the conclusion of some results lead to the next. I.e. how the results of
some of the ﬁrst simulations led to choices of pulse sequence parameters for the ﬁrst scan session,
following which the ﬁrst scan results led to new simulations applying simulation parameters in
better correspondence with the observed phantom environment.
The short summary of content and purpose of each simulation and scan session from the
Methods chapter is repeated below.
Simulation 1: Reproduced aptCEST simulations from the ISMRM abstract by
Miyoshi et al. [28] with the purpose of validating simulation implementa-
tions of the Phase Cycle CEST sequence.
Simulation 2: MICEST simulations using pool system parameters from liter-
ature with the purpose of optimizing the MICEST eﬀects based on the
controllable sequence parameters, saturation time and ﬂip angle.
Scan Session 1: Scan session held on November 16, 2015 of Phantom 1 using
a 32 experimental design to test the eﬀects of the sequence parameters
saturation time and ﬂip angle.
Simulation 3: MICEST simulations with updated pool system parameters cor-
responding to the eﬀects seen in vitro.
Simulation 4: Reproduced simulation results from [14] using the vCEST ap-
proach to validate its simulation implementation using Phase Cycle CEST.
Simulation 5: MICEST simulations using the vCEST approach with the purpose
of investigating achievable eﬀects for the subsequent scan sessions.
Scan Session 2: Scan session held on December 10, 2015 of Phantom 1 using
the vCEST approach to evaluate achievable MI concentration contrast.
Scan Session 3: Scan session held on December 21, 2015 of Phantom 2 using
the vCEST approach to test pH dependence of the MICEST contrast.
Simulation 6: MICEST simulations using the vCEST approach with updated
T2 relaxation for the water pool. The simulations also tested the sensitivity
of the vCEST method towards inhomogeneity.
Simulation 7: MICEST simulations of the MTRasym and MTRdiﬀcurves with
respect to diﬀerent relaxation times of the water pool.
61
==PAGE:86==
62
Chapter 5. Results
5.1
simulation 1
As a ﬁrst step to understanding the EPI CEST sequence developed by Miyoshi Mitsuharu, GE
Healthcare Japan, the phased cycled rectangular RF preparation pulse, Phase Cycle CEST, was
simulated. These initial simulations were made with the purpose of, as closely as possible,
replicating the simulation results presented by the ISMRM abstract by Miyoshi et al. [28]. Figure 5.1
shows the simulation results from Fig. 2 in [28], where the two simulations sought replicated are
represented by the solid red line for the CW simulation and the green asterisks for the Phase
Cycle CEST simulation.
Figure 5.1: Numerical and analytical BM simulations of aptCEST for a two-pool system. The
ﬁgure originates from [28]. The x-axis is read with “-1” corresponding to the phase diﬀerence
“∆φ = π/2” for the simulations using Phase Cycle CEST, while “-1” corresponds to “∆ωb =3.5 ppm”
for the simulations using CW pulse preparation. The y-axis is the normalized water signal. Seven
BM simulations are shown with the numerical matrix inversion simulation using CW preparation
(red solid line), and the rectangular pulse simulation using Phase Cycle CEST (green) correspond
to the reproduced simulations in Figure 5.2.
Figure 5.2 shows the results of the two reproduced numerical simulations, one using the Phase
Cycle CEST preparation pulse (blue) and one using a CW preparation pulse simulated by means
of matrix inversion (red). The ﬁgure also includes the calculated MTRasym on the basis of the
Z-spectrum from the Phase Cycle CEST simulation using Eq. (2.12). The MTRasym curve peaks at
the simulated chemical shift diﬀerence of 3.5 ppm with a value of 32 %. The phased cycled pulse
and the CW pulse simulations are almost identical within ±5 ppm. Furthermore, they correspond
well with the simulations they sought to replicate shown in Figure 5.1.
From these results the Matlab implementation of the Phase Cycle CEST numerical simulation
was accepted. Additionally, the conclusion from the ISMRM abstract stating that the phase cycled
preparation pulse can be a good approximation of the CW pulse in the clinical environment, was
reaﬃrmed.

5.1. Simulation 1

CW pulse preparation and one using Phase Cycle CEST. Reproduced with simulation parameters
from [28].
==PAGE:88==
64
Chapter 5. Results
5.2
simulation 2
After verifying the Matlab implementation of the numerical simulation algorithm, the simulation
parameters were adapted to simulate a two-pool MICEST system.
In the ﬁrst simulation the controllable sequence parameters (saturation time and ﬂip angle)
were kept unaltered from the aptCEST simulation, while the spectrum resolution was increased
to achieve a better resolved spectrum at the MICEST chemical shift diﬀerence. Figure 5.3 shows
the result of the simulation, with the Z-spectrum and the calculated MTRasym. The MTRasym
shows a very broad and shifted CEST eﬀect peaking at ∼1.7 ppm with a value of 4.0 %.
Figure 5.3: Numerical BM simulations of MICEST for a two-pool system using sub-optimal
sequence parameters.
By means of unconstrained nonlinear optimization, the maximum slope of the MICEST
eﬀect as a function of MI concentration, was found. The MICEST eﬀect was estimated as the
MTRasym value at 1 ppm, equivalent to the chemical shift diﬀerence simulated. The sequence
parameters that revealed the greatest slope were a relatively short saturation time of 0.90 s,
and a mean B1 amplitude of only 0.78 µT (equivalent to a ﬂip angle of 6.7◦). New simulations
were run changing only these two simulation parameters. Figure 5.4 shows the results from
calculations of the MTRasym values at 1 ppm for the four concentrations 10 mM, 50 mM, 100 mM,
and 200 mM with the new simulation parameters. The ﬁgure also shows the result of the linear
regression: CEST eﬀect = 0.0645 %/mM×MI concentration, with a coeﬃcient of determination
given by R2 = 0.990. A coeﬃcient of determination denotes the strength of the linear association
between the ﬁtted variables, where 1 indicates the strongest association and 0 indicates no linear
association.
Figure 5.5 shows the Z-spectrum and the MTRasym curve simulated with the optimized
sequence parameters and a MI concentration of 200 mM. The MTRasym shows a more distinct
CEST eﬀect compared to the one in Figure 5.3, and the CEST eﬀect is now peaking at the simulated
chemical shift for MI at approximately 1.0 ppm with a greater peak value of 12 %. A decreased
==PAGE:89==
5.2. Simulation 2
65
spillover eﬀect can also be observed due to the low saturation power and the short saturation
time.
Figure 5.4: Linear regression of the MICEST eﬀect as a function of MI concentration using
optimized sequence parameters.

Numerical BM simulations of MICEST for a two-pool system using optimized
sequence parameters.

Figure 5.14 shows a statistical overview of the MICEST results from the nine diﬀerent EPI CEST
scans run on November 16, 2015. Each bar represents a mean of the estimated MICEST eﬀects
with a respective ROI for the two replicated EPI CEST scans. I.e. each bar is a mean of 2·145 = 290
MICEST values. The error bars represent the standard deviation across these 290 MICEST values.
The statistical signiﬁcance of the hypothesis that estimated MICEST eﬀects are greater for ROIs
with greater concentrations of MI was tested using one-sided t-tests. I.e. the null-hypothesis
was: MICEST[ROI 1] = MICEST[ROI 2], where [ROI1] > [ROI2], and the alternative hypothesis was:
MICEST[ROI 1] > MICEST[ROI 2]. Using a signiﬁcance level of 5 % the non-signiﬁcant test results are
indicated by ‘n.s.’ in Figure 5.14. All other tests had signiﬁcant results. The ﬁgure hereby shows
signiﬁcantly less MICEST eﬀect coming from the 200 mM ROI compared to the 100 mM ROI for
all combinations of ﬂip angles and saturation times, while also showing signiﬁcantly less MICEST
eﬀect from the 50 mM compared to the 10 mM ROI for the EPI CEST scan using a ﬂip angle of 8◦
and a saturation time of 1.0 s. The error bars indicate great variations of MICEST eﬀects within
each ROI. Further inspection of the ﬁgure reveals that the eﬀects seem to increase as a function
of saturation time, while the ﬂip angle seems to have a similar eﬀect although less distinct.

Chapter 5. Results
Figure 5.14: Statistic overview of the estimated MICEST eﬀects across the nine diﬀerent treat-
ment combinations of ﬂip angles (abbreviated FA in the ﬁgure) and saturation times (represented
by T). Non-signiﬁcant results of one-sided t-tests testing for greater MICEST eﬀects for greater
concentrations at a signiﬁcance level of 5 % at annotated with ‘n.s.’.
Linear regression was performed for all scan sessions, except the insigniﬁcant one from the
t-test, while ignoring both the 0 mM and the 200 mM eﬀects for the ﬁtting. The results of these
ﬁttings are in Appendix A, Figure A.2. The linear regression ﬁttings showed overall nice ﬁts with
coeﬃcients of determination, R2, greater than 0.95. However, the ﬁtting was also based on only
three points. The greatest slope of the ﬁtted curves was 0.0041 %·ppm/mM and was found for the
two scans using ﬂip angles of 10◦and saturation times of 1.0 s and 1.5 s. The sequence parameter
combination with ﬂip angle of 10◦and a saturation time of 1.5 s was determined the best, since
its linear regression had the best ﬁt of R2 = 0.999.

5.4
simulation 3
Figure 5.16 shows the ﬁrst simulation result from simulating MICEST with updated parameters
in better correspondence with the environment of Phantom 1 observed in Scan Session 1. The
simulation used a MI concentration of 200 mM. As expected the faster exchange rate (1500 s−1)
and the smaller chemical shift diﬀerence (0.6 ppm) results in a lower and broader MTRasym curve
peaking at approximately 1 ppm with a peak value of 1.4 %. The eﬀect of the shorter longitudinal
relaxation time compared to the original simulations in Simulation 2 is also evident by the less
broad Z-spectrum.
Figure 5.16: Numerical BM simulation of MICEST for a two-pool system using updated pool
system and sequence parameters. The eﬀect of the change in longitudinal relaxation times is
illustrated by the diﬀerence in two Z-spectra; one with all updated simulation parameters including
the new relaxation times T1a = 400 ms and T1b = 333 ms, and the other with all updated simulation
parameters except from the relaxation parameters kept at T1a = 1500 ms and T1b = 750 ms.
The results of the linear regression simulations are shown in Figure 5.17. Figure 5.17a
shows the linear relationship between the MICEST eﬀects and the MI concentration with the new
simulation parameters, when the MICEST eﬀect is estimated as the MTRasym peak at the simulated
chemical shift diﬀerence of 0.6 ppm, i.e. using the same estimation method as in Simulation 2.
Figure 5.17b shows the linear relationship, when the MICEST eﬀect is estimated as the AUC of
the MTRasym curve between 0.5 and 1.5 ppm, i.e. the same estimation method as used for Scan
Session 1. The linear regression for both MICEST estimation methods show nice ﬁts, and almost
identical slopes.
