5.10
simulation 7
Simulation 7 had the purpose of investigating the impact of only the water relaxation parameters
on the MICEST eﬀect. The impact was evaluated for both CEST approaches by simulating both
the MTRasym and MTRdiﬀcurves. The results of the simulations are shown in Figure 5.38.
Figure 5.38a shows how changing the T1 time for the water pool aﬀects the MTRasym and
MTRdiﬀcurves. Both curves show increasing amplitudes as a result of increasing T1, and the
MTRasym curves also demonstrate a shift in peak location towards greater chemical shift diﬀer-
ences as a result of increasing T1.
Figure 5.38b shows how changing the T2 time for the water pool aﬀects the MTRasym and
MTRdiﬀcurves. The most dramatic impact is seen on the MTRdiﬀcurve, which both decreases in
amplitude and in baseline level as the T2 time is decreased. A decrease in the amplitude of the
MTRasym curve is also seen as a function of decreasing T2 time.
(a)
(b)
Figure 5.38: MICEST simulations of relaxation parameter inﬂuence on the MTRasym and MTRdiﬀ
curves for the two CEST approaches. a) Shows the eﬀect of changing the longitudinal water
relaxation, while T2 is held constant at 750 ms, where b) shows the eﬀect of changing the transverse
relaxation, T1 is held constant at 1500 ms.
==PAGE:121==
DISCUSSION
6
Starting from a point with no prior knowledge of the CEST technique, several questions and
discussion areas have arisen throughout the project period. These include concerns regarding
everything from the underlying properties of the CEST eﬀect and results presented in CEST
research papers, to the angle of approach for the experiments of this thesis, unexpected results
and the applied pulse sequence. This chapter will discuss various aspects of the produced results
of this thesis as well as aspects related to the clinical perspective of the CEST technique.
The ﬁrst section of the discussion will assess the methods and results of the performed
simulations. Following, the scan session results will be discussed, both in terms of the two
diﬀerent CEST approaches applied and in terms of artifacts and other observed eﬀects. Last the
clinical perspective of CEST in the light of this study is discussed and future approaches are
suggested.
6.1
cest simulations
Simulation 1 provided a starting point for the following MICEST simulations by validating the
Matlab implementation of the Phase Cycle CEST numerical simulation. The MICEST simulations
were intended to both provide initial proofs of the capability of the CEST and vCEST approaches
to create MICEST eﬀects at 3 T, despite fast exchange, and to provide a decision tool for scan
session parameters.
The simulation parameters for the ﬁrst MICEST simulation, Simulation 2, were chosen based
on literature values. In relation to using the simulations as a decision tool for subsequent scan
sessions, it would have been an advantage if the phantom environment and MICEST system
had been characterized beforehand. However, this is an observation made in retrospect, as the
literature values proved to be far oﬀthe observed eﬀects, but it is also an advice for future research
in imaging optimization for CEST.
Simulation 4 provided the additional capability of the MICEST simulations to simulate vCEST,
by successfully replicating the vCEST simulations from [14], in spite of the diﬀerent choice of
saturation pulse. The vCEST approach was simulated in Simulation 5, 6 and 7 with some of
the more noticeable eﬀects being the negative MTRdiﬀcurve for short T2 times, as well as the
not directly proportional linear relation of the corresponding ﬁtted Lorentzian amplitudes with
regards to concentration. The negative MTRdiﬀcurve resulting from a DWS spectrum being more
saturated than the related Z-spectrum, is most possibly caused by coherence pathways created by
the phase cycling of the saturation pulse, why it would not an expected eﬀect using pulsed-CEST
or CW saturation. However, by using Lorentzian ﬁtting for MICEST estimation including baseline
correction, this does possibly not pose a problem for the ﬁnal evaluated CEST eﬀects, but should
be further investigated. The not directly proportional linear relation is possibly also a product
of coherence pathways created by the phase cycling, but this poses a bigger problem for the
97
==PAGE:122==
98
Chapter 6. Discussion
vCEST approach. This problem is faced when MICEST contrast needs to be interpreted, as the
intersect corresponding to no MI concentration would be a variable depending on the chemical
environment.
A discussion point relevant to both CEST approaches, with respect to both the simulations
and the scan sessions, is the choice of MICEST estimation method. With the assumed two-pool
system investigated in this thesis, the MTRasym metric was evaluated as a useful starting point
for the MICEST estimation for the ‘standard’ CEST approach. However, estimating the MICEST
eﬀect based on a peak value could only be done for the simulation results, and not for the less
consistent scan results. Instead a AUC estimation was used, but the choice of chemical shift
range of the AUC calculations could have been considered more carefully, but that as not chosen
as a focus point in this thesis. The MICEST eﬀect optimization process for Simulation 2 was
performed based on maximizing the slope of the MICEST eﬀect as a function of concentration
values. The idea behind this approach was to maximize concentration contrast for a MICEST
image, however, in retrospect, this might have been at the cost of lower MICEST eﬀects for the
lower concentration levels, i.e. for the physiological concentration levels of MI at 4-9 mM.
The Lorentzian ﬁtting of the MTRdiﬀcurves for MICEST estimation for the vCEST approach
was chosen to provide an in vivo perspective. A Lorentzian function was chosen, since it ﬁt
well with the MTRdiﬀcurve. Optimization of the Lorentzian ﬁtting method could have taken up
a lot more time of the project period by applying appropriate weight functions, choosing the
boundaries of the ﬁt more carefully, by applying derivatives to improve the ﬁt, etc. But due to time
constraints and the broader scope of this thesis, optimization of the Lorentzian ﬁtting method
was given a low priority, why there is room for several improvements in that aspect for future
investigations. Furthermore, considering the variable saturation power of the vCEST approach, it
is not certain that CEST eﬀects with greater chemical shift diﬀerences would have similar line
shapes appropriate for Lorentzian ﬁtting, why the idea of the Lorentzian ﬁtting method providing
an in vivo perspective, might not be as good as initially intended.
6.2
cest imaging approaches
Two diﬀerent approaches were tested for MICEST imaging in vitro, the ‘standard’ approach and
the vCEST approach, both by means of the provided EPI CEST scan sequence.
The ﬁrst scan session applied the ‘standard’ CEST approach with the goal of testing obtainable
MICEST eﬀects as a function of the two sequence parameters, ﬂip angle and saturation time. The
MICEST contrast image in Figure 5.13 demonstrated the capability of the method to achieve a
contrast preferably sensitive to regions with non-zero MI concentration and with a high spatial
resolution. But the capability of the method to achieve contrast sensitive to diﬀerent concentration
levels was not demonstrated as the 200 mM ROI showed less MICEST eﬀect than the 100 mM ROI.
Besides illustrating the lower MTRasym amplitude for the 200 mM ROI compared to the
100 mM ROI, the averaged Z-spectra in Figure 5.11 also showed some other eﬀects. The most
curious eﬀect was the distinct peaks of the MTRasym curves close to the water frequency, positive
for the 200 mM, 100 mM, and 50 mM ROIs and negative for the 0 mM ROI. The cause of these
==PAGE:123==
6.2. CEST Imaging Approaches
99
peaks was not thoroughly examined, but could possibly be related to the diﬀerent T1 relaxation
times for the diﬀerent ROIs or to the inhomogeneity compensation method possibly introducing
non-physiological asymmetry close to the water frequency. The suspicion of the diﬀerent T1
relaxation times as a cause, originates from the big diﬀerence in the eﬀect from the 0 mM ROI to
the other ROIs, and the (assumed) much longer T1 time for the 0 mM ROI compared to the other
ROIs due to the missing gadolinium for Scan Session 1.
As mentioned earlier, the inhomogeneity compensation method relies on an assumption of Z-
spectrum symmetry at samples near 0 ppm. For aptCEST this might have been a valid assumption,
but this is possibly not the case for MICEST with a smaller chemical shift diﬀerence. Other
methods for inhomogeneity compensation should possibly be tested, e.g. the water saturation
shift referencing (WASSR) method [36], which is an emphasized method for the purpose in the
following review papers [3–7].
The ﬁrst scan session was statistically evaluated by means of one-sided t-tests with a level
of signiﬁcance of 5 % for all diﬀerent combinations of ﬂip angles and saturation times tested.
This evaluation method was chosen based on the hypothesis that CEST eﬀect increases with
increasing proton fractions, i.e. with increasing concentration, provided by the PTR equation,
Eq. (2.13). However by using a one-sided t-test, the test outcome can be non-signiﬁcant even
though the tested values are signiﬁcantly diﬀerent. In retrospect, with the observation of less
MICEST contrast from the 200 mM ROI for all scans, using other statistical methods (e.g. a
two-sided t-test) could have helped in explaining this outlier.
Scan Session 1 was also subjected to a 32 experimental design with the purpose of testing
the MICEST eﬀect created in response to three diﬀerent levels of the two factors ﬂip angles and
saturation times. The 32 experimental design was chosen to enable the modeling of a curvature
in the response surface. However, one could argue the a two-level design with center points
should have been used instead, as it is much less expensive (fewer scans) while still being a
good (and simple) way to establish the presence or absence of curvature [35]. The modeled
response surfaces were shown in Figure 5.15 and showed great variation across the diﬀerent
ROIs. Furthermore, none of them resembled the contour maps of Simulation 2. However, these
were also created based on ‘wrong’ simulation parameters. Some diﬀerence across the diﬀerent
concentration ROIs were expected, as a higher concentration of MI might beneﬁt from greater
saturation power without loosing too much spectral selectivity, whereas it might be diﬀerent for
lower concentrations. However, the residual analysis did not show the anticipated normality of
the residuals (Figure A.3 in Appendix A), why the reliability of the models should be questioned,
and why these response surfaces should not be used when choosing saturation time and ﬂip angle
in future experiments using Phase Cycle CEST. One possible suggestion for modeling a more
reliable response surface would be to choose a greater range for the tested levels of saturation
time and ﬂip angle. This is suggested since the evaluated best MICEST image originated from the
EPI CEST scan using the high levels of both factors.
Another comment should be made on the evaluated best MICEST image, as the evaluation
method was based on linear regression of only three out of the ﬁve MICEST ROI values. The two
excluded MICEST ROIs (the 0 mM and the 200 mM) were considered outliers, but only ﬁtting a
==PAGE:124==
100
Chapter 6. Discussion
linear regression on three points results in a very low statistical strength. This is why the results
of the evaluation were not included in the Results chapter but only in the Appendix, and why no
further importance should be attached to them.
Scan Session 2 and 3 used the vCEST approach to image Phantom 1 and 2, respectively. As
mentioned in the Methods chapter, the choice of variable saturation power was made based on
limited investigation. Hereby the chosen variable saturation power cannot necessarily be assumed
optimal and neither can the resultant MICEST contrast from the vCEST images. However, the
observed artifacts in the averaged Z-spectra for both scan sessions (see Figure 5.24 and Figure 5.32)
cannot be explained by a sub-optimal choice of sequence settings alone. The averaged Z-spectra
showed several random ﬂuctuations, and overall unexpected line shapes. This provided a fragile
foundation for the Lorentzian ﬁtting algorithm, which did not perform well. Hereby the created
MICEST contrast images for the vCEST method show a visually low speciﬁcity. This is especially
seen for Figure 5.26 of Phantom 1.
The MICEST contrast image of Phantom 2, Figure 5.33, shows another curious eﬀect in the
shape of a symmetrically increasing MICEST eﬀect towards the edge of the phantom. This eﬀect
was initially suspected to be related to the phenomena of standing dielectric waves within bigger
water-ﬁlled phantoms [37]. However, this phenomena would in that case also have been indicated
in the B1 map in Figure 5.30b and furthermore have been accounted for in the DWS simulation
and the resultant MTRdiﬀcurve.
In addition to a change of phantom, Scan Session 3 also changed the acquisition matrix for the
SE-SSEPI acquisition to reduce readout time and hereby reduce EPI distortions. The minimized
distortions hereby happened at the cost of a lower spatial resolution. But compared to the CSI
scan for Scan Session 3, the resolution was still improved (3.75 mm compared to 6.25 mm for the
CSI). It is diﬃcult to estimate how much impact the EPI distortions of the EPI CEST scans for
Scan Session 1 and 2 had on the ﬁnal MICEST images. But for Figure 5.25, the MICEST contrast
image of Phantom 1 for Scan Session 2, a right-left pattern (in the phase-encoding direction)
is observed that very likely could be related to EPI distortions. Nevertheless, to exclude EPI
distortion as a possible nuisance factor, a compromise of lower spatial resolution is suggested for
future investigations.
When comparing the success of the created MI concentration contrast images for Phantom 1
using CSI imaging versus the CEST and vCEST methods, the CSI image does provide better visual
contrast of concentration as the 200 mM ROI has the greatest signal. But the CSI method applied
in this study is not sensitive enough to detect MI concentration at physiological levels (4-9 mM).
The main downside to the CEST approaches in terms of concentration contrast is their inability
to detect greater eﬀects from the 200 mM ROI compared to the 100 mM ROI. However, as this
proved a very consistent problem throughout the scan sessions and across scan sessions, it must
be questioned whether a mistake happened during the assembling of the phantom, resulting in a
changed chemical environment for the content of this ﬂask, e.g. a change of pH value relative
to the other ﬂasks. Assuming incorrect content of the 200 mM ﬂask and hereby ignoring the
200 mM ROI, the created MICEST contrast image by means of the ‘standard’ CEST approach does
provide a better alternative to the CSI image by providing higher spatial resolution, high visual
==PAGE:125==
6.3. Clinical Perspective
101
speciﬁcity, and by being sensitive enough to detect signal from the 10 mM ROI. The MICEST
contrast image created by means of the vCEST approach, on the other hand, show lower visual
speciﬁcity as MICEST eﬀects have been detected for many 0 mM voxels and lower sensitivity by
measuring minimal MICEST eﬀects from the 10 mM ROI.
With respect to the used pulse sequence, EPI CEST, it should be noted that information about
its exact scanner implementation was limited and hereby introduced unknown factors. E.g. how
the unsaturated S0 image of the sequence was created was unknown, for which reason it is
uncertain to tell whether it was fully relaxed or not. A fully relaxed S0 image for normalization is
stated as an important element in the CEST experiment in [18], since a not fully relaxed S0 image
would create artiﬁcially increased CEST signals diﬃcult to compare.
6.3
clinical perspective
The clinical perspective of CEST as a molecular imaging method is part of the overall aim of
this thesis, and has been illustrated by implementing MICEST imaging at 3 T. Despite being in
the fast exchange regime, MICEST contrast was created with greatest success for the ‘standard’
CEST approach. However, as emphasized at several points in the Background chapter, MICEST
eﬀect estimations based on methods not compensating for relaxation eﬀects should be disputed.
During this study, the importance of obtaining relaxation independent measured where shown,
and therefore this should be a major topic of future investigations in order to approach clinical
translation.
The ﬁnal simulation of the Results chapter, Simulation 7, illustrated the MICEST eﬀect
dependence on both T1 and T2 relaxation times. The MICEST eﬀects were illustrated based on
the MTRasym and MTRdiﬀmetrics from the two diﬀerent applied CEST approaches. In order to be
able to attribute increased MICEST eﬀects to increased concentrations, the measured eﬀects have
to be relaxation independent. This is of great clinical interest.
The pH dependence of the MICEST eﬀect was also investigated. This was done using the
vCEST approach. At a pH value of 5.5 the MI exchange rate was clearly altered, whereas the change
seemed less drastic when comparing MICEST eﬀects at pH values of 7.4 and 6.6, respectively. In a
clinical perspective this is positive, since pH levels of the brain rarely changes more than ±0.4 for
various pathological states, why physiological and pathological changes in pH levels would have
minimal eﬀect on the MICEST eﬀects.
The phantoms constructed for the in vitro experiments of this study were intended to provide
a stepping stone for later human trials. Hereby they were supposed to imitate a simpliﬁed
in vivo environment, but with certain advantages of greater concentrations, in order to allow
optimization of the MICEST method within a controlled environment and for a ‘proof of concept’
demonstration. However, the short T1 times of the phantom did not replicate those seen in vivo
very well, and challenged the MICEST imaging. This of course would have been less of a problem,
had the MICEST eﬀects been estimated independent of relaxation.
To sum up, future research of MICEST imaging should ﬁrst of all focus on developing a
relaxation independent measure of MICEST eﬀects, possibly using the MATLAB simulation code
==PAGE:126==
102
Chapter 6. Discussion
of this thesis. Second of all, the ‘standard’ CEST approach should be pursued as it is much
simpler to optimize than the vCEST approach and as the vCEST approach introduce too many
inﬂuential parameters aﬀecting the ﬁnal measured MICEST eﬀect. Third, a new phantom should
be build to assure a uniform chemical environment across the diﬀerent concentrations of MI. And
furthermore, the sequence parameters of the EPI CEST sequence should be optimized through a
greater experimental design setup.
Finally, after demonstrating successful MICEST imaging in vitro, a next step would possibly
be to adapt the developed method to a multi-pool system to prove its capability of isolating
MICEST eﬀects despite the presence of other saturation transfer pathways.
==PAGE:127==
CONCLUSION
7
The overall aim of this thesis was to investigate CEST from a clinical perspective. With no prior
knowledge of CEST, this was a broad aim that demanded a thorough theoretical understanding
of the technique before experimental investigations were initiated. The clinical perspective was
implemented by focusing on clinical ﬁeld strength throughout the study and by having MI, being
a possible biomarker for several diseases, as the molecular target.
The main principles of the CEST techniques were established with help from several review
papers [3–7], and the underlying mechanisms of CEST were described. From this starting point,
proposing a MICEST experiment at 3 T seemed pointless, as MICEST is in the fast exchange
regime, theoretically outside the limits of the CEST experiment. However by means of BM
simulations of the MICEST experiment, hope was created by distinct CEST eﬀects visible from
the simulation results. The Phase Cycle CEST pulse was applied for all CEST experiments, as
the pulse was already implemented through the EPI CEST sequence on the available GE 3 T
whole-body MRI scanner. Phase Cycle CEST furthermore proved to provide a good approximation
of the CW pulse within RF ampliﬁer limitations.
The MICEST experiment was investigated through BM simulations in terms of both in-
herent properties such as chemical exchange rate, and in terms of pulse sequence parameters.
The MICEST experiment was also investigated by means of the two diﬀerent proposed CEST
approaches, the ‘standard’ CEST approach and the vCEST approach.
The in vitro experiments of MICEST resulted in a conclusion of the ‘standard’ CEST approach
being the best choice for future investigations. Based on the assumption that a mistake was made
when constructing Phantom 1 with diﬀerent concentrations of MI, the 200 mM ROI showing
consistently lower MICEST eﬀects throughout all experiments could be ignored. On that basis
the MICEST contrast image of Phantom 1 created from the ‘standard’ CEST approach proved
substantially improved MI concentration contrast compared to the CSI image of the phantom.
These improvements consisted of increased spatial resolution and a higher sensitivity towards
lower concentration levels.
The vCEST approach on the other hand did not provide as good results as expected from the
results presented in the paper by Clark et al. [14] originally introducing the approach, since it
proved too susceptible to various eﬀects, e.g. caused by sub-optimal estimations of the parameter
maps for DWS simulation.
However the MICEST contrast image created from the ‘standard’ CEST approach, should
not be used as a proof of MICEST being inferior to CSI imaging of MI, as the MICEST eﬀects
were reﬂecting phantom relaxation times as well together with MI concentrations. Investigations
made late in the study showed a great dependence of the MICEST eﬀects on relaxation times, for
which reason a main focus for future investigations should be the development of a relaxation-
independent MICEST eﬀect estimator.
The results of this thesis ended raising more questions than they were able to answer. However,
103
==PAGE:128==
104
Chapter 7. Conclusion
with the broad aim provided and with no prior knowledge of the investigated technique, it is
evaluated a successful outcome that this thesis provides a foundation for further investigations
with emphasis on potential pitfalls and suggested approaches.
All objectives of the thesis stated in the Introduction have been fulﬁlled as:
1. A fundamental understanding of the CEST eﬀect in terms of how it is generated, the
underlying mechanisms, and confounding factors, has been established.
2. Current published research papers within the ﬁeld relevant to the aim of this study were
critically assessed.
3. The MICEST experiment has been numerically simulated using the BM equations as a
starting point, to understand the impact of diﬀerent parameters on the MICEST eﬀects and
to optimize sequence parameters.
4. Scan sessions were carried out using Phase Cycle CEST to evaluate achievable MICEST
eﬀects in vitro and to investigate MICEST eﬀect dependence on MI concentration and pH.

Purpose 
This document is intended to provide the user with basic protocols, instructions, and tips 
related to setting up a scan or use of each of the pulse sequences and/or post-processing 
tools (if applicable) included in this Applications Package. 
Description 
The EPI CEST sequence is intended to provide a Z-spectrum of the CEST imaging with 2D Single 
Shot Multi phase EPI acquisition with pulsed CEST preparation pulses. It is intended to be used 
for evaluating the various anatomies. 
Intended Use Limitation 
The sequences described in this User Manual are intended to be used for evaluation only.  
These sequences are not intended for the purposes of performing diagnoses. 
MR Safety Instructions 
Please refer to the MR Safety instructions that were provided with your System. 
System operation in Research Mode 
The access to the pulse sequence(s) described in the present manual requires activation of 
Research Mode and the use of such features is strictly reserved to investigations having a 
research agreement with GE. This may require prior Regulatory and/or Ethics Committee 
authorizations in the country where the study is being conducted.  
Please contact your GE Field Engineer if you have questions on how to activate Research 
Mode and/or how to return to Clinical Mode.  
Install and uninstall the package 
Installation may be completed by your local GE Field Engineer.  If so, please disregard the 
instructions below. 
The software package is contained in an rpm (Redhat Package Manager) patch.  The patch 
name will be provided with the software package. 
==PAGE:145==
ATSM User Manual Template DOC1233076_r01 
 
GE Healthcare      
 
 EPI CEST  User Manual 
 
 
Caution - Not for use in performing diagnoses 
2015 General Electric Company. GE Internal Information. 
 
 
Page 4 of 9 
 
 
To install the research software package, place the rpm patch on the scanner at 
/usr/g/insite/data, log in as root, and then type: 
pkgmgr –i <patch name> 
For example, for a DV24 installation,  
pkgmgr –i patch_DV24.0_R01_research_EPI_CEST_Dev-14fw27-2.x86_64.rpm 
pkgmgr –i patch_DV24.0_R01_research_EPI_CEST_POST_Dev-14fw42-1. x86_64.rpm 
pkgmgr –i patch_DV24.0_R01_research_MCR_V716-14fw42-1.x86_64.rpm 
 
To uninstall the research software, log in as root and type (for a DV24 example):  
pkgmgr –r patch_DV24.0_R01_research_EPI_CEST_Dev-14fw27-2 
pkgmgr –r patch_DV24.0_R01_research_EPI_CEST_POST_Dev-14fw42-1 
pkgmgr –r patch_DV24.0_R01_research_MCR_V716-14fw42-1 
The expiration key must be placed in /usr/g/bin for the software to become operational.  
Please note that this software will stop functioning upon the expiration of your research work 
statement with GE Healthcare. 
If Matlab Compiler Runtime (MCR) is installed in the system, please check the version of MCR. 
If the version of MCR is v7.16, the RPM file named “patch_DV24.0_R01_research_MCR_V716-
14fw42-1” must not be installed. The MCR directory “MCRROOT” in the shell script named 
“/usr/g/research/cest/ EpiCest” should be corrected. If MCR version is not v7.16 or MCR is not 
installed, this RPM file needs to be installed. 
 
Protocols 
The recommended protocol  is included with the installation under Site/Other/EPI_CEST.  
Start with the protocols provided.  If you encounter image quality issues, try using the provided 
protocols– this will help us identify the problem. The below protocol(s) have been qualified by 
GEHC as part of this ATSM.

Description of User Control Variables (CVs) 
The following CV settings are specific to this pulse sequence and the details are listed below. 
For other CVs, please use the value listed in the protocol table above or the default values 
provided in the system. 
CV18: CEST type 0: Pulse, 1: Phase Cycle: 
Two types of pulsed RF CEST preparation pulse are supported. 
“Pulse (CV18=0)” means a conventional pulsed method. Pulse width is 40msec and iteration 
time 67 ms. RF Shape is sinusoidal (same as Blackman filter). Flip angle is several hundred 
degrees, which is controlled with CV19. RF phase is like the phase spoiling of SPGR. 
“Phase Cycle (CV18=1)” means phase cycled pulsed method. Pulse width is around 0.232 ms 
and iteration time is 0.560 ms. RF shape is rectangular. Flip angle is several degrees, which is 
controlled with CV19. RF phase is phase-cycled. Please refer to ISMRM2014 #3299 for the 
detail. 
==PAGE:147==
ATSM User Manual Template DOC1233076_r01 
 
GE Healthcare      
 
 EPI CEST  User Manual 
 
 
Caution - Not for use in performing diagnoses 
2015 General Electric Company. GE Internal Information. 
 
 
Page 6 of 9 
 
 
 
CV19: CEST RF flip angle [degree] 
This is the flip angle of each CEST RF pulses. Default value is 1 degree. 
In the case with pulsed RF (CV18=0), recommended flip angle is 1440 degree/RF pulse. 
In the case with Phase Cycle RF (CV18=1), recommended flip angle is 12 degree/RF pulse. 
The control variable of Peak B1 of the RF pulse is B1_rfcest. 
 
CV20: CEST RF total period [sec] 
This is the total duration of CEST preparation pulse irradiation. Default value is 1.0ms. 
Recommended value is 3500ms in case with TR=4000ms.  
 
 
 
 
Acquisition and data 
[Multi-Phase] 
The number of phases in Multi-Phase screen is 3 + CEST images (Mz_cest). The second phase is 
used as an M0 image. The first one quarter point of Mz_cest corresponds to +3.5ppm (Amide 
Proton Transfer: APT). The half point of Mz_cest corresponds to 0 ppm. 
==PAGE:148==
ATSM User Manual Template DOC1233076_r01 
 
GE Healthcare      
 
 EPI CEST  User Manual 
 
 
Caution - Not for use in performing diagnoses 
2015 General Electric Company. GE Internal Information. 
 
 
Page 7 of 9 
 
 
For example, 35 phases mean 3 phases for M0 measurement and 32 phases for Mz_cest. 11-
th phase is APT. 19-th phase is 0 ppm. 
Error of RF frequency offset caused by B0 inhomogeneity is not compensated in Mz_cest. Post 
processing is required for B0 compensation and Z-spectrum calculation. See the session 
“Processing” for the detail. 
 
[The number of slices] 
The number of slices must be one. Multi-slice is not supported. 
 
[Other Tips] 
Scanning time of CEST imaging is normally long. EPI data acquisition is used for a fast 
scanning. However, image distortion occurs in phase direction. Signal difference is small in 
CEST imaging and high SNR is required. Following tips are recommended to avoid these 
drawbacks. 
Frequency matrix size should be low. 96 or fewer is recommended. 
SNR becomes low with ASSET. ASSET is not recommended. 
FOV should not be too wide. 22cm or narrower FOV without ASSET is recommended. Phase 
wrap-around is acceptable because ASSET is not used. 
Axial plane and RL frequency is recommended to avoid EPI distortion. 
 
 
Processing 
Acquired CEST images (Mz_cest) have signal intensity 
distribution along the RF frequency offset, which 
corresponds to multi-phase axis. However, because of B0 
inhomogeneity, RF frequency offset has error (right top 
figure) and post processing is required to compensate it. 
Signal intensity distribution near 0-ppm is assumed to be 
symmetric and parabolic function fitting is done for B0 
compensation (right middle image). 
After B0 compensation, Z-spectrum and MTR_asym 
(asymmetric Magnetization Transfer Ratio) is calculated 
(right bottom image). 
This post-processing is implemented in the scanner host 
as a stand-alone procedure, independent from the 
acquisition. To execute it, select the acquired EPI CEST 
series in the patient database, open a command console 
and enter the command "EpiCest".  
==PAGE:149==
ATSM User Manual Template DOC1233076_r01 
 
GE Healthcare      
 
 EPI CEST  User Manual 
 
 
Caution - Not for use in performing diagnoses 
2015 General Electric Company. GE Internal Information. 
 
 
Page 8 of 9 
 
 
After some computation, the CEST map image will be displayed and the user will be prompted 
to select a pixel of interest by clicking on the image. Once a pixel has been selected, the 
corresponding spectrum plot will be displayed. A prompt in the command window will offer 
the user three options: store the current plot, discard it or quit the program. When the last 
option is selected, the CEST map and all stored plots will be sent to the patient database. 
These images should only be used as a reference, as their Dicom header is only partially 
populated. Functool can be used for the visualization of the Z-spectrum or MTR_asym of each 
pixel. 
The series number of Z-spectrum and MTR_asym is “100 + alpha”, where alpha is the series 
number before post processing. The image number of Z-spectrum is between 1 and 32 and 
that of MTR_asym is between 33 and 47. 
The Trigger Delay time (TDEL) of the multi-phase corresponds to the RF frequency offset 
direction. TDEL of each image means “(the Offset frequency in ppm)  * 1000 + (image number  
- 1) * 100000”, e.g. TDEL 106563 and image number 2 means offset frequency 6.563 ppm.  
Image number 9 or 17 of Z-spectrum corresponds to +3.5ppm (APT) or 0 ppm, respectively. 
The series number of Signal Asymmetry image is “200 + alpha“. Signal Asymmetry image is 
calculated by multiplying M0 image by MTR_asym. 
The series number of Z-spectrum for each pixel is “300 + alpha”. Corrected input (black line), 
Flipped signal (black dotted line) and Asymmetry (blue line) are preserved. Transverse axis is 
offset frequency in ppm. Longitudinal axis is MT Ratio in %. 
 
 
Suggested Readings 
1 - Miyoshi M. et al., CEST imaging with phase cycled rectangular RF preparation pulse : 
analytical solution, simulation and phantom study, Proceedings of ISMRM2014 #3299

and/or Applications Package.

for the CSI data processing and analysis.

Source Code C.2: BM2PoolSim.m
1
function [Z,offset_ppm] = BM2PoolSim(Par,plt)
2
%BM2POOLSIM Bloch−McConnell numerical simulation of a two−pool CEST system
3
%
4
% Bloch−McConnell numerical simulation of the Z−spectrum of a two pool
5
% system (where pool a is the water pool and pool b is the solute/CEST
6
% pool) using a phase cycled rectangular RF preparation pulse.
7
%
8
% Steady state condition without RF irradiation: kb*M0_b = ka*M0_a
9
%
10
% The simulation is in the rotating frame of reference with respect to the
11
% frequency and phase of the oscillating B1 field. So when the
12
% 'on−resonant' protons in pool a precess about the z−axis, it is due to a
13
% change in RF phase.
14
%
15
% input:
Par
= struct of system and sequence parameters with the
16
%
fields:
17
%
N (Z−spectrum resolution),
18
%
P (simulation points for one iteration time),
19
%
T (total saturation time),
20
%
kba (chemical exchange rate in [1/s] from pool
21
%
b to a)
22
%
fb (pool ratio / proton fraction)
==PAGE:153==
129
23
%
Ra (R1 and R2 relaxtion rates for pool a in
24
%
[1/s])
25
%
Rb (R1 and R2 relaxtion rates for pool b in
26
%
[1/s])
27
%
B1 (average B1 amplitude for one iteration time
28
%
in [T])
29
%
tp (pulse length in [s])
30
%
dwb (relative chemical shift of the protons in
31
%
pool b in [ppm])
32
%
B0 (field strength in [T])
33
%
M0a (initial magnetization in units of thermal
34
%
M0)
35
%
bw (band width in [ppm])
36
%
plt
= true for display of simulation plots, else false
37
%
38
% output:
Z
= simulated Z−spectrum
39
%
offset_ppm
= saturation offset axis in ppm
40
%
41
% Example of parameters for simulating aptCEST (amide proton transfer)
42
% From reference: Miyoshi et al. "ABSTRACT: CEST imaging with phase cycled
43
% rectangular RF preparation pulse: analytical solution, simulation and
44
% phantom study." In Proc. Intl. Soc. Mag. Reson. Med., volume 22, page
45
% 3299, 2014.
46
% Par.N = 32; Par.P = 16; Par.T = 3.5; Par.kba = 148.5; Par.fb = 1.5/148.5;
47
% Par.Ra = [1/1500e−3, 1/60e−3]; Par.Rb = [1/750e−3, 1/30e−3];
48
% Par.B1 = 1.75e−6; Par.tp = 0.28e−3; Par.dwb = 3.5; Par.B0 = 3;
49
% Par.M0a = 0.99; Par.bw = 28;
50
%
51
% Author: Rie Beck Hansen
52
% GE Global Research Europe in Munich, Germany and Technical University of
53
% Denmark, January 2016
54
55
N = Par.N; % resolution of Z−spectrum, number of points
56
P = Par.P; % points for each T_iter
57
T = Par.T; % saturation time [s]
58
kba = Par.kba; % chemical exchange rate in [1/s] from pool b to a
59
fb = Par.fb; % pool ratio
60
R_1a = Par.Ra(1); % 1/T1 relaxtion rate for pool a in [1/s]
61
R_2a = Par.Ra(2); % 1/T2 relaxtion rate for pool a in [1/s]
62
R_1b = Par.Rb(1); % 1/T1 relaxtion rate for pool b in [1/s]
63
R_2b = Par.Rb(2); % 1/T2 relaxtion rate for pool b in [1/s]
64
B1 = Par.B1; % mean B1 amplitude in [T]
65
tp = Par.tp; % pulse length [s]
66
dwb = Par.dwb; % relative chemical shift of the protons in pool b in [ppm]
67
B0 = Par.B0; % field strength in [T]
68
M0_a = Par.M0a; % free water magnetization in [T]
69
bw = Par.bw;
70
71
gamma = 26.753e7; % gyromagnetic ratio for 1H in [rad/T/s]
72
73
% iteration time between rect pulses (from pulse start to next pulse start)
74
% in [s]
75
T_iter = 0.56e−3;
76
q = 1/(B0*gamma/(2*pi*10^6)*T_iter*dwb);
77
% phase difference between water and the protons of pool b during one
78
% iteration time
79
phase_cest = 2*pi/q;
80
% phase shift acqquired per ppm for the chosen T_iter
81
phase_ppm = phase_cest/dwb;
82
83
dT = T_iter/P; % delta−time (each T_iter is divided into P points)
84
% pulse length in points depending on the total number of simulation points
85
[~,tp_idx] = min(abs(dT*(1:P)−tp));
86
87
PS = ceil(T/T_iter); % number of rect pulses
88
89
kab = fb*kba; % chemical exchange rate in [1/s] from pool a to b (k_ws)
90
91
e_ka = exp(−dT*kab);
92
e_kb = exp(−dT*kba);
93
94
e_1a = exp(−dT*R_1a);
95
e_2a = exp(−dT*R_2a);
96
e_1b = exp(−dT*R_1b);
97
e_2b = exp(−dT*R_2b);
98
99
% on−resonance larmor frequency of excitations pulses in [rad/s]
100
omega1 = gamma * B1 * P/tp_idx;
101
alpha = dT*omega1; % flip angle in [rad]
102
103
M0_b = M0_a*fb; % pool b proton magnetization in [T]
104
105
phase_bw = round((phase_ppm*bw)/pi);
106
step = phase_bw*pi/N; % resolution step size
107
% RF phase difference for pool a
108
dphase_a = phase_bw/2*pi−step:−step:−phase_bw/2*pi;
==PAGE:154==
130
Appendix C. Matlab Source Code
109
offset_ppm = (dphase_a/phase_ppm); % ppm difference for pool a
110
111
% chemical exchange matrix
112
CE = [e_ka 0 0 1−e_kb 0 0;
113
0 e_ka 0 0 1−e_kb 0;
114
0 0 e_ka 0 0 1−e_kb;
115
1−e_ka 0 0 e_kb 0 0;
116
0 1−e_ka 0 0 e_kb 0;
117
0 0 1−e_ka 0 0 e_kb];
118
119
% relaxation matrix
120
RE = [e_2a 0 0 0 0 0;
121
0 e_2a 0 0 0 0;
122
0 0 e_1a 0 0 0;
123
0 0 0 e_2b 0 0;
124
0 0 0 0 e_2b 0;
125
0 0 0 0 0 e_1b];
126
127
% RF excitation matrix
128
RF = [1 0 0 0 0 0;
129
0 cos(alpha) −sin(alpha) 0 0 0;
130
0 sin(alpha) cos(alpha) 0 0 0;
131
0 0 0 1 0 0;
132
0 0 0 0 cos(alpha) −sin(alpha);
133
0 0 0 0 sin(alpha) cos(alpha)];
134
135
% free precession of the off−resonance b pool protons (for one dT)
136
FP = [1 0 0 0 0 0;
137
0 1 0 0 0 0;
138
0 0 1 0 0 0;
139
0 0 0 cos(−(phase_cest)/P) −sin(−(phase_cest)/P) 0;
140
0 0 0 sin(−(phase_cest)/P) cos(−(phase_cest)/P) 0;
141
0 0 0 0 0 1];
142
143
% relaxation
144
B = [0 0 (1−e_1a)*M0_a 0 0 (1−e_1b)*M0_b]';
145
146
% magnetization preallocation
147
% M is a 6D vector with the first 3 dimensions corresponding to Mx, My and
148
% Mz of pool a. The last 3 dimensions correspond to Mx, My and Mz of pool b
149
M = zeros(6,P*PS+1,N);
150
151
% starting magnetization
152
M(:,1,:) = repmat([0;0;M0_a;0;0;M0_b],[1,1,N]);
153
154
% initialize status display
155
x = 0; tmp = zeros(1,N*PS);
156
fprintf('Simulating: ')
157
158
for m = 1:N
159
% RF phase shift for current phase cycling image
160
RP = [cos(dphase_a(m)) −sin(dphase_a(m)) 0 0 0 0;
161
sin(dphase_a(m)) cos(dphase_a(m)) 0 0 0 0;
162
0 0 1 0 0 0;
163
0 0 0 cos(dphase_a(m)) −sin(dphase_a(m)) 0;
164
0 0 0 sin(dphase_a(m)) cos(dphase_a(m)) 0;
165
0 0 0 0 0 1];
166
167
for n = 1:PS
168
169
% rect pulse (divided into tp_idx parts depending on the pulse
170
% length)
171
for k = 1+P*(n−1):tp_idx+P*(n−1)
172
M(:,k+1,m) = CE*RE*RF*FP*M(:,k,m) + B;
173
end
174
175
% between pulses (no excitation)
176
for k = tp_idx+1+P*(n−1):P+P*(n−1)
177
M(:,k+1,m) = CE*RE*FP*M(:,k,m) + B;
178
end
179
180
% phase shift of RF pulse and therefore of the rotating frame of
181
% reference
182
M(:,P+P*(n−1)+1,m) = RP*M(:,P+P*(n−1)+1,m);
183
184
% display progress in percent
185
tmp(n+(m−1)*PS) = round((n+(m−1)*PS)/(N*PS)*100);
186
if (n+(m−1)*PS) > 1
187
if tmp(n+(m−1)*PS) > tmp((n+(m−1)*PS)−1)
188
fprintf(repmat('\b',1,x))
189
fprintf('%d %%\n', tmp(n+(m−1)*PS));
190
s = sprintf('%d %%\n', tmp(n+(m−1)*PS));
191
x = numel(s);
192
end
193
end
194
end

131
195
end
196
197
disp('')
198
199
Mza = squeeze(M(3,:,:));
200
201
% create Z−spectrum by normalizing with the signal without saturation
202
Z = Mza(end,:)/M0_a;
203
204
if plt == 1
205
figure;
206
plot(dphase_a,Z,'−o')
207
axis([−2*pi 2*pi 0 1])

210
set(gca,'XTickLabel',fliplr({'2\pi' '3\pi/2' '\pi' '\pi/2' '0' '−\pi/2'
...
211
'−\pi' '−3\pi/2' '−2\pi'}))
212
213
xlabel '\Delta\phi (phase difference)'
214
ylabel 'Z(\Delta\phi)'

title 'Z−spectrum using a phase cycled preparation pulse'
216

plot(offset_ppm,Z,'−o')

% Par.B1 = 1.75e−6; Par.dwb = 3.5; Par.B0 = 3; Par.M0a = 0.99;

42

Source Code C.5: opt2pool.m
1
function [CEST_effect] = opt2pool(Par,x,a)
2
%OPT2POOL optimizes pulse parameters for a two pool system
3
% estimates the CEST effect for a two pool system using MTR asymmetry
4
% of a numerical BM simulation of the phase cycled preparation pulse
5
6
% setup system and sequence parameters
7
Par.fb = a(1); % proton fraction
8
Par.kba = a(2); % exchange rate [1/s]
9
Par.dwb = a(3); % chemical shift of the CEST pool in [ppm]
10
11
T_iter = 0.56e−3; % iteration time for the phase cycled preparation pulse
12
gamma = 26.753e7; % gyromagnetic ratio for 1H in [rad/T/s]
13
FA = x(2); % flip angle for the rectangular pulse in [deg]
14
% mean irradiation amplitude [T]
15
Par.B1 = (FA/(gamma*Par.tp*180/pi)*Par.tp/T_iter);
16
Par.T = x(1); % saturation time [s]
17
18
% simulate Z−spectrum
19
[Z,offset_ppm] = BM2PoolSim(Par,0);
20
21
% MTRasym calculation
22
sym = fliplr(Z(Par.N/2:end−1));
23
MTRasym = sym−Z(1:Par.N/2);
24
asym_axis = offset_ppm(1:Par.N/2);
25
26
[~,idx] = min(abs(asym_axis −a(3)));
27
28
CEST_effect = MTRasym(idx);

%% perform two sample one−sided t−tests

231

232
% If h = 1, this indicates the rejection of the null hypothesis at
233
% the Alpha significance level.
234
% If h = 0, this indicates a failure to reject the null hypothesis
235
% at the Alpha significance level.
236
% p: p−value, scalar value in the range [0,1]
237
% p−value of the test, returned as a scalar value in the range
238
% [0,1].
239
% p is the probability of observing a test statistic as extreme as,
240
% or more extreme than, the observed value under the null
241
% hypothesis. Small values of p cast doubt on the validity of the
242
% null hypothesis.
243
end
244
end

function T1map = readT1map(path)

%READT1MAP reads inversion recovery DICOMs and constructs a T1map
3

% least−square−curve−fitting
6
%
7

8
%

9
%

21

Source Code C.10: B0compensation.m

function [Z_image, offset_image] = B0compensation(nR,nC,num_data,data_M0,
data_spectrum,FACTOR)
2
%B0COMPENSATION compensates for B0 shifts by means of parabolic fitting
3
% Extracted and slightly edited version of the subfunction
4
% 'cest_offset_comp' in the post−processing function of EPI−CEST data
5
% 'Cest_rfs' by Mitsuharu Miyoshi and Gaspar Delso, Advanced Applications &
6
% Workflow (ASL), GE Healthcare, 2014
7
%
8
% Edited by: Rie Beck Hansen
9
% GE Global Research Europe in Munich, Germany and Technical University of
10
% Denmark, January 2016
11
12
%% Center Frequency Offset (B0) compensation
13
disp('Compensating for B0 inhomogeneities...')
14
15
% num_data: number of rf offset frequency
16
% num_pixel: total pixel size
17
18
%% Initialization %%
19
num_pixel = nR * nC;
20
Z_image = zeros(num_data, num_pixel);
21
offset_image = zeros(1, num_pixel);
22
%/* calculate fft size */
23
n_fft = 1;
24
for tmpi1 = 1:16
25
n_fft = n_fft * 2;
26
if (n_fft > num_data)
27
break;
28
end
29
end
30
31
%% pixel loop %%
32
for tmpi2 = 1:num_pixel
33
%% Initial offset compensation %%
34
% calculate the max/min/B0 of Signal intensity
%
==PAGE:172==
148
Appendix C. Matlab Source Code
35
% si_offset has minimum signal at center_shift_initial + n_offset/2 %
36
si_M0 = data_M0(tmpi2);
37
si_offset = data_spectrum(:, tmpi2);
38
39
si_min = min(si_offset);
40
si_max = max(si_offset);
41
42
% min at center_shift_initial + n_offset/2 + 1 %
43
center_shift_initial = find(si_offset == si_min, 1) −num_data/2 −1;
44
45
% skip all data processing in the following cases %
46
if ((si_max == si_min) || (si_M0 <= 0) || (center_shift_initial +
num_data/2 < 0))
47
continue;
48
elseif (abs(center_shift_initial) > num_data/4)
49
% Maybe Noize pixel %
50
for tmpi1 = 1: num_data
51
if (si_offset(tmpi1) < 0)
52
Z_image(tmpi1, tmpi2) = 0;
53
elseif (si_offset(tmpi1) > si_M0 * 1.5)
54
Z_image(tmpi1, tmpi2) = 1.5 * FACTOR;
55
else
56
Z_image(tmpi1, tmpi2) = si_offset(tmpi1) / si_M0 * FACTOR;
57
end
58
end
59
continue;
60
end
61
62
%% offest comp %
63
n_offset = num_data;
64
center_shift_total = center_shift_initial;
65
% Offset compensation */
66
% Data is shifted depending on the center_shift_total value. %
67
% The center_shift_total must be integer in this function. %
68
if (center_shift_total == 0)
69
si_comp = si_offset;
70
% return;
71
else
72
si_comp = ones(n_offset, 1) * si_max;
73
if (center_shift_total > 0)
74
si_comp(1 : (n_offset −center_shift_total)) = si_offset((1 +
center_shift_total) : n_offset);
75
else
76
si_comp((1 −center_shift_total) : n_offset) = si_offset(1 : (
n_offset + center_shift_total));
77
end
78
end
79
80
%% Parabolic fitting compensation %%
81
% Initialization %
82
center_shift_cntr = 0;
83
center_shift_parabolic = 0;
84
%% Initialize si_IQ1: Vertical swap, Transverse swap and FFT %
85
n_offset = num_data;
86
% Fourier transformation to shift data less than one pixel %
87
%% Vertical swap and Transverse swap %%
88
% Y axis: swap upside down, swap min and max. %
89
% X axis: FFT swap.
%
90
% si_I1 has peak signal near si_I1[0].
%
91
si_I1 = zeros(n_fft, 1);
92
si_I1(1:(n_offset/2)) = si_max −si_offset((n_offset/2 + 1):n_offset);
93
si_I1((n_fft −n_offset/2 + 1):n_fft)
= si_max −si_offset(1:(n_offset
/2));
94
95
%% FFT %%
96
si_fft = fft(si_I1);
97
98
%%
99
% minimum pixel size of window for parabolic fitting is 5 %
100
if (floor(num_data/8) > 5)
101
n_fit_min = floor(num_data/8);
102
else
103
n_fit_min = 5;
104
end
105
% actual pixel size of window of parabolic fitting: n_fit_parabolic %
106
n_fit_parabolic = floor((num_data/8) * 2 −1);
107
if (mod(n_fit_parabolic,2) == 0)
108
n_fit_parabolic = n_fit_parabolic + 1; % n_fit_parabolic must be
odd %
109
end
110
if (n_fit_parabolic < n_fit_min)
111
n_fit_parabolic = n_fit_min;
112
end
113
%% Parabolic fitting loop %%
114
while (center_shift_cntr < 4) % maximum iteration is 4 times %
115
%% parabolic fitting
==PAGE:173==
149
116
n_offset = num_data;
117
n_fit = n_fit_parabolic;
118
119
% Parabolic function fitting (LSQ) to find the center of symmetry %
120
if ((n_offset < n_fit+1) || (n_fit < 3))
121
center_shift_tmp = 0;
122
error_flag = 1;
123
% return;
124
else
125
%% Calculate the min peak of si_comp %%
126
% x axis of si_comp is from 0 to n_offet−1 %
127
% Peak of si_comp should be at n_offset/2
%
128
% select data from n_offset/2−(n_fit−1)/2 to n_offset/2+(n_fit
−1)/2 %
129
if (mod(n_fit,2) == 1) % n_fit is odd number %
130
n_fit_even_flag = 0;
131
start_point = n_offset/2 −(n_fit−1)/2;
132
else % n_fit is even number %
133
% NOTE!! select larger point to avoid OH peak %
134
if (si_comp(n_offset/2 −n_fit/2 + 1) > si_comp(n_offset/2
+ n_fit/2 + 1))
135
n_fit_even_flag = 1;
136
start_point = n_offset/2 −n_fit/2;
137
else
138
n_fit_even_flag = −1;
139
start_point = n_offset/2 −n_fit/2 + 1;
140
end
141
end
142
si_fit = (si_comp(start_point + (1:n_fit)))';
143
144
%% Least mean square fitting with parabolic equation %%
145
x_fit = 0:(n_fit−1);
146
%a = polyfit(x_fit, si_fit, 2); % Don't use polyfit for quick
processing %
147
%%
148
x = x_fit;
149
y = si_fit;
150
n = n_fit;
151
152
%% Least square fitting %%
153
% y = a(1) * x^2 + a(2) * x + a(3) %
154
% A * a = [yx2; yx1; yx0]
%
155
% inv(A) is directly calculated to save processing time. %
156
x0 = n; x1 = sum(x); x2 = sum(x.^2); x3 = sum(x.^3); x4 = sum(
x.^4);
157
yx0 = sum(y); yx1 = sum(y.*x); yx2 = sum(y .* x.^2);
158
159
% A = zeros(3);
160
% A(1,1) = x4; A(1,2) = x3; A(1,3) = x2;
161
% A(2,1) = x3; A(2,2) = x2; A(2,3) = x1;
162
% A(3,1) = x2; A(3,2) = x1; A(3,3) = x0;
163
% A_inv = inv(A);
164
165
% Calculate det(A) %
166
det_A = x4*x2*x0 + 2*x3*x2*x1 −x4*x1*x1 −x3*x3*x0 −x2*x2*x2;
167
if (det_A == 0)
168
error_flag = 1;
169
a = zeros(3);
170
% return;
171
else
172
173
% inv_A_21=inv_A_12;inv_A_31=inv_A_13;inv_A_32=inv_A_23 %
174
A_inv = zeros(3);
175
A_inv(1,1) = (x2 * x0 −x1 * x1) / det_A;
176
A_inv(1,2) = (x2 * x1 −x3 * x0) / det_A; A_inv(2,1) =
A_inv(1,2);
177
A_inv(1,3) = (x3 * x1 −x2 * x2) / det_A; A_inv(3,1) =
A_inv(1,3);
178
A_inv(2,2) = (x4 * x0 −x2 * x2) / det_A;
179
A_inv(2,3) = (x3 * x2 −x4 * x1) / det_A; A_inv(3,2) =
A_inv(2,3);
180
A_inv(3,3) = (x4 * x2 −x3 * x3) / det_A;
181
182
% Calxulate inv(A) * Y %
183
a = A_inv * [yx2; yx1; yx0];
184
error_flag = 0;
185
186
end
187
%%
188
a2 = a(1); a1 = a(2); % a0 = a(3);
189
if (a2 <= 0) % a2 must be possitive at the center of frequency
offset %
190
% a2 <= 0 means that this pixel is noize. */
191
error_flag = 1;
192
end
193
if (error_flag > 0)
==PAGE:174==
150
Appendix C. Matlab Source Code
194
center_shift_tmp = 0;
195
% return;
196
else
197
% peak of parabolic function %
198
if (n_fit_even_flag == 0)
199
center_shift_tmp = −a1 / a2 / 2 −(n_fit−1)/2;
200
elseif (n_fit_even_flag == 1)
201
center_shift_tmp = −a1 / a2 / 2 −n_fit/2;
202
else % n_fit_even_flag == −1 %
203
center_shift_tmp = −a1 / a2 / 2 −n_fit/2 + 1;
204
end
205
end
206
end
207
%%
208
% Incorrect compensation: Maybe Noize pixel, error in
peak_parabolic_fitting %
209
if ((abs(center_shift_parabolic) > 3) || (error_flag > 0))
210
break;
211
else % good parabolic fitting: decrease the window %
212
center_shift_parabolic = center_shift_parabolic +
center_shift_tmp; % total offset compensated by parabolic
fitting %
213
center_shift_cntr = center_shift_cntr + 1;
214
215
%% offset comp %
216
center_shift_total = center_shift_parabolic +
center_shift_initial;
217
n_offset = num_data;
218
si_fftX = si_fft;
219
220
% Inverse Fourier transformation to shift data less than one
pixel %
221
%%
Offset compensation */
222
si_angle = (2 * pi * center_shift_total * ((1:n_fft) −n_fft/2
−1) / n_fft)';
223
si_angle = fftshift(si_angle);
224
si_fftX = si_fftX .* exp(1i * si_angle);
225
226
%%
iFFT, Vertical swap and Transverse swap %%
227
% Y axis: swap upside down, swap min and max.
%
228
% X axis: FFT swap.
%
229
% si_comp has peak signal near si_comp(n_offset/2+1). %
230
si_tmp = si_max −abs(fftshift(ifft(si_fftX)));
231
si_comp = si_tmp((n_fft/2 −n_offset/2+1) : (n_fft/2 + n_offset
/2));
232
233
%%
234
if ((abs(center_shift_tmp) < 0.1) && (n_fit_parabolic <=
n_fit_min)) % Fitting is completed %
235
break;
236
elseif (abs(center_shift_tmp) < 1.0) && (n_fit_parabolic >
n_fit_min + 1)
237
n_fit_parabolic = n_fit_parabolic −2; % n_fit_parabolic is
odd %
238
elseif (abs(center_shift_tmp) < 1.0) && (n_fit_parabolic >
n_fit_min)
239
n_fit_parabolic = n_fit_parabolic −1; % make
n_fit_parabolic odd %
240
end
241
end
242
end
243
244
%% offset pixel image %%
245
offset_image(tmpi2) = center_shift_parabolic + center_shift_initial;
246
247
%% Z (si/si_M0) spectrum %%
248

Source Code C.14: BM2PoolSim_vCEST.m
1
function [MTRdiff,Z,DWS,offset_ppm] = BM2PoolSim_vCEST(Par,plt)
2
%BM2POOLSIM_VCEST BM numerical simulation of a two pool vCEST system
3
%
4
% Bloch−McConnell numerical simulation of the Z−spectrum of a two pool
5
% system (where pool a is the water pool and pool b is the solute/CEST
6
% pool) using a phase cycled rectangular RF preparation pulse. The
7
% preparation pulse is applied with variable saturation power according to
8
% the vCEST method presented in "Investigating hydroxyl chemical exchange
9
% using a variable saturation power chemical exchange saturation transfer
10
% (vCEST) method at 3 T." Magn. Reson. Med., (2015), by Clark, DJ et al.
11
%
12
% Steady state condition without RF irradiation: kb*M0_b = ka*M0_a
13
%
14
% The simulation is in the rotating frame of reference with respect to the
15
% frequency and phase of the oscillating B1 field. So when the
16
% 'on−resonant' protons in pool a precess about the z−axis, it is due to a
17
% change in RF phase.
18
%
19
% input:
Par
= struct of system and sequence parameters with the
20
%
fields:
21
%
N (Z−spectrum resolution),
22
%
P (simulation points for one iteration time),
23
%
T (total saturation time),
24
%
kba (chemical exchange rate in [1/s] from pool
25
%
b to a)
26
%
fb (pool ratio / proton fraction)
27
%
Ra (R1 and R2 relaxtion rates for pool a in
28
%
[1/s])
29
%
Rb (R1 and R2 relaxtion rates for pool b in
30
%
[1/s])
31
%
B1 (mean B1 amplitude per ppm in [microT/ppm])
32
%
tp (pulse length in [s])
33
%
dwb (relative chemical shift of the protons in
34
%
pool b in [ppm])
==PAGE:180==
156
Appendix C. Matlab Source Code
35
%
B0 (field strength in [T])
36
%
M0a (initial magnetization of water in units of
37
%
thermal M0)
38
%
B0inhom (B0 inhomogeneity in [ppm])
39
%
plt
= true for display of simulation plots, else false
40
%
41
% output:
Z
= simulated Z−spectrum
42
%
offset_ppm
= saturation offset axis in ppm
43
%
44
% Example of parameters for simulating MICEST with vCEST
45
% Par.N = 64; Par.P = 16; Par.T = 3.5; Par.kba = 1000; Par.fb = 0.0108;
46
% Par.Ra = [1/500e−3, 1/60e−3]; Par.Rb = [1/500e−3, 1/30e−3];
47
% Par.B1 = 1; Par.tp = 0.232e−3; Par.dwb = .6; Par.B0 = 3;
48
% Par.M0a = 0.99; Par.B0inhom = 0;
49
%
50
% Author: Rie Beck Hansen
51
% GE Global Research Europe in Munich, Germany and Technical University of
52
% Denmark, January 2016
53
54
N = Par.N; % resolution of Z−spectrum, number of points
55
P = Par.P; % points for each T_iter
56
T = Par.T; % saturation time [s]
57
kba = Par.kba; % chemical exchange rate in [1/s] from pool b to a
58
fb = Par.fb; % pool ratio
59
R_1a = Par.Ra(1); % 1/T1 relaxtion rate for pool a in [1/s]
60
R_2a = Par.Ra(2); % 1/T2 relaxtion rate for pool a in [1/s]
61
R_1b = Par.Rb(1); % 1/T1 relaxtion rate for pool b in [1/s]
62
R_2b = Par.Rb(2); % 1/T2 relaxtion rate for pool b in [1/s]
63
B1 = Par.B1; % mean B1 amplitude per ppm in [microT/ppm]
64
tp = Par.tp; % pulse length [s]
65
dwb = Par.dwb; % relative chemical shift of the protons in pool b in [ppm]
66
B0 = Par.B0; % field strength in [T]
67
M0_a = Par.M0a; % free water magnetization in [T]
68
B0inhom = Par.B0inhom; % B0 inhomogeneity in [ppm]
69
70
gamma = 26.753e7; % gyromagnetic ratio for 1H in [rad/T/s]
71
72
% iteration time
73
T_iter = 0.56e−3; % iteration time between rect pulses
74
q = 1/(B0*gamma/(2*pi*10^6)*T_iter*(dwb+B0inhom));
75
76
dT = T_iter/P; % delta−time (each T_iter is divided into P points for
simulations)
77
[~,tp_idx] = min(abs(dT*(1:P)−tp));
78
79
phase_cest = 2*pi/q;
80
% phase shift acqquired per ppm for the chosen T_iter
81
phase_ppm = phase_cest/(dwb+B0inhom);
82
83
if B0inhom == 0
84
phase_water = 0;
85
else
86
q = 1/(B0*gamma/(2*pi*10^6)*T_iter*B0inhom);
87
phase_water = 2*pi/q;
88
end
89
90
kab = kba*fb; % chemical exchange rate in [1/s] from pool a to b (k_ws)
91
e_ka = exp(−dT*kab);
92
e_kb = exp(−dT*kba);
93
94
e_1a = exp(−dT*R_1a);
95
e_2a = exp(−dT*R_2a);
96
e_1b = exp(−dT*R_1b);
97
e_2b = exp(−dT*R_2b);
98
99
M0_b = M0_a*fb; % CEST proton magnetization in [T]
100
% fb = M0_b/M0_a, proton fraction => fb = (nb*[b])/(na*[a]), n is the
101
% number of protons per molecule for the respective pool
102
103
step = 2*pi/N;
104
dphase_a = pi−step:−step:−pi; % RF phase difference for pool a, water pool
105
offset_ppm = (dphase_a/phase_ppm); % ppm difference for pool a
106
107
% variable saturation power chemical exchange transfer = vCEST
108
% variable saturation power in [microT], amplitude of the rectangular
109
% pulses
110
B1vcest = abs(offset_ppm)*B1*P/tp_idx;
111
% since 0 power is not allowed, the saturation power at 0 ppm is a
112
% repetition of the previous value
113
B1vcest(N/2) = B1vcest(N/2−1);
114
115
% analytic simulation of the direct water saturation (DWS)
116
% S = R_2a + ka −ka*kb/(R_2b + kb); % in [1/s] (P in Clark et al. 2015)
117
% Q = R_1a + ka −ka*kb/(R_1b + kb); % in [1/s]
118
% C = ones(1,N)*c/B0; C(N/2) = 0; % variable power constant (dimensionless)
119
% DWS = C.^2./(C.^2+Q/S);
==PAGE:181==
157
120
% DWS = (B1vcest*10^(−6)*gamma).^2./((B1vcest*10^(−6)*gamma).^2 + Q*S ...
121
%
+ (offset_ppm*gamma*10^(−6)*B0).^2*Q/S);
122
%%
123
% chemical exchange matrix
124
CE = [e_ka 0 0 1−e_kb 0 0;
125
0 e_ka 0 0 1−e_kb 0;
126
0 0 e_ka 0 0 1−e_kb;
127
1−e_ka 0 0 e_kb 0 0;
128
0 1−e_ka 0 0 e_kb 0;
129
0 0 1−e_ka 0 0 e_kb];
130
% relaxation matrix
131
RE = [e_2a 0 0 0 0 0;
132
0 e_2a 0 0 0 0;
133
0 0 e_1a 0 0 0;
134
0 0 0 e_2b 0 0;
135
0 0 0 0 e_2b 0;
136
0 0 0 0 0 e_1b];
137
% Free precession of the off−resonance CEST pool (for 1 dT)
138
FP = [cos(−(phase_water)/P) −sin(−(phase_water)/P) 0 0 0 0;
139
sin(−(phase_water)/P) cos(−(phase_water)/P) 0 0 0 0;
140
0 0 1 0 0 0;
141
0 0 0 cos(−(phase_cest)/P) −sin(−(phase_cest)/P) 0;
142
0 0 0 sin(−(phase_cest)/P) cos(−(phase_cest)/P) 0;
143
0 0 0 0 0 1];
144
145
B = [0 0 (1−e_1a)*M0_a 0 0 (1−e_1b)*M0_b]';
146
147
PS = ceil(T/T_iter); % number of rect pulses
148
149
% magnetization preallocation
150
M = zeros(6,P*PS+1,N);
151
% starting magnetization
152
M(:,1,:) = repmat([0;0;M0_a;0;0;M0_b],[1,1,N]);
153
154
% initialize status display
155
x = 0; tmp = zeros(1,N*PS);
156
fprintf('Simulating Z−spectrum: ')
157
158
for m = 1:N
159
for n = 1:PS
160
% RF phase shift for current phase cycled image
161
RP = [cos(dphase_a(m)) −sin(dphase_a(m)) 0 0 0 0;
162
sin(dphase_a(m)) cos(dphase_a(m)) 0 0 0 0;
163
0 0 1 0 0 0;
164
0 0 0 cos(dphase_a(m)) −sin(dphase_a(m)) 0;
165
0 0 0 sin(dphase_a(m)) cos(dphase_a(m)) 0;
166
0 0 0 0 0 1];
167
168
% RF excitation matrix with variable saturation power
169
RF = [1 0 0 0 0 0;
170
0 cos(gamma*dT*B1vcest(m)*10^(−6)) ...
171
−sin(gamma*dT*B1vcest(m)*10^(−6)) 0 0 0;
172
0 sin(gamma*dT*B1vcest(m)*10^(−6)) ...
173
cos(gamma*dT*B1vcest(m)*10^(−6)) 0 0 0;
174
0 0 0 1 0 0;
175
0 0 0 0 cos(gamma*dT*B1vcest(m)*10^(−6)) ...
176
−sin(gamma*dT*B1vcest(m)*10^(−6));
177
0 0 0 0 sin(gamma*dT*B1vcest(m)*10^(−6)) ...
178
cos(gamma*dT*B1vcest(m)*10^(−6))];
179
180
% rect pulse (divided into P parts)
181
% (chemical exchange, relaxation, excitation, and free precession)
182
for k = 1+P*(n−1):tp_idx+P*(n−1)
183
M(:,k+1,m) = CE*RE*RF*FP*M(:,k,m) + B;
184
end
185
186
% between pulses (no excitation)
187
% (chemical exchange, relaxation, and free precession)
188
for k = tp_idx+1+P*(n−1):P+P*(n−1)
189
M(:,k+1,m) = CE*RE*FP*M(:,k,m) + B;
190
end
191
192
% phase shift of RF pulse and therefore rotating frame of reference
193
% before the next phase cycled pulse is applied with new phase
194
% difference
195
M(:,P+P*(n−1)+1,m) = RP*M(:,P+P*(n−1)+1,m);
196
197
% display progress in percent
198
tmp(n+(m−1)*PS) = round((n+(m−1)*PS)/(N*PS)*100);
199
if (n+(m−1)*PS) > 1
200
if tmp(n+(m−1)*PS) > tmp((n+(m−1)*PS)−1)
201
fprintf(repmat('\b',1,x))
202
fprintf('%d %%\n', tmp(n+(m−1)*PS));
203
s = sprintf('%d %%\n', tmp(n+(m−1)*PS));
204
x = numel(s);
205
end

206
end
207
end
208
end

Mza = squeeze(M(3,:,:));

213

% relaxation matrix
215
RE = [e_2a 0 0;
216
0 e_2a 0;
217
0 0 e_1a];
218
219
% free precession of the water pool to account for off−resonance
220
% effects caused by any B0 inhomogeneity (for 1 delta time (dT))

222
sin(−(phase_water)/P) cos(−(phase_water)/P) 0;

0 0 1];

sin(dphase_a(m)) cos(dphase_a(m)) 0;
241
0 0 1];
242

% RF excitation matrix with variable saturation power

RF = [1 0 0;

0 cos(gamma*dT*B1vcest(m)*10^(−6)) ...
246
−sin(gamma*dT*B1vcest(m)*10^(−6));
247
0 sin(gamma*dT*B1vcest(m)*10^(−6)) ...

cos(gamma*dT*B1vcest(m)*10^(−6))];

%% Z_spectrum

332
disp(['Start time: ' num2str(time(4)) ':' num2str(time(5)) ':' ...

335

336

337
338

%% define Z−spectrum for current voxel

% for debugging

% r = 75; c = 49; index = (r−1)*double(nR)+c;
381

Zspec = Z_image(:,index);
383
384
%% simulate DWS spectrum
385
386
if simDWS == 1
387
T = 3; % saturation time [s], long enough to approach steady state
388
P = 16; % points for each T_iter
389
390
step = 2*pi/64;
391
392
% RF phase difference for pool a, water pool
393
dphase_a = pi:−step:−pi+step;
394
dphase_a = dphase_a(idx_offset);
395
396
% iteration time
397
T_iter = 0.56e−3; % iteration time between rect pulses
398
dT = T_iter/P; % delta−time
==PAGE:188==
164
Appendix C. Matlab Source Code
399
tp = 0.232e−3; % pulse length
400
[~,tp_idx] = min(abs(dT*(1:P)−tp));
401
402
% define relaxation times for current voxel
403
T_1a = T1mapR(index)*10^(−3);
404
T_2a = T2mapR(index)*10^(−3);
405
406
R_1a = 1/T_1a; % 1/T1 relaxtion rate for pool a in [1/s]
407
R_2a = 1/T_2a; % 1/T2 relaxtion rate for pool a in [1/s]
408
e_1a = exp(−dT*R_1a);
409
e_2a = exp(−dT*R_2a);
410
411
M0_a = .99;
412
B = [0 0 (1−e_1a)*M0_a]';
413
414
% variable saturation power chemical exchange transfer = vCEST
415
% with used flip angle per ppm = 8.5841 degrees
416
c = 8.5841/(gamma*10^(−6)*dT*tp_idx*180/pi);
417
% variable saturation power in [microT]
418
B1vcest = abs(axis_offset)*c*(relB1R(index));
419
% since 0 power is not allowed, the saturation power at 0 ppm is a
420
% repetition of the previous value
421
B1vcest(ceil(num_data/2)) = B1vcest(ceil(num_data/2)−1);
422
% FA = B1vcest*10^(−6)*(gamma*dT*tp_idx*180/pi);
423
424
PS = ceil(T/T_iter); % number of rect pulses
425
426
% magnetization preallocation
427
M = zeros(3,P*PS+1,num_data);
428
% starting magnetization
429
M(:,1,:) = repmat([0;0;M0_a],[1,1,num_data]);
430
431
% relaxation matrix
432
RE = [e_2a 0 0;
433
0 e_2a 0 ;
434
0 0 e_1a];
435
436
% B0_inhom in ppm
437
B0_inhom = B0mapR(index);
438
q = 1/(B0*gamma/(2*pi*10^6)*T_iter*B0_inhom);
439
% phase shift acqquired by the water pool during the iteration time
T_iter
440
phase_shift = 2*pi/q;
441
442
% free precession of the water pool to account for off−resonance
443
% effects caused by B0 inhomogeneity (for 1 delta time (dT))
444
FP = [cos(−(phase_shift)/P) −sin(−(phase_shift)/P) 0;
445
sin(−(phase_shift)/P) cos(−(phase_shift)/P) 0;
446
0 0 1];
447
448
for m = 1:num_data
449
for n = 1:PS
450
% RF phase shift for current phase cycled image
451
RP = [cos(dphase_a(m)) −sin(dphase_a(m)) 0;
452
sin(dphase_a(m)) cos(dphase_a(m)) 0;
453
0 0 1];
454
455
% RF excitation matrix with variable saturation power
456
RF = [1 0 0;
457
0 cos(gamma*dT*B1vcest(m)*10^(−6)) −sin(gamma*dT*
B1vcest(m)*10^(−6));
458
0 sin(gamma*dT*B1vcest(m)*10^(−6)) cos(gamma*dT*B1vcest
(m)*10^(−6))];
459
460
% rect pulse (divided into P parts)
461
for k = 1+P*(n−1):tp_idx+P*(n−1)
462
M(:,k+1,m) = RE*FP*RF*M(:,k,m) + B;
463
end
464
465
% between pulses (no excitation)
466
for k = tp_idx+1+P*(n−1):P+P*(n−1)
467
M(:,k+1,m) = RE*FP*M(:,k,m) + B;
468
end
469
470
% phase shift of RF pulse and therefore rotating frame of
reference
471
M(:,P+P*(n−1)+1,m) = RP*M(:,P+P*(n−1)+1,m);
472
end
473
end
474
475
Mza_DWS = squeeze(M(3,:,:));
476
477
DWS = Mza_DWS(end,:)/M0_a;
478
479
DWSsim(:,index) = DWS;
480

else

484

%% isolate vCEST effect by means of a lorentzian fit
545
546
% starting points for the fit parameters, baseline, amplitude and FWHM

548

549
ub = [.025 .2 5 .65];
550
551
options = optimoptions('lsqcurvefit','Algorithm',...
552
'trust−region−reflective','MaxIter',1600,'FunValCheck','on',...
553
'Display','off');
554
555
fun = @(x,xq) x(1)+x(2)*1./(1+((x(4)−xq)./(x(3)/2)).^2);
556
557
fitregion = [1 −1];
558
559
Lpar = lsqcurvefit(fun,x0,xq(xq > fitregion(2) & xq < fitregion(1)),...
560
MTRdiff_interp(xq > fitregion(2)&xq < fitregion(1)),lb,ub,options);

%% simulate DWS spectrum
332
333
if simDWS == 1
334
T = 3; % saturation time [s], long enough to approach steady state
335
P = 16; % points for each T_iter
336
337
step = 2*pi/64;
338
339
% RF phase difference for pool a, water pool
340
dphase_a = pi:−step:−pi+step;
341
dphase_a = dphase_a(idx_offset);
342
343
% iteration time
344
T_iter = 0.56e−3; % iteration time between rect pulses
345
dT = T_iter/P; % delta−time
346
tp = 0.232e−3; % pulse length
347
[~,tp_idx] = min(abs(dT*(1:P)−tp));
348
349
% define relaxation times for current voxel
350
T_1a = T1mapR(index)*10^(−3);
351
T_2a = T2mapR(index)*10^(−3);
352
353
R_1a = 1/T_1a; % 1/T1 relaxtion rate for pool a in [1/s]
354
R_2a = 1/T_2a; % 1/T2 relaxtion rate for pool a in [1/s]
355
e_1a = exp(−dT*R_1a);
356
e_2a = exp(−dT*R_2a);
357
358
M0_a = .99;
359
B = [0 0 (1−e_1a)*M0_a]';
360
361
% variable saturation power chemical exchange transfer = vCEST
362
% with used flip angle per ppm = 8.5841 degrees
363
c = 8.5841/(gamma*10^(−6)*dT*tp_idx*180/pi);
364
% variable saturation power in [microT]
365
B1vcest = abs(axis_offset)*c*(relB1R(index));
366
% since 0 power is not allowed, the saturation power at 0 ppm is a
367
% repetition of the previous value
368
B1vcest(ceil(num_data/2)) = B1vcest(ceil(num_data/2)−1);
369
% FA = B1vcest*10^(−6)*(gamma*dT*tp_idx*180/pi);
370
371
PS = ceil(T/T_iter); % number of rect pulses
==PAGE:199==
175
372
373
% magnetization preallocation
374
M = zeros(3,P*PS+1,num_data);
375
% starting magnetization
376
M(:,1,:) = repmat([0;0;M0_a],[1,1,num_data]);
377
378
% relaxation matrix
379
RE = [e_2a 0 0;
380
0 e_2a 0 ;
381
0 0 e_1a];
382
383
% B0_inhom in ppm
384
B0_inhom = B0mapR(index);
385
q = 1/(B0*gamma/(2*pi*10^6)*T_iter*B0_inhom);
386
% phase shift acqquired by the water pool during the iteration time
T_iter
387
phase_shift = 2*pi/q;
388
389
% free precession of the water pool to account for off−resonance
390
% effects caused by B0 inhomogeneity (for 1 delta time (dT))
391
FP = [cos(−(phase_shift)/P) −sin(−(phase_shift)/P) 0;
392
sin(−(phase_shift)/P) cos(−(phase_shift)/P) 0;
393
0 0 1];
394
395
for m = 1:num_data
396
for n = 1:PS
397
% RF phase shift for current phase cycled image
398
RP = [cos(dphase_a(m)) −sin(dphase_a(m)) 0;
399
sin(dphase_a(m)) cos(dphase_a(m)) 0;
400
0 0 1];
401
402
% RF excitation matrix with variable saturation power
403
RF = [1 0 0;
404
0 cos(gamma*dT*B1vcest(m)*10^(−6)) −sin(gamma*dT*
B1vcest(m)*10^(−6));
405
0 sin(gamma*dT*B1vcest(m)*10^(−6)) cos(gamma*dT*B1vcest
(m)*10^(−6))];
406
407
% rect pulse (divided into P parts)
408
for k = 1+P*(n−1):tp_idx+P*(n−1)
409
M(:,k+1,m) = RE*FP*RF*M(:,k,m) + B;
410
end
411
412
% between pulses (no excitation)
413
for k = tp_idx+1+P*(n−1):P+P*(n−1)
414
M(:,k+1,m) = RE*FP*M(:,k,m) + B;
415
end
416
417
% phase shift of RF pulse and therefore rotating frame of
reference
418
M(:,P+P*(n−1)+1,m) = RP*M(:,P+P*(n−1)+1,m);
419
end
420
end
421
422
Mza_DWS = squeeze(M(3,:,:));
423
424
DWS = Mza_DWS(end,:)/M0_a;
425
426
DWSsim(:,index) = DWS;
427
428
else
429
% define DWS from previous simulation
430
DWS = DWSsim(:,index)';
431
end
432
433
%% display spectrum (before interpolation)
434
435
MTRdiff = DWS −Zspec';
436
437
B0_inhom = B0mapR(index);
438
axis_offset_inhom = axis_offset −B0_inhom;
439
440
if plt == 1
441
figure;
442
plot(axis_offset_inhom,Zspec,'−k','linewidth',1)
443
axis([−inf inf −.1 1])
444
set(gca,'XDir','reverse')
445
xlabel('Chemical shift [ppm]')
446
ylabel 'M_z / M_0'
447
title(['vCEST, index = ' num2str(index)])
448
449
blue = [0 0.4470 0.7410];
450

hold on
452
plot(axis_offset_inhom,DWS,':','linewidth',1,'color',blue)
453
plot(axis_offset_inhom,MTRdiff,'−','linewidth',1.5,'color',blue)
==PAGE:200==

Appendix C. Matlab Source Code

hold off

legend('Z−spectrum','DWS','MTR_{diff}','location','west')
457
legend('boxoff')

%% interpolate spectra
461
462
% remove 'outliers' close to the water resonance
463
thr = 0.3;
464
MTRdiff_fitting = [MTRdiff(axis_offset_inhom > thr),...
465
MTRdiff(axis_offset_inhom < −thr)];
466
offset_fitting = [axis_offset_inhom(axis_offset_inhom > thr),...
467
axis_offset_inhom(axis_offset_inhom < −thr)];
468
469
% interpolate MTR_diff spectrum before fitting
470
MTRdiff_interp = interp1(offset_fitting,MTRdiff_fitting,xq,'spline');
471
472
spectra.Z(:,index) = Zspec;
473
spectra.DWS(:,index) = DWS;
474
spectra.MTRdiff_int(:,index) = MTRdiff_interp;
475
spectra.axis(:,index) = axis_offset_inhom;

%% calulate AUC
484
485
f_MI = .5;
486
AUC_MI = [f_MI−.25 f_MI+.25];
487
488
AUC(index) = −trapz(xq(xq > AUC_MI(1) & xq < AUC_MI(2)),...
489
MTRdiff_interp(xq > AUC_MI(1) & xq < AUC_MI(2)));

%% isolate vCEST effect by means of a lorentzian fit
492
493
% starting points for the fit parameters, baseline, amplitude and FWHM
494
x0 = [0 0.02 3 .5]; % baseline, amplitude, FWHM, chemical shift
495
lb = [−.025 −.2 1.5 .35];
496
ub = [.025 .2 5 .65];
497
498
options = optimoptions('lsqcurvefit','Algorithm',...
499
'trust−region−reflective','MaxIter',1600,'FunValCheck','on',...
500
'Display','off');
501
502
fun = @(x,xq) x(1)+x(2)*1./(1+((x(4)−xq)./(x(3)/2)).^2);
503
504
fitregion = [1 −1];
505
506
Lpar = lsqcurvefit(fun,x0,xq(xq > fitregion(2) & xq < fitregion(1)),...
507
MTRdiff_interp(xq > fitregion(2)&xq < fitregion(1)),lb,ub,options);
508
509
FIT(index,:) = Lpar;
510
511
Zlab = fun(Lpar,xq);
512
Z_base = repmat(Lpar(1),nOffsets);
513
514
spectra.Lfit(:,index) = Zlab;
515
516
residual = Zlab−MTRdiff_interp;
517
resnorm = sum((residual).^2);
518
519
end

28

29
